The Red Page
Strindberg
At last a biography of Strindberg.
August Strindberg: the Spirit of Revolt,
Studies and Impressions, by L. Lind-af-
Hageby (Paul) isn’t much of a biography;
but with respect to a genius so extraordinary
and so multifarious any biography is wel-
come. Thus, if Cain, for instance, had left
a full confession, or Moses had kept a diary,
both works, however lacking in stvle the
hieroglyphics were, would be worth reading.
For even in literature we can’t get on with-
out facts and dates: that is what makes a
railway time-table the finest piece of litera-
ture in the world when you want to catch
that last train home—or don’t want to
catch it home. So Miss Lind-af-Hageby
supplies the necessary dates and facts,
though surely a woman is the last sort of
animal that should be allowed to interpret
this supreme hater of womanhood. Well,
for our facts, and the opinions of L. Lind-
af-Hageby.
d*
Strindberg died on May 14, 1912. His
collected works, shortly to be published in
a new edition —-or, possibly, a new library—
consist of 115 plays, novels, collections of
stories, essays and poems. The diary in
which, during the last 14 years, he re-
corded his visions and supernatural experi-
ences, will not be published for many years
to come. His dying words were: “Every-
thing personal is now obliterated. I have
settled with life. My account has been
rendered. This (picking up a Bible) alone
is right.”
From his four autobiographical novels,
The Bondswoman’s Bon (recently reviewed
here as The Son of a Servant), Fermenta-
tion Time, In the Red Room and The
Author, all written at the age of 39, we
get a groundwork of facts. Born in 1849.
His school life we have already summarised.
In 1867 he went to Upsala University, his
sole capital being under £5. He returned
after one term to Stockholm, and got a job
as teacher at a board-school at £5O a year.
At the age of 19 he possessed all the usual
vices of virile youth. Next he became a
medical student, failed to pass his examina-
tion, decided to become an actor, was em-
ployed as a super, failed in an ambitious
part, meant to commit suicide, but was
persuaded by a friend that getting drunk
would be easier. Four days after he wrote his
first play. It was rejected. He returned
to Upsala. His one-act play, In Rome, was
performed at Stockholm when Strindberg
was 21 years old. Another attempt at sui-
cide was frustrated by his friends with the
kindly aid of alcohol. He could not be
happy at home, and lived on one of the
Baltic islands in a fisherman’s cottage. In
bitter poverty he got his Viking play
accepted, and the King gave the dramatist
a yearly pension. Next he was dismissed
from the university as being insolent and
crazy. He took up painting, came to the
conclusion that he was going mad, and
offered to go to an asylum for cure. The
King stopped his pension, and Strindberg
went to Stockholm as an art-critic, even
contributor to a women’s illustrated paper,
and dramatist. But his paper died, Strind-
berg turned to drama; his first great play.
Master Olof, was written in an interval of
asceticism, and, of course, rejected. Next
he became editor of a paper devoted to in-
surance, evidently a sort of Swedish Wild
Oat Column. The paper failed. He fell
seriously ill, and on recovery fell violently
in love with his housekeeper. After an in-
terval he became a telegraph operator, and
then once more a journalist. But he and
his paper could not agree, and he left, to
return to poverty. Then the post of
assistant librarian in the Royal Library of
Stockholm came his way. In 1869 his novel,
The Red Room, was published, and made
the author’s name; and thenceforward
Strindberg, having found himself and found
his audience, went on feverishly writing.
The record of his life now becomes merely
the dating and examination of his many
plays and stories.
d*
Of Strindberg his biographer says:—
“There was something cometic in the swift-
ness and intensity with which he appeared,
disturbing the well-ordered orbits of tradi-
tions and conventions... .No sooner had
people congratulated themselves that his ter-
rible lust for destruction had passed by their
favorite systems and their cherished ideals,
than his ruthless force was upon them, ex-
posing ugliness and scattering treasures....
He wrote books which were decidedly and
unblushingly immoral. He compelled atten-
tion by blasphemous outbursts He dis-
sected the human heart, laid bare its mean-
ness, its uncleanliness ; made men and women
turn on each other with sudden understand-
ing, and loathing, and walked away smiling
at the evil he had wrought. He turned on
himself with savage hatred, and in book*
bearing the mark of the flagellant and reflect-
ing the agony of a soul in torment, he pointed
to his sins and his stripes. .. .He was—him-
self; and he worked ceaselessly at the task of
finding himself. Strindberg the atheist, the
scientist, the spiritualist, the mystic, the
sensualist, the ascetic, took equally import-
ant parts in his theatre of life. The critic
met him day by day in different attire and
pose, incarnations of the elusive self which
was stage-manager of this extraordinary per-
formance.
“There can be no doubt that it was difficult
to live with Strindberg. The Swedes had to
live with him, and the household of those who
set themselves up to guard the propriety and
integrity of literary art was day by day
threatened by his revolutionary ideas, his
personal attacks on spotless individuals, his
coarse-grained descriptions of indescribable
things.”
As a result of the many attacks made upon
Strindberg in Sweden, theatrical managers
fought shy of his plays. It took 14 years for
The Father, after its production in Paris, to
reach Stockholm. He suffers, too, seriously
from translation.
“His sentences are wrought with a nervous
force which is untranslatable. He is often
ungrammatical—each sentence is a living
whole which cannot be divided. . . .Translated
from German into English he is unrecognis-
able. The difficulty of fusing his meaning
and style in a new form is also apparent in
the direct translations into English which
have been made.... The belief is expressed
that Strindberg’s prose will be rendered bet-
ter in “American” than in English.... He
wrote because he had to, without any desire
to build up a style ; without any desire, in-
deed, but to get his thoughts down on
paper.”
J*
“My brain works incessantly,” he said.
“It grinds and grinds like a mill, and I can-
not make it stop. I get no peace before I
see my thoughts on paper, and then some-
thing new begins immediately, and there is
the same misery. I write and write, and do
not even read through what I have written
....When I have finished my work for the
day, I always note on a piece of paper what
I shall begin with the next day. The whole
long afternoon and , evening I collect
material for next day’s work. During my
morning walk my thoughts are further con-
densed, and when I return from my wander-
ings I am charged like an electric machine.
As soon as I have paper and pen ready it
bursts out. The words literally tumble
over me, and the pen works under high
pressure in order to get everything down on
paper. When I have written for a while I
have a feeling that I am floating in space.
Then it is as if a higher will than my own
made the pen glide over the paper, guide it
to write down words which seem to me en-
tirely inspired.”
Strindberg used absinthe, but he cannot
be put on the long list of literary drunks
who required alcohol before they could
write. Strindberg required alcohol to pre-
vent him writing. In one of his plays he
explained why he drank so much. “Because
I am always lying on the operating-table,
and have to chloroform myself.” The only
way to ease up the mental machinery was
to clog it up with alcohol.
So with his misogynism:—
“I have always worshipped women, these
enchanting, criminal minxes whose worst
crimes are not registered in criminal statis-
tics. But I have had sufficiently bad—or
good—taste to tell them the truth, and they
have revenged themselves by calling me a
woman-hater. Just think, if these priest-
esses of revenge knew how many successes
with the fair sex their revenge has brought
me! Inquisitiveness, the original sin of
Eve, drew the little ingenues to the monster,
and the monster put no obstacles in the way
for even the most inquisitive to satisfy their
curiosity Many thanks, my charming
enemies.”
That was the sort of man he was. How
maliciously he could hit back!
Jt
His last years in Stockholm passed in
calm. He lived a literary hermit, com-
panioned as always by poverty, and though
affluence was on its way toward him — a
publisher paid £ll,OOO for the Swedish
rights of all his published works—it came
too late. On January 22, 1912, the whole
Swedish nation celebrated his 63rd birth-
day, with presentations, torchlight proces-
sions, and the performance in all the
theatres of his plays. The apotheosis came
to a dying man.
Georgian Poetry
The Poetry Bookshop publishes under the
above title a collection of verses di'awn en-
tirely from the publications of the past two
years in England, and ventures modestly to
hope “that we are at the beginning of an-
other ‘Georgian period’ which may take rank
in du-e time with the several great poetic
ages of the past.” Why not? The majority
of these 17 selected modern English poets
have something individual to say, and much
that they say is poetry. Had the book been
by one pen it would have w’on unanimous
praise; but its homogeneity remains though
it is made of the siftings of 17 poets. The
Poetry Bookshop is hereby exhorted to make
an annual anthology of English verse with
the same discriminating taste; and, judging
by the fact that this present volume is already
in its fifth edition, the yearly venture would
pay.
Lascelles Abercrombie, a poet whose pro-
lixity has hitherto prevented the present
scribe from reading him, appears in a quite
interesting selection, “The Sale of Saint
Thomas.” Gordon Bottomley has a curious
poem on Babel, in which he most cunningly
represents the confusion of speech mac -ame
suddenly upon the builders of that ancient
sky-scraper:—
Little men hurrying, running here and there,
Within the dark and stifling walls dissent
From every sound, and shoulder empty hods:
u The god’s great altar should stand in the
crypt
Among our earth's foundations." “The god's
great altar
Must be the last far coping of our work.”
"It should inaugurate the broad main stair."
“Or end it." “It must stand toward the
East!" ....
Then a lean giant, "Is not a calyx needful?”
“Because round grapes on statues well ex-
pressed
Become the nadir of incenst, nodal lamps,
Yet apes have hands thad but and carved
red crystal."
“Birds molten, touchly talc veins bronze buds
crumble
Ablid üblai ghan isz rad eighar g1aur1."....
Words said too often seemed such ancient
sounds
That men joryot them or were lost in tnem;
The guttural glottis-chasms of language
reached,
A rhythm, a gasp, were curves of immortal
thought.
Rupert Brooke presents “Dust” and “The
Fish” :
In a cool, curving world he lies
And ripples with dark ecstasies....
Those silent waters weave for him
A fluctuant mutable world and dim. ...
and “Town and Country”—all fine poems,
stuffed with thought and lit with restrained
fire. G. K. Chesterton is not worthily re-
presented. But William H. Davies has a
varied selection from that exquisite thing,
beginning:—
When primroses are out in sprang,
And small blue violets come between;
When merry birds sing on boughs green,
And rills, as soon as born, must sing ....
to more weighty work. Walter de la Mare
tells of “Miss Loo” and other daintily-
painted tea-table trifles. Wilfrid Wilson
Gibson’s “The Hare” is a finely wrought
piece of dramatic narration, in which he
shows a striking kinship to John Masefield.
This form of stark realism, finding poetry
and drama in common, modern things, is
the most individual contribution made by
the English school to modern poetry. We
have nothing like this form out here. W.
Vv. Gibson begins thus:—
My hands were hot upon a hare,
Half-strangled, struggling in a snare —
My knuckles at her warm wind-pipe —
When suddenly, her eyes shot back,
Big, fearful, staggering and black;
And, ere I knew, my grip was slack;
And I was clutching empty air,
Half-mad, half-glad at my lost luck.
When I awoke beside the stack. . . .
John Masefield, in “Biography,” contrives
to be interesting, though one fancies that
he could be more interesting in prose. T.
Sturge Moore is another of these narrative
poets, writing “A Sicilian Idyll” in a man-
ner most modern. But James Stephens is
a truer poet. “The Lonely God” is a thing
finely imagined :
So Eden was deserted, and at eve
Into the quiet place God came to grieve....
This was, the thought in Eden as He trod —
It is a lonely thing to be a g0d....
The other poets are dull or devitalised;
but there is enough modern poetry here of a
kind that challenges the reader’s interest.
Fiction
Henry Sydnor Harrison, author of that
striking success, Queed, keeps in its suc-
cessor, Y.V.’s Eyes (Constable, copies from
Dymock’s Library and George Robertson)
his high level of attainment. The eyes be-
long to Dr. V. Vivian, who possesses a hesi-
tating manner, a limp, and a John the Bap-
tist soul. Chance brings him into contact
with a beautiful, rich, but quite conven-
tional American girl, the daughter of a
cigar maker, the conditions of whose fac-
tory are shocking, even in the United States.
The girl’s sole aim in life is to make a good
marriage, and, aided by her managing
mother, she succeeds in captivating a lordly
male millionaire. But through a series of
dramatic encounters with this Christ-like
slum doctor, with his fierce zeal for reform,
the girl learns that there are other aims in
life. Her conversion to V.V.’s ideals is
convincingly told, though at exorbitant
length, but the style is so winning, the tone
so genial, the humor so human, and the
author’s personality so lovable, that the
book almost rta hes greatness. Not quite,
however. T! 11 j oral is too neat, the plot
too theatrically ucve-tailed. At times Har-
rison drops to the level of the society
novel of Robert W. Chambers. V.V.’s death
is too obviously necessitated—for one could
not marry a modern Christ—and the pathos
of it is absurdly overstrained. Solid work,
nevertheless, having much of the quality of
Frank Norris. Valuable, too, in that it is
a direct attack upon that tiresome person,
the American heroine.
J*
Edna Ferber is a feminine O. Henry. Her
work has all the slickness of the American
master of the journalistic story, but she
views life from a captivating feminine angle.
Roast Beef, Medium (Stokes, per Dymock’s
Library), tells in brilliant, business slang
the adventures of Emma McChesney, who
travelled the Vroad” for T. A. Buck’s
Featherloom Petticoats, and with her profits
kept her big son at college. Emma McChes-
ney is a delightful person, and Edna Ferber
introduces us to her business methods under
such frivolous and frilly chapter headings as
“Pink Tights and Ginghams.” “Simply
Skirts” and “Knee-deep in Knickers.” List
to her philosophy of the male: “Men are the
cusscdest creatures. This chap occupied the
same room with you last night and you don’t
even know his name. Funny! If two
strange women had found themselves oc-
cupying the same room for a night they
wouldn’t have got to the kimono and back-
hair stage before they would not only have
known each other’s name, but they’d have
tried on each other’s hats, swapped corset-
cover patterns, found mutual friends living
in Dayton, Ohio, taught each other a new
Irish crochet stitch, showed their family
photographs, told how their married sister’s
little girl nearly died with swollen glands,
and divided off the mirror into two sections
to paste their newly-washed handkerchiefs
on. Don’t tell me men have a genius for
friendship.’’
J*
The Outlaw, by David Hennessey (Hod-
per and Stoughton, per Sydney Book Club),
the much-heralded second-prize winner
in that firm’s recent novel competition,
looks exactly like a second-prize winner
in a novel competition. It has no
subtlety, no literary style, no plot
construction; it lumbers along with
a dull narrative flow. But —it has for hero
a noble bushranger, and it is packed with in-
cident, realistically depicted. It is true that
the bushranger is something new in Austra-
lian bushranging: he is a Hebrew. But he
has all the other marks of the ideal bush-
ranging hero. He rides the finest horse in
Australia ; he is courteous and cultured; he
possesses a superb physique; and he had
every excuse for taking to the bush. And
David Hennessey has made good use of his
background: he contrives to give a trust-
worthy picture of the conditions that made
bushranging possible. And he ends with the
inevitable midnight wedding and the escape
of the hero and his wife. What more can
the average reader want? It is not a great
book, but it is a quite readable book, fully
up to the level of several of the bushranging
yarns published by the N.S.W. Bookstall
Co.
J*
D. H. Lawrence, author of those curious
inchoate novels, The White Peacock and I’he
Trespasser, has gained command of his
material in his latest work. Sons and Lovers
(Duckworth, per Sydney Book Club). This
is a story of a miner’s family, painstakingly
detailed at such length as to frighten the
frivolous reader. Yet the people are real
people, unforgettable. The author has a
curious disdain for the conventional formula
of the novel. Thus he interests us mightily
in the eldest son and in his love affair, then
casually kills him and allows the girl to drop
out of his narrative. Having disposed of
him, Lawrence takes up a younger son and
makes him the hero. But the construction of
the novel is sound; for the chief character in
the book is the mother, who, disappointed in
her drunken husband, transfers her love in
turn to her sons, and sees all her hopes sud-
denly smitten down in the death of her
eldest born, but finds in the love of her other
son the compensation she had thought for
ever out of her reach. The figure of this
commonplace woman is depicted with an ex-
traordinary impressiveness; and her death
makes the scene of horror hardly to be
matched in any literature except the Rus-
sian. The love affairs of the second son are
told with a frankness that seems innocuous,
surcharged as Lawrence’s realism is with
poetry, and informed throughout with a
keen delight in nature. Indeed, were Law-
rence to learn, as he is learning, to render
his material more submissive to his hands,
to control, the course of his story, he should
some day be regarded as the legitimate suc-
cessor of Thomas Hardy.
The new, cheap edition of Roget’s
Thesaurus (Longmans, Green) places in the
literary neophyte’s hands, for 3s. (in Aus-
tralia) the one indispensable tool for his
craftsmanship. It was in 1852 that Dr.
Roget published his first Thesaurus, and,
till this present edition, the work had been
.reprinted from the 1855 edition with little
alteration. His son has now enlarged the
work and brought it up to date, with a new
and elaborate index. A dictionary is of less
use to the writer than a Thesaurus; for the
problem which a dictionary purposes to solve
is this: The word being given, to find its
signification. In a Thesaurus the problem
is the converse: The idea, being given, to
find the words to express that idea. The
problem solved by the dictionary is a
reader’s problem: that solved by the
Thesaurus is a writer’s problem.
The regulations framed for the conduct of
the Australian National Song competition,
by the Musical Association of' N.S.W., to
whom Farmer and Co. presented £lOO for a
set of verses, comprise: The song must not
exceed 40 lines; the verses must not
have hitherto been published, and must be
“in pure English, free from colloquialisms
or slang” ; each sheet of MS. must bear a
nom de plume for identification purposes,
but must not have the writer’s name; a
postal note, or stamps, for 55., a sealed
envelope containing the name and pen-name
of the competitor, and bearing the pen-name
on the outside of the envelope, must accom-
pany the MS., the package to be registered
and addressed: “National Song Competi-
tion.” the Hon. Secretary, the Musical Asso-
ciation of N.S.W., 16 Paling’s Buildings,
Ash-street, Sydney, and forwarded to reach
that address by September 30. Competitors
submitting more than one entry must follow
these directions for each entry, including a
separate entrance fee of ss. MS. will not be
returned, and the sealed envelopes will be
destroyed. The adjudicators are: Profs.
MacCallum and Tucker, and W. Arundel
Orchard, Mus. Bac.

The Inky Way.
J. M. Le Sage, managing editor of London Daily
Telegraph, has completed half a century of con-
tinuous service on that paper, and has had to un-
dergo the usual dinner and silver salvers. At the
dinner he reminisced about his early reporting ex-
periences. He beat “Billy” Russell, of the Times,
in the race to get the first news to London of the
fall of Paris and the entry of the Germans. He got
first news of the death of Lord Palmerston, Prime
Minister, and he reported the last speech of Richard
Cobden. He was the friend of George Augustus
Sala—remembered in Australia for his rubicund nose,
his dreary lecturing, and his failure to turn up at
the Melbourne Yorrick Club dinner in his honor.
Whether Sala fell asleep at Menzies’ or was otherwise
incapable of attending was never definitely ascer-
tained. H. M. Stanley was also an intimate friend.
After Stanley had found Livingstone, Le Sage asked
him whether he had any other big job in mind. He
replied: “Yes, a big journey of exploration through
darkest Africa.” Lord Burnham, who owns the
Telegraph, asked the cost. Stanley named a figure
(it turned out to be £20,000 short of the actual).
Burnham acquiesced, and the “Darkest Africa” affair
was fixed up in three minutes.
From London: The Era —generally known in Lon-
don as the Actor’s Bible —announces that in future
its price will be a penny instead of the old sixpence.
This must be one of the most sudden reductions on
journalistic record. It also changes its publishing
day from Saturday to Wednesday, thereby gaining
24 hours on its rival, the Stage, which comes out
on Thursday. The idea evidently is to get square
by a more vigorous pursuit of the nimble advt.
“J. 8.”: Dropped a well-worn pen at Auckland
(M.L.) lately, P. H. Robertson, fine old pressman.
Starting as so many good men have done, from the
mechanical side, he made himself a master of short-
hand (his note was as beautiful as a high-school
girl’s first love letter), and developed a style of writ-
ing rarely seen in these days of hurry-up. Dong ago,
in Sydney, when his friend Horace Rogers, of the
Herald, was a young blood about town, there was
no great distance to go with the diploma for the
most concise paragraphists and law reporters. They
worked much in line. After courts, Rogers supplied
dramatic criticism and “Robbie” musical criticism.
And age did not rust Robertson in that line. Not
many years back he drifted on to Wellington (M.L.)
Times, and did for that harassing paper many a
criticism that astonished musicians of world-wide
reputation who had expected to meet with outland-
ish do-re-mi ignorance, and were blessedly disap-
pointed.
“Naumai”: Peter Robertson, the well-known
Maoriland pressman, who died at Auckland a few
days ago, commenced as a comp, in that city, and
at one period “snatched stamps” for John Ballance
on the Wanganui Herald. After drifting to the
writing side of the business, he roamed over a
good deal of Australasia, and the old hands at
Brisbane, Melbourne, Sydney and various other vil-
lages on both sides of the Tasman still enthuse over
his musical criticisms. Lemare, the organist, stated
that Robertson’s notices of his recitals were the best
from the musical point of view that had been printed
in Australasia. ,
Another inky wayfarer with a long and profitable
record draws off the Sydney track when W. J.
Bailey goes to Hobart presently. Somewhere about
15 years ago he joined S. M. Herald and helped to
keep the wolf at a reasonable distance from Mr.
James’s doorstep. After about seven years’ struggle
with the animal he skied his inkpot amid general
lamentations, and took over the publicity work of
Farmer’s, Ltd., further up the street. He remained
there until a few days ago, when he decided to go
into business for himself and ascertain how it felt
to collect all the dividends.
The Smart Set (U.S.) for July contains an Aus-
tralian feature. It describes a journey down the
Murray, the hero being the author, R. M. Hallett, and
contains bales of local color. Also, there is a con-
signment of “cuckoo borroughs,” whose idiotic cackle
startled the hero so that he became considerably
annoyed. That’s the worst of these cursed, small
local-governing bodies.
* * * *
A Canterbury (M.L.) man lately wrote a note re-
garding the scarcity of lambs in that province, and
remarked: “This shortage is attributed to the high
mortality among the ewes.” But the comp, knew
better, and left out the first “t” in mortality.”
In the same spirit, when the lady reporter wrote of
a wedding, “The bridesmaids’ dresses were modelled
from a picture of Laurence’s,” the comp, altered it
to “muddled,” and got much nearer the truth.
A new novel of Fleet-street, “Outside The Ark,”
by Mrs. Adelaide Holt, has some cynical remarks on
journalism by a successful editor. The only sure
way with a Woman’s Page is to have it written by a
man. “Don’t have a woman, or you will find your
page will be exactly like every other Woman’s Page
in London. Women are such damned sheep, bless
’em.” The writer, who wants to pose as prophet
must get notoriety first. “Go out and dance per-
petually in the limelight an indecorous can-can, if
you want people to appreciate the spirituality of
the article you are yearning to write for a great
quarterly review. That’s journalism.” And a class
to which so many Australian writers really prefer to
belong is scornfully dismissed as “That well-known
product of the journalistic farmyard who calls him-
self a free lance, while his friends call him a
failure.”
NEXT FEDERAL ELECTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I mot him at the house of Bung,
And bought him Bacchic fire.
He seemed a most deserving case,
Dull care was stamped upon his face,
And want, and sorrow dire ;
Worst thing of all, it did appear
11c took no pleasure in his beer 1
“What ails you, brother?” I inquired.
He blinked and choked a sob.
“It’s all this Labor Government,"
He muttered, “Yes, the blighters went
And made me lose my job.
I used to make five quid, no less,
Each week, by writing to the press!
“My pen-name? I had many, sir.
1 was ‘A Working Man.’
‘Fair Play,’ ‘Pro Bono Publico,’
‘Disgusted Unionist,’ also
‘Non-Union Artisan’ ;
When Lard and Labor were at strife,
I often was ‘A Striker’s Wife’!
“The person on the land opined,
What ‘Farmer’ said was right;
And citizens were much distressed
When ‘Banker’ wrote that interest
Was high, and money tight!
When I held forth as ‘City Man,’
I nvestors grabbed their cash and ran !
“Now I am silent. If I wrote,
I’d have to sign each day!”
Quoth I: “My friend, don’t think me rude
Your silence will be misconstrued ;
Your enemies will say:
‘To sign his name he can’t afford,
For lie’s a liar and a fraud !’ ”
VICTORIA.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fish and Chips.
B * l _?- nd G hips,” the maiden cried—
Tho a fi! Dg through the menu bored her.
Ihen the waiter coughed (aside),
bravely bowed and took t'he order.
Wever had more lovely lips
bent h»i o£f for Fish and Chips.
Though the girl looked wondrous fair
j Ssiied her boa sable,
Ked I blushed, and feigned to stare
July at the supper table.
What a bust, and waist and hips !
V\ hat a queen for Fish and Chips !
“Penny for your thoughts,” said she,
‘Coodness! What have you been
drinking?”
Two fine eyes she turned on me,
yue of them serenely winking.
(h ull I'd been of merry quips
Fre she ordered Fish and Chips.)
Not a word could I reply,
But my silence did not matter.
Dainty feeders, seated nigh,
r „. Gr l i . nned - methought, at Polly’s chatter,
iingling to my finger tips
Still I mused on Fish and Chips.
Burdened with a sense of shame,
How was I to be amusing?
Polly (what an awful name!)
Put my silence down to boozing.
All her vulgar verbal slips
Harmonised with Fish and Chips.
Beauty ripe as hers could tempt
Stoics into sinful follies
(Bishops are not quite exempt
From the risk of charms like Polly’s) ;
My romance was torn to strips
By her flippant “Fish and Chips.”
If, when supping & la carte,
Passion’s last surviving ember
Flickers feebly in my heart
And my bygones I remember,
Like the lash of cruel whips
Comes the thought of “Fish and Chips.”
VICTOBIA.
O. K.
Gippslanu Gullies.
Wind waves over the white cloud ridges,
Wan noon over the timber’s crest.
Tangled bracken and hop-scrub yellow
Whispering weirdly in deep unrest.
For the bush sprite brushes their silent cover
By stealthy tracks where the dews drip
white,
By gleaming holes in the crooked cutting
And gullies dipped in a wedge of light.
Lonely and sad I hear her calling,
And one w:ld lover —the creek —sings back
From steep dipped gullies where gum blooms
dropping
Ripple the moons in his deep, wet track.
But she flits away in a whispered fancy
And drifts in light by the whimpering
shrub.
And peers through boughs of the wind-
washed wattles
And ghost-white blooms in the cauliflower
scrub.
And down by the mounded trunks uprooted
She dips her feet in the well’s white flume,
By a floating web of misted silver
Flung in the fronds of the king fern’s
plume.
By dim locked archway and saplings bended
She flits in phantoms of drifted light....
By wilted bark on the big trunks tapping
And boughs a-throb to the pulse of night.
But hours wane dim in the deep fern gullies,
And cometh the mysteries the shadows
teach ;
For the moon’s white wreck on the ridge is
dipping
Her drifting hull on a cloud-washed beach.
And fainter and farther the bush sprites
singing,
And lonelily lilting it dies away.
Till only she whispers to white stars ebbing
Through deen locked branches and trunks
dawn-gray.
And she sinks like a nun by the Holy Chalice,
And kneels in silence awhile to pray
To the Host aflame on an azured altar,
Shrined in the love of the White God, Day.
Victoria.
L. KENNY.
Morning.
Night slowly dies ; the changing skies
With wondrous hues its death are scorn-
ing.
Dawn breaks, and all the little pieces
Go into morning.
A soft breeze freshly from the seas
Wakens the world. Within my mind
A thought stirs: Would I, like the dawn,
Could raise the wind !
A lark soars upward to the sky
And trills its joy to meet the sun ;
I feel that way, too, for the dawn
Is full of fun.
Yes, there is morning in my heart.
And care is no more in my mind;
For all my troubles with the night
Are left behind.
Left far behind where trouble may
Seek me again, and yet again ;
Knock at the door or climb the backyard
fence,
But all in vain!
Thus in my heart the worth of dawn
Sung much by poets, well is proved ;
For morning finds me in a suburb new
That is, I’ve moved.
Fresh seems the morning with its breeze;
Fresh hopes from dawn mv bosom gets ;
Fresh thoughts I think, and right in front
of me
Are long, fresh debts.
N.S.W.

all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How Ho Address 'Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

fused.

publishers. The right' to reproduce or. translate any

THURSDAY, AUGUST 14, 1913.
THE GROWING DEPRESSION IN
AUSTRALIA —ALSO SOME RE-
MARKS ABOUT A PERSIAN KING.
Tiie above-mentioned animal—the Depres-
sion, not the Persian King—which has
hardly been alluded to (in Sydney, at all
events) for eight or nine years, has, again
become a subject of conversation. There
seems to be a sort of falling-off everywhere.
The usually-crowded pub is- a little less
crowded, and consequently the, .blue ruin is
served with greater alacrity. The fatted
demoiselle at the tea-room is a trifle more
prompt with the Irish stew, and a shade less
arrogant with her eye, through the falling
off in trade. The expensive barber has a
man waiting for a customer instead of six
customers waiting for a man. from which
it is inferred that the individual who used
to have a shave, face massage, singe, sham-
poo and expert opinion on his pimple now
does with a shave alone, and. takes up only
a quarter the time that he used to do. The
shipping trade is not so good as it was. The
bank manager inclines to go down on all
fours in front of the new depositor and to
fix an eye of scorn on the person who wants
an advance, whereas he once did the reverse.
And, as already mentioned, there is a tend-
ency, which has not been noticeable for quite
a number of years, to talk about the Depres-
sion. At the same time there is a great
effort (so far as Sydney is concerned) to set
down the sudden and visible slump to the
small-pox epidemic, and thus shift the re-
sponsibility off the shoulders of man, and
plant it beside the throne of God, it being a
pleasant, easy-going theory that all diseases
are the invention of the Almighty. But an
epidemic which has been raging for months
without (up to the date when this was
written) killing a single white inhabitant is
hardly to be taken seriously. Certainly it
isn’t to be taken as the reason why people
begin talking about the Depression as if it
were a familiar spectre and a constant
haunter of the Cornstalk highways. New
South Wales, despite its small-pox epidemic,
has almost the lowest death-rate in the
world. In these circumstances it is absurd
to profess that business is falling off because
the Shadow of Death is hanging over the
Ma State’s capital.
* * * *
The fact is that the great bulk of the com-
munity suffers from shortage of memory.
One of the wisest among Asiatic kings,
Darius, of Persia, made a note of this fact,
so he hired a man to remind him three times
a day that the soldiery of Athens had made
things unpleasant for him, and had dragged
his pomp and his banner in the dust. This
was to prevent him letting his military
establishment fall into decay, and to remind
him that it comported with his dignity to
get square with the City of the Violet Crown
before he died. Australia, and especially
New South Wales, lacked the foresight to en-
gage a person of that sort. It might be
good for the State and the Commonwealth if,
every day on which Parliament was sitting,
the legislative proceedings commenced with a
brief address from a tall, thin person attired
in tight-fitting black, and painted to represent
a skeleton. He should explain to the as-
sembled legislators that the drought will
come again, even if it doesn’t run according
to Clement Wragge’s timetable; that the
sheep and ca ttle will one day die ; that the
crops will one day diminish ; that the Lon-
don money market will slump; that the old
loan will fall due and the new loan become
scarce; that the interest rate will rise, and
the land boom will fall, and the wild cat
will become thin about the ribs for want of
food; and that it isn’t possible for local
wages to go up, and cheap-labor imports to
increase, and high-wage local exports to
diminish, without some kind of a breakage.
In fact, he should remind the politicians that
men and booms are alike mortal. Then he
might justly add that invasion and rapine
will come in time, for they come to every
country in time ; and that the country which
prefers to import foreign goods, instead of
building ud its defences by importing the
people who make the goods, is living in a
fool’s paradise. And, having spoken his
daily warning, this hired apparition would
gather up his skull and cross-bones, his
coffin, his tombstone, his hatchment, his
black flag, his. cypress tree, and the other
apparatus of his job, and depart silently by
the back door. After that he would get into
h waiting hearse and retire with dignity to
his home (painted black) alongside the ceme-
tery.
The value of a stipendiary Solomon
Eagle .would depend on the amount of atten-
tion paid to his words. Certainly he wouldn’t
lack for words in Australia, and especially
in New South Wales, if he knew his sub-
ject. The State Government—the unspeak-
able Holman-McGowen-Griffith combina-
tion —has closed the financial year with a
shortage of £1,228,385—50me estimates put
it at over £1,(300,000. This is despite the
fact that it had the biggest revenue which a
New South Wales Ministry ever enjoyed,
that it cut down the payments to the public
works fund, the closer settlement fund and
the sinking fund, and that it borrowed on a
wholly unprecedented scale. The situation
is almost hopeless, according to present
methods, and it is impossible to propound
any scheme for putting the finances square
which won’t involve a huge and unpopular
increase of taxation, or a vast and unpopular
cutting down of the easy-going, casual, af-
fable system of expenditure on which the
Harold Skimpole Government, or Ministry
of All the Unreliables, depends for votes. So,
as the present New South Wales Legislature
must presently expire through effluxion
of time, the Holman-McGowen-Griffith
crowd proposes to present no formal finan-
cial statement whatsoever, unless it is driven
to it. by public, opprobrium. It won’t say
definitely how it got into the present mess,
or how it intends to get out of it, or how it
proposes to prevent next year’s mess being as
bad. or perhaps worse. It will make a few
perfunctory remarks, and then invite the
electors to take a leap in the dark. In
gambling parlance, they will be requested to
“go it blind.” If they answer to the call
and send the Holman-McGowen-Griffith
aggregation back to office, the new Legisla-
ture will meet when the financial year is
about half-finished. It will be too late to
repair the wreck for the year which ends
on oOth June, 1914, and the crowd of impos-
sible muddlers will probably be anchored to
its. job till the end of 1916, no matter what
ruin and confusion it brings about. There
is every reason to believe that the crowd in
question wouldn’t have a hope if it told the
country in detail, before the election, either
how many men it was going to sack, or how
many salaries it was going to cut down, or
how many works it was going to hang up, in
order to reduce the expenditure to the level
of the income, or how many new taxes it
proposed to put on in order to raise the in-
come to the level of the expenditure. So
it intends, so far as any formal Budget is
concerned, to build up a masterly silence.
Incidentally, it pleads as an excuse for the
monetary downfall, that the subsidy from the
Australian Treasury is less than it used to
be. That is certainly true, but Queensland
and Victoria and S. Australia and Westralia
have -suffered from the same disadvantage,
and they haven’t made the heroic smash that
the Ma State—alleged to be the richest
of all —has made under the control of
Holman, McGowen, Griffith and co. And
the decline in the Commonwealth subsidy has
nothing to do with the fact that the big
profit formerly earned by the State railways
has turned into a deficit; that the tram-
ways are not looking so well as they did ;
that the State Government is offering its
friend Bull-Cohen of England high inter-
est for comparatively small loans, where it
used to get big loans at fairly low interest;
or that the State is littered with unfinished
lines which were started in a spirit of joyful
over-night drunkenness, and repented of
when the morning brought an aching head
and partial sanity and a vision of empty
bottles.
Though the trouble is worst in New
South Wales, because New South Wales has
hired the worst gang of spendthrifts and
political profligates to run its affairs, the
fact remains that the affairs of Australia in
general are not so gorgeous as they were.
Whether the trouble arises out of scarce
rainfall, or accident, or the cussedness of
inanimate objects, it is certain that the sheep
and cattle, which constitute a very large
part of the country’s wealth, are again
diminishing. A large section of New South
\\ ales and S. Australia is visibly drying up.
The mineral output grows less. The loans
are harder to get and cost more.
Immigration is not so brisk. There is a
tall not only in bank deposits, but in the
banks cash reserves. Agriculture isn’t ex-
panding as it should do. Under the ab-
surdly inefficient tariff the foreign imports
are still very much larger than the country
can honestly afford to pay for. Everything
in the way of finance, loans, faith, “froth,
boom, lOU, swelled head and unpaid-for
pomp is on too big a scale as the result of
the sanguine Australian temperament and
10 years of good fortune. Everything in
the way of plain, hard work and plain self-
reliance is on too small a scale. This coun-
try would do well to read up the history
of Darius, son of Gusiitasp, the great king
who hired a civil servant to remind him
three times daily that he had once been
badly licked, so that he might not acquire
undue magnificence of the cranium in his
prosperity and begin to think himself im-
mortal._ As a result of this sane outlook
upon life Darius was a very successful
ruler. On fhe other hand a Holman or a
Nebuchadnezzar is liable to go mad with
political inflation, and be chased out into
the paddock at last to eat grass.
THE PARTY GOVERNMENT BLIGHT
IN ITS RELATION TO THE N. S.
WALES CIVIL SERVICE.
This is the sermon of ‘TO-2-4.” :
Some day someone of a statistical turn of mind
will begin a calculation of the number of curses
which the different Australian States suffer from
as the outcome of their adherence to the Party
Government system. He will never finish. The
subject is too vast. There is hardly a political ill
that does not arise directly or indirectly from this
rotten old device. Take, for example, a clamant
scandal—the present condition of the Civil Service
in N. S. Wales. From the earliest ages the Civil
otTv ice has been used as a handy dumping-ground
for the friends, dependents and hangers-on of the
politicians in power for the time being. In the
days of plural voting a man with 10 or a dozen
votes who was in need of a little ready money could
always command a job as public service commis-
sioner from the Ministry that was in power, in his
new capacity he inquired earnestly into some utterly
immaterial question; drew a fee' of £lOO or
1000 ; and later returned to private life happy
and solvent. The sort of man who not only had
half a dozen or more votes of his own, but who
controlled perhaps 30 or 40 other votes, was in a
position to aspire after a permanent billet; and if
so disposed he mostly got it. Since, in the nature
of things, about 90 per cent, of these appointments
were made cynically irrespective of merit, the Ser-
vice ultimately reached deplorable depths in the
way of incapacity and leisureliness. So far as most
of its members were concerned, life was always
afternoon—and five o’clock p.m. at that. The thing
became a scandal. In the early ’Bo’s Pakkes who
had himself appointed as many wasters to fat billets
as any other man, created the Public Service Inquiry
Commission. The Commission was composed of re-
putable and able men. Their duty was to identify
and, where possible, gently to oust the more glaring
incompetents; and especially to see that such new
men as were appointed had some sort of a claim on
their billets. The Commissioners quickly discovered,
to their fury, that they were meant to be ciphers!
No incompetents were shifted. All their suggestions
were blandly disregarded by the Government.
1 olitically influential nonentities continued to be
shoved into the Service by Ministers and Ministers’
friends. In brief, the abuses of the old regime
raged as briskly as ever. Much to the annoyance
of Parkes, the then Premier, James Watson, Chair-
man of the Commission, hurriedly and very firmly
resigned. The Commission carried on for a while,
but it was hopelessly discredited. The time had be-
come obviously ripe for the handing of some fresh
sop to scandalised public opinion.
It was at about this stage that the Public Service
Board was established. The original idea was that
it should be entirely free from political influence.
The good old wooden-headed elector had this point
inserted into his intellect with considerable force.
Of course, he began by believing the yarn He
always does. The fact is that the Board has never
been free from political influence, and, under the
existing system, never can be. Sometimes the
Board is more under the thumb of Ministers, and
sometimes it is less so ; but always they have a large
share in its activities. When elections are far away
on the distant horizon, or when the party in power
feels absolutely sure of getting back again by a
commanding majority, the tendency is to run things
on what may be termed the level, more or less. But
in every case where votes are badly needed and the
chance of an appoinlment looms, the candidate’s
eligibility for the billet is decided almost entirely
by a consideration of how much support he
is likely to bring to the party in power. The effect
on the State, so far as the value of the services
rendered is concerned, may be gi ged by estimating
the sort of bank manager or , -captain or inter-
national cricketer a country would get if each man
were selected not because of his merits in any one
of these lines of effort, but on the basis (sav) that
he had made more motor-trips to Manly Beach than
any of his competitors, or had read more issues of
the S. M. Herald, or was distinguished by some-
thing else equally immaterial. It is related of a
fine and particularly frank old N. S. Wales citizen
that, on being tackled by a friend concerning his
invariable custom of appointing near relatives to
the best billets at his command, he expressed the
view that it was both natural and right to give
preference to one’s own. The friend said doubtfully:
“Well, other things equal, I suppose it is.” “Other
things equal, be damned,” said the ancient, spiritedly.
“I’d give a relative a job as against a genius.” The
fact that something closely resembling this attitude
has alwavs been in vogue amongst N.S.W. politicians,
as regards the Civil Service, has done a lot to make
it the pleasaunce of mediocrity which it notoriously
is.
Another powerful factor in the same direction is
the seniority system. An immutable and likewise
imbecile rule lays it down that if a member of the
Service isn’t incompetent to an absolutely flaming
extent, he shall be preferred, in the matter of ap-
pointments, to any outsider, no matter how brilliant.
The politicians—Liberal or Labor—wouldn’t dare to
alter this state of affairs, if they could. The senior
of a department who was not appointed to a charge
position whieh tradition had previously informed
him was to be his, could, by influencing juniors and
the like, lose the party that was responsible for
slighting him a heap of votes. So the party
lies low on the weather bow in all such cases, and
refrains from interfering, even when the most ah-
surdly foolish old gentlemen rise to the dizziest of
official pinnacles by the mere effluxion of time ii
is hardly necessary to stress the fact that such «
demented arrangement does not prevail in ant
other walk of life. In commerce and in the uro
fessions venerable age counts for nothing by com
panson with brains. Even in the'naval and military
services there are careful provisions for the ejecting
of the futile and the unfit. The haberdashery firm
which, as a matter of course, elevated its oldest
floor-walker to the position of general manager
would automatically begin to render itself
ripe for the Bankruptcy Court. The newspaper
which was habitually edited by the most ancient
man in the building would soon burst. The hotel
which transferred the barman to the office merelv
because he had been employed about the premises
longer than anyone else would pine and die. Such
propositions are plain to the dullest intelligence
The Civil Service of N. S. Wales (and of the other
States as well) is full, figuratively speaking, of
general managers who should be floor-walkers’ or
nothing; it is creeping with editors who are fit
only to sweep out the office; and it fairly teems
with hotel-managers who should never have aspired
to anything higher than the honest, forthright toil
of pulling beer. There is hardly one first-rate man
in the whole vast institution. The slackness and
casualness and splendid indifference to such trifles as
time and the public convenience which prevail on
all sides, fill the normal man with a paralysed sur-
prise that is akin to reverence. It is all so different
from the eager, hasty scuffling methods of ordinary'
life. Energy, drive, initiative, administrative ability,
commercial and financial knowledge—all are lacking’
to an astonishing degree in the N. S. Wales Civil
Service. It is not too much to say of many of its
highly-paid officers that, if they were cast sudden]'’
upon the grim mercies of that outside world of
which they know' so little, they would'have a parlous
job of it to connect wiyj, a junior clerkship.
To put the position in plain terms, N. S. Wales
(and the anguished old Mother State is merely cited
as an example; the other States being in much the
same plight) is paying the majority of its public ser-
vants heavily for work which is either not being
done at all, or which is being done extremely badly.
Thirtv-odd years ago it awoke to an understanding
of something of the sort. It is now up to it to
awake again. The Board system has proved a fail-
ure. The wisest move would probably be for the
Government to select some first-class man; appoint
him Commissioner over the entire service ; and make
him as completely independent of political pressure
as the present. Chief Railway Commissioner is. He
would need to be intimately acquainted with local
conditions and personalities, and hence would re-
quire to be of local origin or upbringing. He would
be instructed to make all appointments to the ser-
vice on purely business lines ; that is to say, promo-
tion would depend not on age but upon capacity.
From the beginning of the new regime applicants for
position in the Service would be clearly told, on
joining, that their promotion to charge or semi-
charge billets would be dependent purely on their
merits. The Civil Service would thus cease gradually
to be a sort of charitable institution devoted largely
to the housing of the friends of the party in power';
and the State would begin to be governed on some-
thing the same lines as a well-run business venture.
The present crowd of Service incompetents—the
combined result of the efforts of dozens of different
administrations to keep in power—would be gradually
eliminated. But there is a lion in the path, and a
reef in the fairway And the name of the obstruc-
tion is, in each instance, Party Government—the
imported imposture which everybody deplores, but
which no politician appears to have sufficient nerve
to tackle.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia’s Cool North.
The Railways Investigation Committee,
which is now exploring the Northern Terri-
tory, has been heard from, and the news is
cheering. It hasn’t got a very long distance
yet, but it has travelled as far as Bitter
springs, which is about 200 miles or less
hom the coast. So, in theory, these
OoLUMBUSES-by-land have only begun to
climb on to the tableland and aren’t half-
way, or a quarter-way, up the roof of Aus-
tralia. There are, as the crow flies, 700
more miles of gradual ascent to be nego-
tiated before they reach the inward top of
this country. Yet even on the road to Bitter
Springs they speak of cold nights (down to
degrees) ; cool days (down to 40 deg-
rees), “ideal weather” and an almost com-
plete absence of flies and mosquitoes.
Also they enthuse over the good condition
of the cattle and the high promise of the
soil. This is along the route which would
be followed by a really central railway ex-
tending from Port Darwin in the north to
Port Augusta and Adelaide in the south.
The average unthinking person, whose edu-
cation is based on the daiiy paper, requires
to be spoken to on the head with an axe to
make him realise that Australia owns a
high, vast pleasant—sometimes very chilly
region in the Territory ; that it has in its
centre a huge cluster of hills with a rainfall
and a fairly good river system; and that
most of this central region, if it were opened
up by a railway, would be an ideal land for
the best class of European immigrant. All
he knows, or thinks he knows, at present is
the temperature of low-lying Port Darwiu
and a flowerpot-full of land adjacent thereto.
Certainly if the party which wants to drag
everything to the biggest centres of popula-
tion has. its way, and the transcontinental
railway is taken round at great extra mile-
age and cost through the hot, dry, depressed
regions of western N.S.W. and Queensland,
and nailed down to almost-desert country
like that around Broken Hill —in that case
the great fertile tableland and the inland
mountain groups might as well, for the pre-
sent, be . non-existent. But however that
may be, it is up to Australia to remember
that it owns the place, and that the place is
everything . which the nigger-labor crowd
says it isn’t. It is a white man’s land if
the white man is regarded as a low-tempera-
ture animal, and it has no special points
for Ram Rhag and his brother Ghoeab
Turban. The local citizen wants these
facts gummed in his hat, notified on his bed-
room wall, printed on his tram ticket, men-
tioned to him at church, inscribed on his
wash-bill and specified on the label of his
whisky bottle. And then the news might at
last soak in upon him and do him good.
+
The Corpse on the Doorstep.
The recent maternity bonus frauds trial in
Melbourne disclosed the unpleasant fact that
at an unregistered private hospital (regis-
tration had been refused, but apparently it
was nobody’s business to see that the law
wasn’t defied) at least three mothers of ille-
gitimate children had parted with their
infants at birth to persons of whose names
and addresses they were ignorant. And
one of the three gave away £3O along with
the incubus. Under the Vic. Infant Life
Protection Act all such trafficking in babies
is illegal. Meanwhile, however, unregis-
tered private hospitals flourish, and newl.v-
born babies continue to be handed over to


unknown foster-parents—and no questions
asked. Is it any wonder that citizens going
home through quiet streets at night are
perpetually stumbling over deceased infants?
The child is born in a “hospital” over which
there is no State supervision ; the mother
mostly bears an assumed name, and the
child, which is registered under that bogus
name, is untraceable. In fact, there is no
certainty that it is registered under any
name, or that it ever achieved a legal
existence. It is born —and “adopted” ; and
that is the end of it. The whole business
suggests a calculated, organised system of
murder winked at by the State. The un-
identifiable dead babies, which have been
found so often of late, are proof of it. So
the State that urgently needs population
that pays away thousands of pounds yearly
to get an inferior class, deliberately permits
the waste —to put it delicately—of hundreds,
if not thousands, of the most virile, acclima-
tised and otherwise desirable lives obtain-
able. A State Foundling Hospital—an in-
stitution where the mother who, from fear
of obloquy, from financial necessity or other-
reasons, finds herself compelled to part with
her baby, could leave the incubus in peace
and safety—would obviate an incalculable
amount of murder, misery and sundries. And
it would be of more benefit to Australia than
four George Reids or 40 Percy Hunters.
What valid objection is there to the found-
ing of a State Foundling Home —or, rather,
a lot of them, scattered over the country?
A Parson Who Appears to be Maligning
Himself.
The Rev. Yarrington, of Sydney, had some
more to say, last week, on the subject of
Sydney’s alleged opium-dens, and his own
reticence as to their whereabouts :
“I consider,” he told the press, “that it is not my
duty as one who works in and about the places
where opium smoking is carried on to inform the
authorities of their (the dens’) existence, and I
think Mr. Flowers should recognise my position in
that respect. My duties as a worker in the slums
°f Sydney take me to places where I know opium

wishes, the good work done by me and my band of

The Scare that is Moribund; and the
Far More Needed Scare that is Still
Unborn.
For weeks past the Sydney daily papers
have been conducting a strenuous anti-small-
pox crusade. Columns and columns have
been filled with warnings in regard to the
hideous possibilities which face the unvac-
cinated. No reasonable citizen is likely to
object to that. But with the opening of
Parliament and the renascence, so to speak,
of the ever-stimulating Willis and other
happenings of interest, the papers have to a
great extent gone out of the Solomon
Eagle business as regards the small-pox
epidemic. This is quite illogical and wrong.
The people who were vaccinated during last
month needed little persuasion. The 50 per
cent, who have not been operated on so far
require to be frightened most thoroughly and
nagged at with extraordinary assiduity
if they are to be driven to the vac-
cination depots. If there is anything in the
dictum of the Health authorities that Sydney
will not be safe till practically the whole
population has oeen treated, the papers
should be now writing up the menace more
furiously than ever. But the searcher after
strict logic has a way of drawing blank
where these sheets are concerned. Take
their persistent silence on an infinitely
greater and more prevalent horror than
small-pox—namely syphilis. They shrink
from so much as giving the hideous disease
its proper name, preferring with old lady-
like gentility to indicate it, where indication
is absolutely necessary, by means of some
form of polite periphrasis. Syphilis is kill-
ing people every day in every city and in
every fair-sized town in N. S. Wales. Small-
pox has killed no one in N.S.W. to date.
The effects of the complaint which hardly
anybody dares to name are infinitely more
loathsome and agonising than the effects of
the one which everybody discusses as though
it were an old friend. The small-pox con-
valescent is, after a time, incapable of doing
any damage to his neighbor. Sufferers from
the other disease are liable, in certain cir-
cumstances, to be menaces to those with
whom they come in contact for the remainder
of their lives. Small-pox is not hereditary ;
and though it may kill or blind its victim
it rarely afflicts him with subtly dreadful
after-effects. Syphilis is hereditary. It is,
as Brieux has well remarked, above all
else a child-murderer.” Club-footed chil-
dren, hump-backed children, twisted chil-
dren children with ill-shapen skulls and
diseased hips and curved spines and a thou-
sand other physical miseries are so because
one or other of their progenitors was not
nrotected from the syphilis curse. It would
be worth a little plain speech now. merely
to save future generations from having their
innocent lives blasted by this disease. And
then there is the case of the unhappy wretch
who contracts it personally .Locomotor
ataxia, softening of the brain, general
of the insane, and a thousand
dreadful nerve troubles threaten him He
is the worst sort of pariah. . If the
V S Wales small-pox epidemic lasts,
at nt vea ra.e * . ? t«t,on. for
” population will, still remain
unattached ; and if it maintains its present
ack of virulence no one will have died.
How horribly different a. storw sypbflie- will
Kto tell! And the fe.tnre of the bna,-
ness which makes it resemble a sort of joke
is that the doctors have ready to their
hands a means of robbing this scourge of
tu |‘ y three-parts of its terrors. Whether
salvarsan is a lasting cure for acquired
syphilis is a question that cannot be estab-
lished for decades. But that it renders the
person who is treated with it incapable of
spreading infection is now accepted by the
medical profession as a proved fact. ' Why
is not syphilis made a notifiable disease like
diphtheria and the plague? Why is there
no heavy fine imposed on the citizen who
tails to tell the authorities of the existence
°* case of what he has reasonable grounds
to believe to be syphilis, as there is in the
case of the man who helps to hush up a
small-pox incident? One reason is that our
daily press, inspired by a nationally-calam-
ltous spirit of mock modesty, fears to start
an agitation in this matter. It is prepared
to fight small-pox with all the weapons in
its armory ; at syphilis it will not deal one
blow—even as a man might perform feats
of tremendous, daring in regard to a terrier
that was robbing his hen-roost, while know-
ingly leaving his wife and children to be
assassinated in their beds.
A Change that is Arriving too Date.
Various indications point to the intention
ot Melbourne’s reconstructed Harbor Trust
to largely adopt the scheme, outlined in The
Bulletin years ago, whereby the temporary
capital of the Commonwealth would be sup-
plied with an adequate, up-to-date harbor
and port, equipment. The scheme provides
for additional dock and wharf accommoda-
tion ; the reservation of the huge area behind
Fishermen’s Bend for factories, warehouses,
elevator sites, etc.; direct railway communi-
cation between Spencer-street and Port Mel-
bourne, through this now unoccupied land; a
future straight cut between the city and
Hobson’s Bay, and other co-ordinate items.
It is estimated to cost between £3,000,000
and £4,000,000, and the Trust proposes to
spread this outlay over 20 years. The idea
threatens to be effective in creating adequate
port accommodation—the trouble is that
when the accommodation is finally provided
it may not be needed. It would have been
a good and up-to-date policy when The
Bulletin propounded it, but circumstances
have altered since then. It is beginning to
dawn on the country citizens that it is not
exactly, advantageous— to them—to export
everything and import everything through
Melbourne. They see that if exports found
their way to their natural outlet —the near-
est port—considerable saving would be
effected, as well as time, which in this case
is money. Also, in those days the big ships
had not yet arrived. The 12,000-ton mer-
chantman can just scrape through Port
Phillip Pleads and up the long channel to
Melbourne; the 20.000 or 30,000 tonner of
the future Will have to pass by. The Harbor
Trust is dimly aware of this, and proposes
to deepen the entrance and channel —in 15
years—and. judging by the way the big ships
progress, that channel will be out of date
and useless, except for the smaller inter-
state boats, long before then. There is here
a fine chance for the McLEOD-led Vic. Par-
liamentary Country party to justify its
existence and nut in practice its alleged “de-
centralisation” policy. Decentralisation
doesn’t wholly consist in persuading the
State to build grain elevators along existing
lines in the backblocks. or in getting rail-
way repair shops erected at inland stations.
A truer conception of that policy comprises
the construction of railway lines —laid out
on the plan of a tree and its branches—from
every feasible port directly inland, and tap-
ping the country behind it. Of course this
wouldn’t suit Melbourne, but then the
Country party has never been accused of
considering Melbourne, which is not an ade-
quate port for a State, and looks as if it
never would be. Wherefore, the Watt-
Weary Government is advised to ponder
thoughtfully over the Harbor Trust’s scheme.
There is no need to urge it to go slow. Un-
seemlv and leaping haste never has been
one of its faults.
A few words concerning a rise in the price
of bread: —
Another “honorable understanding” has just struck
Melbourne, and a number of the inhabitants are
entering protests and passing resolutions—deeds
which are about as effective as a popgun aimed at a
mausoleum. The trouble concerns the staff of life,
the common, everyday 41b. loaf, for which the
Master Bakers’ Association has decided to charge
7d., as against the 6d. to 6ld. previously charged.
So far, the sole reason given for the increase is the
recent rise in bakers’ wages—-4s. per week of 48
hours. Against this, there are several facts as to
which the Master Bakers haven’t offered any explana-
tion. (1) The average journeyman baker will do
from 18 to 20 bags of flour per week ; as each bag
yields 60 loaves, the rise in price—Id. per loaf —
gives the master baker an increased revenue of
£2 10s. less the employee’s 4s. — £2 65., (2) in
’O9, when the M.B.A price for bread was sld.
to 61d., the Flour Millers’ Association quotation for
flour was £ll 10s. per 20001 b.; to-day it is only
£8 15s. ; (3) certain large bakers who are not of
the M.8.A., especially in closely settled suburbs like
Brunswick and Collingwood, have hitherto charged
no more than 6d., delivered, and have announced
their intention to continue selling at that figure; (4)
when, in ’O9, the relatively high price of flour may
have justified an increase, the huge bakery of the
Civil Service Co-operative Stores at Richmond (Yic.)
was, in a measure, fighting the private bakers, and
would have wiped some of them out had they raised
their prices—to-day the M.B.A. and the C. S. Stores
are working together in a sweetly reasonable frame
of mind that dimly suggests a “horrible under-
standing” ; (5) some of the large city restaurants
and some of the hospitals are still getting bread for
4ld. from associated bakers, while a Government
Gazette, issued since the rise to 7d., contains a
notice accepting a tender for supplying bread to gaols,
lunatic homes, etc., at 4d. per 41b. loaf. Now, if the
bread manufacturer makes Id. per loaf on this con-
tract a paltry 14 per cent.—it follows that he is
netting 100 per cent, on what he sells to the public,
and 100 per cent, is a shade over the odds; (6) not
only is flour 10s. per 20001 b. cheaper than this time
last year, but horse-feed is fully 30 per cent, cheaper,
the decrease far more than counterbalancing the rise
in bakers’ wages. The Associated Master Bakers have,
as stated, maintained a thick silence about these
points; they have merely told the consumer that he
will have to pay 7d. for the future, and, except for
the grumbling, that settles it.
Unless it changes its mind at the last
moment the N.S.W. Ministry is going to
hang a man —one Wright, who did an
obviously-premeditated murder. Yet it has
reprieved most murderers up to date, and
some of them also did the corpse that was
obviously premeditated, and some of them
were much more horrible in their methods
than Wright. Then the fact remains that
Wright was once in a lunatic asylum,
which raises a certain doubt about his men-
tal responsibility, while some of the re-
prieved slayers had never been in an asylum.
It the Government, which commenced its
career by saving Phillips, the most
unpleasant homicide of them all, hangs a
person like Wkight it can’t escape the sus-
picion that it saved Phillips, not on the
merits of his case, but by way of throwing
mud at its predecessors. Of course it may
be that the Government has simply decided
that its methods have been all wrong, and
has resolved, in a general way, to turn over
a new leaf. But if that is so it would be
better to start with the difficult leaf —not
the easy one. Any Minister —even if he
were a nigger Minister with an indigestion
and a wooden leg—could decide to let a man
hang. There is no worry or brain-fag about
that sort of thing.
“Comus” on Ma’s neglected education; —
N.S.W.'s new Minister of Education could put the
crowning touch on a well-spent Ministerial life by
abolishing the secret reports on teachers now sent in
by inspectors. As tilings are, an inspector can
put a very fair report in the Observation Book, and
send in a very different one to the Head Office —and
some of them do. This secret report the Head Office
jealously guards, and the teacher has no hope of find-
ing out where he was struck or why he was struck.
Some newly-made inspectors (and a few old ones, too)
sutler from bloated cranium, and expect, and I am
sorry to say, sometimes get, a certain amount of
adulation from teachers. If this kind of inspector
does not receive some sort of hero-worship—well, it
is safe to assume the secret report will get in some
vigorous work. The syllabus was supposed to confer
the blessing of individuality on teachers ; but, if the
teacher dare show any individuality, contrary to the
fads or fancies of his inspector, the secret report
usually brings him a censure from the 1 Chief, or from
some clerk, who most likely attends to this kind of
business. No straight inspector should fear to put
an adverse report in the Observation Book, if the occa-
sion demands it, and that report, and that only,
should be sent to the Head Office. The teacher
affected would then see which brick had struck him.
The efficiency mark is another sore point with
teachers. We are led to believe there is no such
thing as seniority now; ability is the only thing
that counts. This is a fine, democratic principle;
but, then, the ability is gauged by the “efficiency
mark,” and Heaven knows how that was ob-
tained. Most likely another clerk attends to this
business. In the days when this system was intro-
duced —when fads in plasticene, etc., were boom-
ing, and solid education at a discount—an in-
spector would recommend extra marks for, say, a
plasticene kid milking a plasticene cow. Well, since
then, the plasticene cow has been shot, but the marks
she got for the teacher still remain, and give that
teacher a pull over his senior in classification and
length of service.
Again, in the Department’s mad haste to staff its
High Schools with graduate teachers, it appointed
boys and girls, comparatively speaking, fresh from
the University. The academic qualification of these
teachers is not in question, but they can have , little
teaching experience, as most of their time must
necessarily have been spent in study. Lads of 15. 16
or 17 have thus been placed in the classes of girl-
teachers but a few years older. There is the danger
of effeminacy, and what is most essential, there can
be no character-formation by teachers whose age pre-
vents them from having experience and a knowledge
of men and the world. A high standard of education
among teachers is most desirable: but we are sacri-
ficing too much in Oui worship of an academic degree.
During the first half of 1913 New South
Wales, the maddest State in the Common-
wealth, bought from oversea countries goods
to the value of £14,905.466. and sold goods
to the value of £12,300,466. As N.S.W.
requires to export about £2,500,000 worth
per half-year more than it imports, in order
to cover "the interest on its numerous foreign
debts, this means that it went about
£5,000,000 to the bad inside six months.
Now it is certain that a State with a mere
handful of population can’t go to the Devil
at) the rate of £10,000,000 a year without
presently arriving at the Devil. The State
got to the Devil in 1893, and the poverty, the
starvation, the unemployed processions, the
array of homeless folk who “slept out,” the
pathetic readiness of strong men to work
for 3s. a day, the beggary, the soup-kitchens,
the Government relief works, the array of
tramps along the country roads, the enlpty
houses, the deserted shops, and all the other
elements of depression were terrible to see.
Before 1893, as before 1913, N.S.W. got into
a fatuous habit of relying on foreign loans,
on public works, on insufficiency of local
toil, and on tremendous imports of cheap-
labor goods brought in under an ineffective
tariff. Whether the devil that N.S.W. is
going to in 1913 is a bigger devil than the
one it went to in 1893, no man can tell.
There is now an Australian Parliament
which, being strongly Protectionist, may in-
terfere at the right moment. But N.S.W. is
taking exactly the same road to the Devil
that it did- 20 years ago. ■

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Wednesday, 10.50 p.m.
Underwriting arrangements are progressing for the
issue of £1,500,000 worth of New South Wales 4
per cent, stock at 97J.
The original amount was £2,000,000, but this
has been reduced in deference to a strong opinion
in financial circles that loans should be reduced to
the lowest possible figure in present market circum-
stances. —Cable in daily paper.
Summer days in London—the sun is beating
down,
Gilding every building in that dingy town —
Specially in Lombard-street, where the Noses
throng,
“Peezness” in Lombard-street is going pretty
strong!
The Three Brass Balls
Hang from the walls —-•
Telling us of Interest and Dividends and
Calls,
Drafts and lOU’s,
For the world to choose,
Underneath the aegis of the Three Brass
Balls!
Grim and grey and grimy Cohen’s temples
stand ;
In and out their portals goes a motley band ;
Russ and Jap and Yankee; Spaniard, Dago,
Swede—
Hurried are their footsteps, urgent is their
need !
The Great God Cash
Urges them to dash.
Wrestle, swear and blither where his mill
goes Crash!
In and out they go,
While Sol and Ike and Joe,
Serve the green-baize altars of the Great
God Cash!
All (he earth is racing round that mighty
Mill,
Offering as sacrifice bond, P.N. and bill,
Pawning half the Future, mortgaging the
kids,
Blowing all Posterity to “keep in” with the
Yids!
The glad cry— Loans!
In divers tones,
Agitates the welkin and penetrates the
stones;
And Cohen lends,
To foes and friends,
United (for the moment) in the glad cry—
Loans!
See! a tattered female takes her beery stand,
With the loudest pawners—lOU in hand ;
And she waves a bottle drunkenly on high,
While the loan-craze glistens in her bulging
eye!
’Tis New South Wales!
She girds and rails —
“Jus’— (hie) —’nother millionsh ! Dead
loansh tell no tales!
Wot? Yer won’t lend me?
( Well, y’ll ’ave to —See!....
’Nother drink for Gorsake!” —says New
South Wales.
Angry grow the acolytes, Sol and Ike and
Joe,
But they cannot quiet her, and she will not
.. . g°;
Vainly they remind her she has had enough,
For that tatteyed female only answers —
“Stuff!”
Upon their mat
She lies down flat, '
Just a mass of incoherent tears and beer and
fat!
Begging with loud moans
For a few more loans.
And she cuts a splendid figure—loan-drunk
Ma on Cohen’s mat!
N.s.w.
O. C. CABOT.
