A Few Words about Ex-Postmaster-
General Frazer.
The Bulletin has received a copy of the
Boulder (W.A.) Evening Star, containing
a report of a speech by ex-Postmaster-
General Frazer. This item was an inter-
esting feature of the report:—
In responding to the vote of thanks accorded to
him on the motion of Messrs. Levetus and Miller,
Mr. I razer said that had the Fisher Government
been returned to power he would probably have been
still their Postmaster-General, despite a statement
in The Bulletin as to his probable retirement.
Some time back he turned down an offer made to the
Government by the Australasian Wireless Syndicate,
which was willing to sell its patent rights for
£45,000. Mr. William Macleod was a member
of that syndicate.
A Voice: Is he any relation to George?
Mr. Frazer: No relation. He was the managing
director of The Bulletin, and since the offer he
referred to, The Bulletin writers could see no
good in Frazer’s Administration.
The Bulletin, which took Mr. Frazer
for a gentleman, is sorry to see him descend
to this sort of thing. The cheapest attitude
a politician can take up is expressed in the
words: “I am such a perfect administrator
that no one can possibly object to me or
criticise me except from motives of personal
corruption.” An especially vile Tory poli-
tician of the larrikin variety used to circu-
late the lie (perhaps he still does so)
that The Bulletin owned a great estate
on the Snowy River, and therefore advo-
cated a Federal capital at Dalgety. Another
had a theory that it owned large properties
about Alice Springs, and therefore supported
the direct transcontinental railway. Pos-
sibly the Devil fancies that the only reason
why the angels can object to him is that
they are envious of his tail. It is a pity
to see Frazer joining in this degradation.
The mahi'ging director of The Bulletin
was not before aware that Mr. Frazer was
responsible for the turning-down of a pro-
position in connection with the Australasian
Wireless Co. and the Marconi Co. He had
accepted Mr. Frazer’s oft-repeated state-
ment, made to the managing director and
others, that he (Frazer) favored the ac-
ceptance of the offer, but was overruled by
his cantankerous colleagues— (Fisher and
others). And the editor is in a position to
say that he never heard of the negotiation
until the ex-P.M.G. began to hold forth
about it. His only objection to the ex-
P.M.G. was based on the fact that, no less
than three times in quick succession, the
Melbourne theatrical notices arrived 24
hours late, and what Melbourne theatrical
notices appeared had to be telegraphed at
the last moment in response to urgent ap-
peals. And the secretary’s only grievance
is that, for several weeks running, he
couldn’t get a sufficient supply of
stamps at Sydney G.P.O. because Frazer’s
department had run dry. However,
Frazer will do a service if he will
show the date of the rejection he
alludes to, and prove by extracts that The
Bulletin changed sides in Federal politics
at that date, or at any other date. Its
hostile comments on the Federal Labor atti-
tude re the tariff, re the Northern Territory
railway route, re the Fisher Government’s
refusal to. consider the mono-rail proposition
(nobody in this office owns any shares in
the mono-rail), re the Federal capital, and
especially the Fisher Ministry’s refusal to
submit the selection of the capital site to a
referendum (this paper doesn’t know
whether Mr. Frazer owns any land at
Yassberra, but it possesses none), re the
lack of any really strenuous effort to nush
on the Federal note circulation (The
Bulletin staff has no more interest in the
Commonwealth Bank than Mr. Frazer has)
—all these things date pretty far back. It
is really up to Mr. Frazer, the gentleman
who apparently thinks that no paper could
criticise, even in the mildest fashion, such
a genius as himself unless one of its share-
holders was filled with personal animosity,
to reconsider his attitude.
“Galleryite” supplies this
Anyone who knew anything of the personalities in
the Reps, would have been glad to bet heavily that
as soon as Willie Kelly got a chance he would start
nosing out non-existent cases of corruption. Cook
should have made him Assistant-Minister for Mare’s
Nests. The N. S. Wales politician would then have
been entirely happy, in the way a person always is
who has discovered his true vocation. For years
Kelly has been seeing wickedness everywhere. A
vast panorama of crime rushes tirelessly past his
scandalised eyes. The figures on this vast canvas
are invariably Laborites. With a shamelessness that
is mad;* in its cynical audacity, these representa-
tives of\ a deluded democracy openly steal, bribe,
plot, boodle, breed wild cats, float bogus companies,
pilfer, burgle, rob, buccaneer, bolt with each other’s
wives, rifle collection-plates, betray innocent females,
tell untruths, laugh in church, insult pure-minded
barmaids, cheat the tram and railway commissioners,
drink, fracture the Sabbath, and in other respects
disclose the lowliness of their origin and habits. The
horrified Willie is now in a position to verify his
worst suspicions. Already he has been instrumental
in discovering that, during last Federal elections,
three people in every thousand voted irregularly—
though how many of the three acted deliberately and
how many were Laborites are questions that have
yet to be decided. He has also sheeted home the
damning fact that 40 acres of the best-grassed land
around Adelaide were let by a W.A. Laborite to the
Fisher Government ("together with a large house) at
an average rental of £lO per acre, for agistment
and other nurposes. Lord alone knows what Kelly
will unearth next. Meanwhile the rush of euiltv
Laborites to foreign parts remains temporarily in
abeyance.
SIXTEEN DEATHS IN TWO WEEKS.
“When a baby my little girl had diarrhea, and
as 16 children had died from it in this locality in
two weeks I was greatly alarmed,” says Mrs. Alice
Williams, Cressy (Tas.). “The doctor could do ab-
solutely nothing for her, but as. I am a great be-
liever in all Chamberlain’s Remedies I gave her
Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy with mar-
vellous results, for she was soon quite well again.”*

On Saturday Maoril.ander Alfred Hill was
At Home at Sydney Town Hall. In the
atternoon the Symphony Orchestra pro-
duced his JVl&ori liha,psody M j in the even-
ing, at the Butt farewell concert, Kennerley
sa “S Ms vigorous and tuneful
YVaiata Poi Song.” Hill conducted his
own Khapsody; but the orchestra did not
seem to thoroughly warm to it. Yet there
is true music as well as vivid portraiture
.^ e big thing was Tchaikowsky’s
I athetique Symphony, last conducted
here by Bath, the young man of the Quin-
lan Operatics, who is remembered because
he carried the heavy composition through
W l ,°ut. a _ glance at the music. On the
whole it is noisy—which suited present-
conductor Bradley, who loves noise. The
“Pathetique” was a distinct success. The
vocal soloist was Miss Bertha Dickens,
whose little voice was much too small for
the hall and the occasion; and to make
matters worse, what there was of it was
poorly produced. The farewell Butt pro-
gramme was mixed and mostly popular. The
magnificent lady, who stood behind a second
palisade of flowers before the evening was
through, charmed most in the “Fairy
Pipers,” the big “Creation’s Hymn” and a
setting of the Breton air of an “Angelas.”
Kennerley Rurnford did the usual sound
work, and at the piano Walter Murdoch’s
added number, a Chopin “Nocturne,” was
easily his most attractive. As a finale the
Butt-Rumford pair duetted, of course, in the
banal “Keys of Heaven.” Apparently they
have the fixed idea that audiences like the
stupid little thing. It would be a mercy if
they would get rid of it.
** * *
At Sydney Palace, William Anderson’s
hired party of U.S. Americans, who com-
menced business as “The Grafters,” and
with the proceeds became “The Speculators,”
will cease to songandance to-morrow night.
On Saturday, Bert Bailey and co. wili
start in to tear the city from some of its sur-
plus cash with the aid of an Australian
drama called “The Native Born.” Nothing
is known locally about the piece except
that it was written by Albert Edmunds,
who received all the congratulations and a
generous helping of profits when “The
Squatter’s Daughter” and “The Man from
Outback” were produced. If “The Native
Born” is in the same class (as is claimed
for it) Albert Ed. should be about due to
hire a bank for his nersonal convenience.
** * *
There is a heavy shower of tears every
evening, at Sydney Royal, when Butterfly
Madge Titheradge collapses pathetically in
the witness-box, after Lewis Waller’s cross-
examination. The average fall each evening
is 251 points. The official forecast for the
night is as follows : —Situation cloudy and
squally, with much rain; high domestic
winds, rough seas, but gradually becoming
finer and drier towards midnight. Some
haze and fog, but no frost.
* sfc *
Moving pictures of wild animal life are
to boom in Sydney for a time, and West's
(the Olympia) is in early with a long film
entitled “Wamba, the Child of the Jungle ”
The production is in the form of a drama,
and as a novelty it can be recommended to
the utterly blase and the entirely surfeited.
Also it is good for the person burdened witli
a heavy dinner, to the individual who carries
too much of himself in front of him, and to
the elderly citizen who sleeps in his talk
and trumpets heavily in the dead waste and
hub of the night. All these people require
wakening. This play is said to be a
wakener.
sk * * *
The most interesting remark this paper
has to make about the National Amphi-
theatre this week is that manager Ben
Fuller nas decided to revive the amateur
trial nights. It’s a long time since “the
bird” shrieked in Sydney, and its joy at
being uncaged once more will be done proud
to-morrow ni£ht and every Friday there-
after.
** * *
The gap caused in the large, warm heart
of Sydney by the departure of Hugh Buckler
from the Little Theatre has been admirably
filled by “The Punchinellos,” a gang of
entertainers who really do entertain. These
clever people have made a sufficient reputa-
tion in other States. Dressed as pierrots.
and not, fortunately, as “Punchinellos.”
against an artistic arrangement of black
curtains, the company presented on Satur-
day evening a smooth, refined and humorous
programme of solos and concerted work, and
evinced the possession of much musical and
character talent. The lighting effects and
the concerted work were thoroughly artistic ;
and few of the many items could be nossiblv
deemed dull. In the four “punehinelloesses”
the company possesses a quartet of beautiful
voices and much vivacity and versatility.
A farce, burlesquing melodrama, might be
commended to certain professional comedians
who introduce a similar burlesque in a
Sydney musical comedy. Any professional
comedian’s burlesque of melodrama has now
become as conventional as melodrama. But
in the Punchinellos’ farce there was quaint
characterisation, and genuine, not stage,
humor. The Punchinellos are commended
to those who desire refinement, real music,
and humor without dullness.
* :js :1:
The spring show of the newest feminine
modes at Sydney Criterion, under the fan-
tastic title of “The Dancing Mistress,” is
being well attended by fashion-lovers. The
dresses and hats are admirably shown, to
the accompaniment of pretty, innocuous
music; and the “mannequins” are talented
and good to look upon. The room in whicii
the costumes are shown has been trans-
formed to look, first, like the interior of a
girls’ finishing school—in which a number
of costumes for Misses are attractively ex-
hibited. Then it appears as a Swiss chalet,
in order to display an assortment of walking
dresses suitable for Kosciusko, in comparison
with which costumes the Bulgarian atroci-
ties are mild and anaemic. Finally, it is a
ball-room, where the latest semi-transparent
evening dress effects are admirably dis-
played. The chief shop-walker, Mr. Jack
Cannot, works hard; and the manageress,
Miss Grace Palotta, is a spring show in
herself.
* * * *
Although Miss Nellie Fergusson, alius
Jess Walton, has had a fortnight to learn
the error of her ways, she has made no pro-
gress, and the management, after carefully
taking the box-office’s temperature, has given
her another week. After that she will
probably be given up as hopeless.
Till then the Adelphi Theatre, Sydney, will
be the business address of “Her Road to
Ruin.”
** * *
Sydney’s Crystal Palace found Dr. Maw-
son such a draw as a cinematograph actor
that his season was prolonged until the
middle of this week ; and as the expedition
is to share the proceeds, it should get a fair-
sized cheque. Besides this South Pole film,
another of local interest shown this week
was the Australian Steeplechase, run in
Melbourne on Saturday. To date the same
horse has won at each performance. He is
the most consistent animal at present on the
turf.
** * *
By the end of this week, the military
film, “Australia Calls,” and the Kinetophone
will be but pleasant memories. The big hole
left in the Sydnev Lyceum programme will
be filled by a picture of millionaire Paul J.
Rainey’s, big-game hunt in Central Africa,
which will take the whole evening to unwind.

PALACE.
The Jewish Blossom (Bert Le Blanc) and
the Dutch Bloom (drank Vack) discover a
tame property crocodile.
Merian s dogs at Sydney Tivoli this week
provide something out of the ordinary in the
way of animal turns. There are 25 hounds,
who manage without any visible human
assistance; and though it would be more
acceptable with a few changes in the cast,
it is as good an animal act as the Tiv. has
had since Drunken Dan gave a fillip to these
four-legged performers. The only other new
item is provided by Frank Fox, an English
comedian who owns rather a good voice
The Primrose Four, the De Bruns, the Six
Hoboes and the Bussian Golden Troupe are
other leading lights.

The Genee season at Adelaide Royal fol-
lowed a famous precedent, and began with
the second performance. The music was
left behind in Melbourne, and after
agonised afternoon, managers Hazlitt and
Myers had to chase the early-comers off the
gallery steps, and head off the booked-seat
people as they arrived. The season has
since been highly successful. Setting aside
the “butterfly” and “thistledown” ecstasies
of the dailies, Gen£e appears to this critic
to be a superb toe-dancer of the old Italian
school one had imagined obsolete, and which
is distinctly less graceful than modern skirt-
dancing. But it is her acting that is even
more striking. Her facial expression i s
remarkable, and she entirely dominates

The Quinlan Co. has returned to Mel-
bourne for a two-months’ camnaign, and
the strenuous musical life begins at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday. It is understood
thus far that 14 operas will be produced in
succession without a pause for first-nighters.
Quinlan has undertaken to accomplish this
tremendous feat, and season-ticket buyers
are prepared to bear the strain. A gala
performance of Wagner’s “The Meister-
singers ’ will set the ball rolling. On Mon-
day comes "Rigoletto.” introducing Felice
B.vne.as the hunchback’s daughter. “Rhine-
gold, the first opera of the Ring series, is on
luesday ; ‘Tales of Hoffmann” on Wednes-
j Tosca” on Thursday; “Samson
on Friday; and the inevitable
I aust on Saturday. Which is as far as
the advt. has gone at time of publishing,
hour familiar Quinlan names—Jeanne
Brola, Mabel Dennis. Robert Parker and
Spencer Thomas—figure on the first bill.
Gladys Ancrum and W. J. Samuell are in
* Rigoletto” cast. Edna Thornton and
Charles Magrath will be received with the
usual tokens of remembrance later in the
first week. Two principal tenors and the
leading .\\ agnerian lady are new to Aus-
tralia, like several other members of the co.
Chorus, orchestra and Quinlan forces in gen-
eral number 175 souls, or thereabouts, and
the operas—two dozen altogether—will be
sung in the language of the country which
is about to give them an enthusiastic hear-
ing. 10-night (Thursday) the final per-
formance of “Bella Donna” happens, and to-
morrow night the theatre will be prepared for
the impending storm.

All the Melbourne theatres are chang-
ing the bill next Saturday. Meanwhile
“Queen of the Redskins” is doing the final
laps of a six nights’ sprint at the Princess’s
This lamentable drama was produced in the
same spot a year ago, but it is still without
a rival in its particular line of foolishness
The villain takes the heroine from her oehred
and feathered relatives, and plants her as his
lawful wife in the home of his Noo Yark
uncle, where he (the villain) is sharing a
guilty secret with his wicked aunt. He
pretends to sell his virtuous Wahneetah to
an oil-king, or other millionaire, and is also
bent on poisoning his mixed piccaninny. For-
tunately, Broncho Bill, the adopted son of
the Redskins, rescues the lady from her
husband’s uncle’s mansion, and carries her
back to the forest glades. The insane story
must have been borrowed from a picture
drama, and fitted with dialogue, which, by
the way, is not too bad. Mann’s scenery for
the redskin section of the entertainment is
even better than the talk, and a million
times too good for the plot. George Cross
again wears the weird broncho pants of Bill
the deliverer, whilst the other members of
the co. are mostly different from the people
who sinned and suffered aforetime. Another
Marlowian revival, for six nights only, is
due on Saturday next, and there will be yet
one more hasty attraction before Allen Doone
comes along.
** * *
The wet season has set in at West’s Mel-
bourne Palace. “East Lynne” has turned
un. seemingly more agonising than ever. In
this version, which is in five acts and 117
scenes, a nipper named Pippin perishes at
great length as Little Willie, and the other
characters are apparently taken by English
mummers who play their parts as to the
drammer born. The rest of the bill on Sat-
urday was made up of Path<§ Gazettes and a
comedy called “The Fortune,” which is the
adapted yarn of old Christopher Sly, bio-
graphed by kind permission of Mr. W. 'Shaks-
peare.
** * *
Dad and Dave and the rest of the Rudd
family are to pass from Melbourne King’s
to-night. Maggie Moore and co. come along
on Saturday for a short season. “Meg the
Castaway” being the immediate business in
hand.
* * * *
To-night (Thursday) also is the last of
“Within the Law” at Melbourne Royal. The
Wallingford co. returns on Saturday with
“The Fortune Hunter” for the first Mel-
bourne time, and as the comedy professes
to be four .acts of joyful instruction in how
to make a million dollars, the city antici-
pates the event with sordid interest. Now,
in “Within the Law,” the burglars are con-
tent to go burgling after a swag which is
alleged to be worth no more than half a
million dollars.
* * * *
At Melbourne Bijou, where Carrie Moore
was lately quartered, the company from the
Little Theatre in Sydney will make a first
bid for enlightened support next Saturday
(9th). “The Man on the Box” is the open-
ing play, and the co. will have Hugh Buckler
and Miss Paget in foremost parts.
** * *
Nordica’s fourth Melbourne concert falls
due to-night (Thursday) at the Auditorium.
Time has dealt mercifully enough with Nor-
dica and her sweet voice, and the stamp o*'
the artist is on all she does. With Paul
Dufault as the tenor and Franklin Holding
violin soloist the concerts are particu-
larly interesting. Their one fault is the
tariff of admission, beginning _ with half a
guinea and ending at five “bob.”
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House Flynn and
McLaughlin are divulging the secrets of
“Texas Tommy” and other dances wherein
the artists are thrown much together. Other
recent arrivals include Martynne, the elec-
trical surprise packet, and Lola Krasavina,
the melodious Muscovite who drops _ from
opera to ragtime without loss of prestige as
a popular warbler. The Kebbles continue
to get the loud hand from the multitude for
their song and dance act, and Lillie Langtry
is doing a new song with the full support of
a choir in the twelvepenny seats. Lucy
Gillet, Cummin and Seaham, and De Mario
and Lady are booked for other halls, and on
Saturday there will be -a fresh shower of
stars to fill their places.

Yet another Melbourne art show :
Melbourne harbors a versatile artist in Miss J.
Cumbrae Stewart, who has crowded 165 pictures in t°
the upper hall of the Athenseum. The young lady
works cleverly in pastel, water-color and oil, and
nearly every effort is a graceful achievement. Her
pastellised portraits are things to look at more than
once; and her oils are entitled to even more bouquets.
“The Billabong,” in this latter section, is a piece
of crowded trees and deftly-painted water that would
be a credit even to the late John Ford Paterson.
“Like Sunshine She Lingers” is a thing of sweetness
depicting a pale, pre-Raphaelitish female sniffing
rose as if performing a rite. The lady seems
affectedly “too-too” in style, but her pose is uncom-
monly pleasing. Among the other offerings the most
taking is a fire screen, the feature whereof is a pan
displaying three ladies in a sort of dreamy landscap
of black opal and gold. All that the artist dema
for this charming thing is 14 guineas for w
modesty she is hereby sentenced to six months
a commercial college.

AT POVERTY POINT. poverty point.
Sparrow”: You can’t beat a mining town for
squelching insectarian follies. At the Vic. coal-
mining hamlet of Wonthaggi the Orangemen thought
, was U P to them to have a banquet—and they
had it. Also they had a Scotch piper who insisted
upon playing The Wearin’ of the Green.” Nobody
broke anything; in fact, what ought to have made
the congregation foam went down as the best joke
of the evening.
Genee is the diamond danseuse—hard, white, glit-
tering and polished to perfection. Halina Schmolz
is a rosy tourmaline, less rare and less artistically
cut, with a comparatively soft surface, but more
sensuously beautiful.
Gregan McMahon has Galsworthv’s “Strife” in re-
r his next Melb - Repertory Theatre season.
Strife means a trade strike, and the play, whilst
breathing the labor spirit which justifies the strike,
preaches the moral that industrial struggles are likely
to make matters worse.
Obituary notices of the late Erskine Scott have
omitted to give him credit for fighting the battle of
the Sunday picture showmen in Melbourne. Years
ago Scott was prosecuted for running pictures on
Sunday on the silver-coin-collection-at-the-door sys-
tem, and the unpaid fines and costs imposed upon
him amounted to hundreds or thousands of pounds
bv the time he eventually got the decisions over-
ruled. Also, he deserved well of his contemporaries
by his ingenious method of putting shame into the
hearts of them who gave not even a “tray”-bit to
the collection. Admission was free to such as re-
fused to fork out any silver coin at all, but they
were always passed into a particular section of the
hall set apart for “rotters.” One Sunday a sheet
was fixed over the free section, and during an inter-
val in the show a lantern slide appeared on the sheet
stating that “the people seated beneath this notice
have contributed nothing to the cost of the enter-
tainment,” or words to that effect. After which the
abashed deadheads were converted into financial mem-
bers of the society.

The principal tenor of the Quinlan Opera Co. this
time is an Englishman whose name is Maurice
D’Oisly on the bill. The principal tenor last Quinlan
time was also an Englishman, but his name did not
disguise him. It was just Coates, as we remember.
John Coates. And he looked it. The new man, per-
haps, will look D’Oisly.
Told, in a new volume of musical reminiscences,
that Arditi, opera conductor in London and com-
poser of the song “II Bacio,” was once advised,
being near Stratford-on-Avon, that he ought cer-
tainly to visit the birthplace of Shakspeare. “I
not know him,” said he. “What, you don’t know
the man who wrote ‘Romeo and Juliet,’ ‘Otello,’
‘The Merry Wives of Windsor’? You know the
operas, anyway.” “Ah,” said Arditi, with all the
condescension of tune-spinner to word-spinner, “you
mean ze librettist.”
“J.F.H.” (from London) : We are threatened with
an avalanche of “religious” plays. This is a conse-
quence of the permission given by the Lord Cham-
berlain to Beerbohm Tree to produce “Joseph and
His Brethren” —a complete reversal of the hitherto
established rule and practice. Theatrical managers
are exceedingly gregarious; so the Lord Chamber-
lain has been "rushed with manuscripts. The Times
rejoices that “the ban upon the religious drama has
at last been lifted,” but sounds a note of caution:
“No doubt good use will be made of the liberty,
though managers will be wise to move warily at
first.”

TOM AIKEN , the cham-
pion Scottish billiardist,
now Playing in Melbourne.
Foe ’: Isn’t it wonderful how the impatient city
patiently endures the blind man’s barrel organ, and
refrains from kicking the blind man’s occasional
able-bodied A.D.O. or private secretary, who steers
him round the streets and collects the taxes, or
blackmail ? In Melbourne, years ago, there used to
be a blind man whose impatient and bustling
A.D.C. would hardly give him time to drop the
handle, after churning out a brief selection, before
hustling him along to the next stand. The pair
used to get along at a sort of scuttling trot, the
A.D.C. towing the sightless performer by the slack
of the sleeve. I often wondered why they travelled
with such velocity. Some days I was sure that
they considered their disgraceful uproar too valuable
to squander, and merely played sufficient to announce
their arrival. Other days I felt convinced that they
were ashamed of it, and just loathed to take the
money. Maybe they had a big round of victims or
patients, and so were compelled to hurry as if en-
gaged in some useful and urgent work. Whatever
their reason was, that A.D.C. was a devil to get
along. He looked capable and spry, and I once
heard a business man say he wished his employees
would travel at something like the same speed. Is
the job of managing guide to a light-footed blind
musician a profitable one? (Barrel-organising is a
humble form of Art, and I feel a certain delicacy in
introducing the subject. But I would point out to
any offended Poverty Pointer who yearns to kick
me out of the column that there was no monkey on
this organ. I am ready to agree with anyone that
the monkey is not Art. If anything, he is Litera-
ture. )

The record run (9hrs. lOmin.), made
by the Union Steam Ship Co.’s new turbine
steamer Wahine, between Lyttelton and
Wellington, 175 miles, on Sunday, August
10, illustrates the remarkable improvements
in coastal travelling in Maoriland within
comparatively few years. The “ferry” ser-
vice between Wellington and Lyttelton is
the main connecting link between the North
and South Island, and was inaugurated as
such in 1896, when the old Penguin, of 524
tons (which came to such a disastrous end
in Cook Strait a few years ago), was put
on to ply back and forward. The next big
step was the putting on of the then grey-
hound, Rotomahana, now the recipient of
frequent and hearty curses on the Mel-
bourne-Tasmania ferry. That was 16 years
ago, and it remained till 1907. The cross
ferry was the Mararoa, the tonnage of the
pair being 1117 and 2598 respectively.
Then, in 1907, entered the turbine Maori
(3399 tons), followed by the Wahine (4435
tons) this year. They are providing a short-
range service unsurpassed in the world.
A cable message mentioned last week
that G. Harry Hawker, an Australian, had
met the King and Queefi at the opening of
the Cowes regatta on a waterplane, and
performed various sensational manoeuvres.
Hawker has done extraordinarily well at
the flying game. Two years ago he was
squatter Ernie de Little’s chauffeur in the
western district of Victoria. At the time of
writing he is about the most capable flier in
the United Kingdom. He holds three British
height records, and last year he won the
Michelin Cup, and incidentally put up the
duration record for the United Kingdom.
Sydney Pickles is another Australian who
has made a name for himself at the same
risky job. Flying a Caudron monoplane—
generally esteemed to be the most perilous
of air-machines —he did the journey between
Hendon and Brooklands and back, the other
day, a matter of 42 miles, in 291 minutes.
Easily a record. Half a dozen other Aus-
tralians, in addition to the above, are fig-
urine prominently on the British flying
grounds, and Stephen Douglas, a second-year
medical student at Sydney University, has
just taken his “brevet” at Farman’s school
in France.
The absence of any fragment of a sense of
the ludicrous is apparently an indispensable
feature of the militant suffragette’s- make-
up. Consider the hymn which 40 of these
ladies recently chanted three times in St.
Paul’s Cathedral, London :
Save Emmeline Pankhurst,
Spare her, spare her,
Give her light, and set her free,
Save her, save her,
Hear us while we pray to Thee.
A mammoth lack of proportion is displayed
in the effort to make the bearer of such a
name as Pankhurst the heroine of a hymn.
There are certain names which should never
be what one might term lyricised. Sarah
Pony, for instance, Arethusa Grubb, Jane
Tubbs —and, particularly, Emmeline Pank-
hurst. Incidentally, the hymn itself is
awful, as most earnest endeavors are. It
isn’t even in the same street with that
(real or alleged) Mormon anthem of bygone
days:—
Let the chorus still be sung
Long live Brother Brigham Young,
And blessed be the Vale of Deseret-ret-ret;
Blessed be the Vale of Deseret.
Plainly, the Pankhurst cause ought to hire
a good bard. It would be worth its while
to offer a man like David McKee Wright
£lOOO a year, with a steady, five-years’ en-
gagement and a bank guarantee of the sol-
vency of the salary, on condition that he
turned out a brief Pankhurst hymn once a
week. Then the suffragettes wouldn’t be
afflicted with symptoms akin to the above,
as the patent medicine advertisment has it.
From the Oldest Inhabitant:—
Sydney Telegraph on Saturday published a picture
of the historic house known as The Rangers, at
Mosman. The place, which has Brierley’s painting
of “The Rattlesnake in a Storm” on one of the
walls, is on the market now, and there is a proposi-
tion to retain the premises as “The Northern Suburbs
Institute” for the public. The Telegraph says:
“Every room in this building, which is nearly a
century old, is teeming with historic interest. The
building was at one time the residence of a Governor,
and one room is pointed out as that in which the
young Princes slept when they visited Australia. In
the cellars are cells which date back to the convict
days.” As a matter of fact, the building was erected
in 1844, and by free labor. The builder
was Oswald Bloxsome, a squatter on the
Liverpool Ranges (the house, by the way, was orig-
inally known as The Ranges), and afterwards manager
of the Loan Co. and British Colonial Bank. The
Bloxsomes would not have any convict labor about
the place. No Governor of this State has ever lived
there, and if the “young Princes” (one now dead,
the other George V.) ever did visit the place, they
make no mention of the fact in their journals and
diaries. The alleged cells are merely wine cellars and
storerooms. Henry Stuart Russell, a Queensland pioneer,
pastoralist and explorer, purchased the house after
the Bloxsomes had done with it, and spent £lO,OOO
on it. Russell’s hand was so open that in 1866 he
left it; the moths had crept into his riches. Oswald
Brierley’s painting was done on the wall of Russell’s
dining-room.
From one who knew him :
S. F. (“Colonel”) Cody, who met the aviator’s
usual end last week, was originally a showman in
America. He ran a circus there, and when the weather
permitted he flew outside his tent a string of those
weird box kites which were invented by Hargreaves,
of Sydney. He was “discovered” by Colonel Capper,
R.E., commanding the British Balloon Corps. Capper
installed him at Laffan’s Plain to experiment with
man-lifting kites for the British army. He worked
there successfully for some years, though it was only
a year ago, after a hard-won victory in the courts,
that he succeeded in wresting from the War Office
the money he had been promised for his kite work.
While at Laffan’s Plain he evolved a cumbersome and
intricate biplane, and had the Wright Bros, not fore-
stalled him by their flights in America, he would
undoubtedly have been the first man to fly. This
machine was christened “the Cathedral,” and its
first attempts to fly, coupled with the old man’s child-
like optimism, called forth derisive comments from
the crowd, which was so soon to see its triumph
under his amazing guidance.
This machine he flew in the circuit of Britain in
July, 1911, when it finished fourth, being the only
biplane to complete the course. A year later, Cody
started, to the terror of all his friends, to build an
immense monoplane, to compete in the British War
Office trials. There was a general feeling of relief
when he hit a cow and smashed both the machine
and the animal to pieces. He proceeded then to
bring out his old “Cathedral,’.’ and, fitting it with an
enormous 120-h.p. engine, fairly bullocked through
all the tests, and won the first prize open to the
world, in competition with pilots one-half of his age.
It was his wonderful pluck more than the merits
of the machine which won this for him ; and the
ever-misguided War Office took over the ancient ruin,
which shortly after broke in the air and added
another unnecessary (Vagedy to the annals of the
British flying corps.
Cody’s methods of construction were peculiar, and
he was no mathematical designer. He would work
away at his bench and produce a strut or a stay, and
then tell one of his few assistants to “make another
like that for the other side.”
He was famous for flying close to the ground. I
remember seeing him arrive on the Hendon aero-
drome from Brooklands during the circuit of Britain,
just clearing the tree-tops, and I asked him if he
always ran it as close as that. “Yes,” he said, “I
feel safer low down. I reckon on Laffan’s Plain
there isn’t a tree-top left.”
He was a naturalised Englishman, but the title of
“Colonel” was never really accorded him till last
year, when George V. addressed him as “Colonel
Cody,” at Aldershot. Then everyone was willing to
concede his military rank.
He was a familiar sight in the Aero Club in
London. A large, loosely-built man, with a keen
eye and a mobile, humorous face, talking, talking
always about flying and his own dauntless self.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

Oh, grievous is thq actor’s life,
A never ending wear and tear!
With gravest cares his hours are rife,
And long the burden he must bear.
, He acts each night upon the stage;
He acts all day upon the street.
His hat, his hair, his equipage,
His lobks of joy, surprise and rage—
All-these must be designed to heat
Our interest, and so accent
The merciless advertisement.
The painter only paints at times;
the sculptor sculps in some retreat ;
The poet wrestles with his rhymes
In peace and quiet. But we meet
The actor everywhere, poor chap,
And always acting like the deuce!
He does it in his sleep, mayhap.
That’s why I never pay a rap
To see him play. Where is the use?
Our task, indeed, our problem grim,
Is how to get away from him.

PENFOLD'S
WINES
The two oldest houses in George-street.
and the last bit of picturesque old Sydney
in the locality, near the G.P.0., have just
been demolished. They dated back to the
Macquarie era, and were at one time the
property of Mrs. Mary Reibey, who lived
near-by in Reibey cottage, which later on
made way for Sands’s printing office. On
the death of Mrs. Reibey in 1855 the pro-
perty fell into the hands of “Bill” Long,
and from him passed to his daughter, wife
of Chief Justice James Martin. At one
time —1834 —the whole of the frontage be-
tween Hunter and King streets, barring the
post office, was owned by six persons:
Edward Riley, of Raby ; Prosper De Mestre,
an American merchant, who was naturalised
by special Act of Council; S. T. MacDonald,
Samuel Terry, Mrs. Mary Reibey and W.
H. Moore —the last-named the Crown
Solicitor, who in that year cut up and sold
his portion, the King-street end.
“Croson”: The electoral reform business
has begun. The new men are showing their
mettle. Willy Kelly has got to work. Last
week my wife, who has resided with me in
our present abode, dwelling or habitation for
several years, and still so resides, received
a notice from the returning officer of our
Federal Electoral Division (name completely
undecipherable) stating that one Wm.
Smith, an electoral registrar (apparently of
parts unknown) objected to her further con-
tinuance on the rolls as she “had left the
district.” The homeless person, Smith,
mentioned firmly that my wife may, within
20 days, orally or in writing, answer the
above-mentioned Smith’s objection. If she
doesn’t —well, there. My wife would like
to answer the objection of the returning
officer, preferably in writing, but the only
address supplied is “his office in Prahran.”
I’rahran is a big place, and, as above-men-
tioned, the R.O.’s writing is almost incred-
ibly rotten. To search through a large direc-
tory for a name which looks like a blot of ink
that has been trodden on, is too big a
contract. So I suppose the wretched wife
is more or less Doomed. Heaven help us
all; for Horse Sense in public places never
will, seemingly.
The sort of news never cabled to this
country is the story of the judicial joke in
India which was told to the House of Com-
mons last month. , Three natives of Oude
were tried for murder, in connection with
an “agrarian outrage,” and were acquitted.
Five months later they were re-arrested
and re-tried, on appeal. This time no wit-
nesses were heard, and the prisoners were
not even present at their own trial. The
Judicial Commissioners simply considered
the record of the previous trial, and found
the three men guilty. Two were sentenced
to death, and the other to penal servi-
tude for life. The accused appealed to the
Lieutenant-Governor, who refused to do
anything to prevent the law from “taking
its course.” Then they made an appeal to
the Governor-General. but the Lieutenant-
Governor refrained from forwarding it,
on the ground that it could not have
reached Simla until the day previous to the
date fixed for the appellants’ execution,
whereas the regulations require that the
Government of India shall have at least 48
hours’ notice in these little matters. So
the two acquitted-and-condemned men were
duly executed, and the Lieutenant-Governor
(Sir John Hewett) was promoted to the
Grand Cross of the Star of India. Such
stories throw brilliant sidelights on the un-
rest of India’s colored millions. The House
of Commons rose to the occasion by re-
roarkinsr “Oh, oh” when the 48 hours regu-
lation was mentioned, and it seems that “the
question of amending the rules will be taken
into consideration.” Natives in future will
not be convicted behind their backs of a
crime of which they were nreviousiv ac-
quitted in front of their faces, nor will they
be hanged without the option of an appeal
to the G.-G.

“Inchiquin”: Premier Peake (S.A.) is
publishing through the Register a diary of
his trip to the country he found so bitterly
Cold. He complains, in the last instalment,
of the sleeping arrangements of the French
trains. The compartment he was in held
six passengers—four men and two women.
Attached were two sleeping apartments,
with three berths in each. A satisfactory
arrangement by which each person had a
berth was, he says, made; but, very un-
advisedly, he omits to state exactly how
things were arranged.
QUITE FAMILIAR.
"Give's a match, chum!"
The recent case of a young fellow who
blew out Ins brains in Invercargill (M.L.) is
right up against the theory that every girl
wants to grab the other girl’s heart’s-desire.
The young man was conversing with Lottie
and Irene, when the question arose as to
which maiden should possess him. Lottie
said she loved him, whereupon the young
man declared for Irene; but Irene firmly
declined to take him. In effect, she told him
that Lottie would do very nicely for a wife.
Then, in his perversity, the young man killed
himself after making a feeble pretence of
meaning to kill the unobtainable she.^
The City Bank of Sydney celebrates its
jubilee this year. Business was commenced
in November, 1863, in premises on the north-
east corner of King and George streets,
known as the Golden Fleece
Corner. An inn (bearing
that sign was there then.
The corner belonged to the
late William Toogood. His
widow married the Rev. C.
C. Kemp, of Newtown, who
induced the lady to have no-
thing to do with an hotel
business. The City Bank
coming along, it got posses-
sion and held the premises
for 10 years. Then it re-
moved to a haunt in Pitt-
street. Burned out there in
the great fire of 1890, it
occupied temporary prem-
ises for a while until the
present bulding was erected.
Of the first board of direc-
tors Fred H. Dangar, now
resident in London, is the
sole survivor. The first
manager, J ohn Thomas
Ford, is still a resident of
Sydney. The Union Bank,
by the way, commenced busi-
noss in Sydney on the same Golden Fleece
corner a quarter of a century earlier.
Adelaide University has started out to
encourage poets, there being a local scarcity
of the article. The encouragement is directly
traceable to Miss Bundey, the tuneful daugh-
ter of the late Judge, who has endowed a
£lO prize to be given every year in memory
of her parents. The competition is restricted
to the Australian-born, of not more than six
years’ standing in the Adelaide University.
Unfortunately, the University has adopted
the old-fashioned conditions of Oxford and
Cambridge on such matters. The subject
is to be set and the length of the alleged
poem prescribed; that is, so many lines have
to be ground out on a theme that is not
spontaneous. Further, the prize will not be
given more tnan once to the same person;
wherefore, after a few years, the prize poem
will no doubt be issued with this warning
note: “We have two or three better poets,
but they are barred.” Also, it may perhaps
be justly added: “This one could do much
better work if he wrote on his own subject.”
“Scalpel”: Much rubbish is being talked
about the efficacy of “vaccination” against
plague as well as against small-pox. Plague
is certainly treated by inoculation, but not
with vaccine-lymph. The agent used is a
sterilised culture of the plague bacillus, which
either confers complete immunity for about
six months, or greatly diminishes the sever-
ity of an attack occurring within that period.
The “protected” person, in fact, remains all
the time in a faintlv pestiferous condition.
Present writer knows, for he has had four
interviews with “Haffkine’s Prophylactic
Fluid,” as the stuff is called, and, though he
didn’t get the plague, he got the blue-devils
pretty badly every time.

A DULL TRIP
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The trip of the P. and O. Company’s Macedonia,
which arrived to-day, was described as the dullest
on record. There were not as many as 100 passen-
gers on board at any time, and only 78 were landed
in Sydney. Not a' dance or a concert could be
arranged in the first-class. The inter-State traffic
hardly justified the vessel calling at the various
ports! Only 43 people were brought from the capi-
tals.- —Evening paper.
Each dreaming maid, with drooping lip,
Builds all her hopes upon the trip
She’s going to take some day with Ma,
With money earned by patient Pa,
Who does not seek from toil release.
He makes no moan,
If let alone
To toil and shed his hair in peace.
It is the dream of maids to go
Aboard the gleaming P. and 0.,
And travel cheek-by-jowl with those
Who look severely down the nose.
Tea-planters, rajahs, lords and earls
Infest the day-dreams of these girls,
And give them very “meaning” looks,
Exactly as they do in books.
For, after lonely years “Out There,”
It is the girl with wind-blown hair
Who gets the sunburnt man, you know,
Aboard the splendid P. and O.
He follows with adoring eyes,
And vainly of his passion tries
To speak. ’Tis useless quite! And she
Enjoys the poor chap’s agony.
He calls her “Little Girl.” His voice
Is “stifled” —but he has no choice,
For Strong Men always mumble so
Aboard the fiction P. and O.
He also “pales beneath his tan,”
And runs to fetch her book or fan,
Or see the time, or read the log.
She treats him like a poodle dog
That once a week is washed with soap,
And never gives him any hope.
For, be it understood, he feeds
On hope! That’s all the poor brute needs !
He watches her and fawns around
Like any humble, well-kicked hound,
And hardly dares to lift his eyes,
While in her chair she calmly lies,
Surrounded by a ducal herd
Who “hang upon her lightest word,”
And “vie”—that is the word! They “vie”
With one anoth&j .' With her eye
She maddens >...c, or breaks his heart,
And makes an-•'.her vow to start
For Africa, wl.< re lions spring
And suffer for this sort of thing.
It seems a shame to tell the truth
To ardent and expectant Youth ;
But England’s old nobility
Don't dwell exclusively at sea ;
And liners sometimes come to port
Without a duke of any sort.
Such worthless hulks ’tis vain to catch
In hopes that you will “make a match,”
Or queen it over heroes dark
Who act like dogs that wag and bark.
O Maiden with the pensive lip !
Expect not too much from the trip
You plan to take one day with Ma,
With lucre begged from toiling Pa.
On leaden feet some sea hours go,
Even aboard the P. and O. —
The galleys of Romance that steam
Across your Paris-hatted dream.
N.B.W.

Under Fusion rule a military order has
been made that no non-commissioned officer
or man can be placed on the “marriage estab-
lishment” unless he informs his commanding
officer of his intention to commit matrimony,
and afterwards flourishes his marriage lines
in that gentleman’s face. This order is
calculated to bring about again the condi-
tion of affairs which existed some years
back, at the time the notorious Webb' case
was ventilated in the House of Reps, by R.
A. Crouch. It was then complained that
it was more or less the custom at Queens-
ciiff tor a soldier to have to parade his
blushing donah for inspection by the com-
manding officah, and in at least one instance
that gentleman withheld his benediction on
the ground that the lady was a nonentity
or a vegetarian or something. As the result
of the Webb scandal, a section was inserted
in the Defence Act which read that “all
married men in the Permanent Forces who
have served for three years shall be en-
titled to be placed on the Marriage Estab-
lishment.” The need for the new order is
not half so apparent as its liability to abuse.

Evidently the gin cure for kidney trouble
can bh overdone. A Melbourne photographer
last |week was. suffering more than usual
from pains in the lower part of his
back,) He then consumed a whole bottleful
of schnapps, with the worst possible results.
The Coroner decided that the sufferer had

of alcohol.
“Sardonyx” : The cabalistic scrib-
ble of most medical men is re-
sponsible for many more dispensing
errors than ever come to the sur-
face. Everybody, of course, hears
of such a case as that cited by
“Carton” (B. 31/7/T3), but there
are numerous unexploited in-
stances of the • use of an
ingredient other than the one
prescribed. The chemist, in
fact, has often to depend upon his
knowledge of what the prescription-
item is likely or almost certain to
be, and, though of course a tea-
spoonful of opium-wine for a child
would at once pull up any com-
petent dispenser, less important
misreadings are far more fre-
quent than they would be if the
doctor—like the chemist himself—-
were obliged to write a decently
legible fist in the language he is
most accustomed to. The use of
Latin is a mere survival from the
time when people spoke of
the “art and mystery of medi-
cine”—a belated desire to preserve
some of the “mystery” that causes
mutton-broth tb be styled ovillum
jusculum, and renders “Continue
with the medicine” as “Perga in
usum medicinarum,” or more
esoterically still, “Perg. in us.
med.” The surgeon might just as
sensibly perform some mumbo-
jumbo tricks before getting to his
work. But the surgeon is not such a fool.
He has progressed: the physician, in a
general way, hr^n’t.

of the main planks in the Social Democrat
party’s platform is the repeal of the Com-
pulsory Military Defence Act. Thus, the
party is to be kept pure so that the nation
may be polluted.
The office of the Catiioltc Press (Syd-
ney) was burned out on Monday morning.
According to the usual courtesy of one pub-
lication to another the management of the
Watchman will print the Catholic Press
until the proprietary can make new arrange-
ments. This information is quite false. The
drunken ruffian who invented it has since
been discharged.
There was not much money in bushrang-
ing, seemingly. Jack Dermody, who
escaped from Ballarat Gaol, 40 years ago,
with his leader, “Captain Moonlight,” put
himself in the hands of the local police last
week and was taken to the Benevolent
Asylum. From bushranging to the Benevo-
lent! For the last 30 years Dermody has
wandered about the country, coming gradu-
ally to the conclusion that bushranging as
an industry is played out.
The Proverbs and Precepts of “Solomon
Malaprop” :
All women are the same kind of women except
those women who are different, and even they aren’t
very different.
There is as much resemblance in some people as
anybody. This is a deep saying and difficult of accep
tation.
There may be such a thing as immortality, but
nobody has lived for ever up to date. Therefore
there is no precedent for immortality. It is a
socialistic innovation.
Better is a dinner of herbs and beef therewith
than a brawling woman in a corner of the housetop.
If sinners entice thee with an infallible hairwash,
consent thou not. .
Some of the old superstitions are not so foolish as
they look. For instance, it is unlucky to owe <£l3
on a Friday. It is. also unlucky to fall down 13
stairs on a Friday.
An argument in favor of the State leasehold sys-
tem is that Judas Iscariot hanged himself on free-
hold property.
No man ever bit his own ear.
Nor did any man ever make a success of cutting
his own hair. He can’t get round fast enough. It
is this fact which keeps society 'together. The bard
Who longed for some stodge in a vast wilderness was
probably bald.
Any accomplished hypocrite can pull his own leg.
Most people are dead, and as a rule they deserve
it.
Most people aren’t born, and as a rule they don’t
deserve it.
Slander is a curse. The only person who can
partially escape backbiters is the Devil. He is able
to haul his tail round to the front and keep his eye
on it —or on most of it. But even he has to leave
the root of his caudal appendage exposed to the
comment of his enemies.
The only thing that is really fatal is death. Most
people die of something serious.
You can only die once. That is where the annoy-
ance comes in. If you could die as often as you
pleased perishing wouldn’t matter.
Never lend money to a friend ; you will lose him.
Lend it to an enemy ; you want to lose him.
There are just two things wrong with Hell —the
climate and the company. Many places have more
numerous drawbacks. Canberra is one of them.
Moses was the man who was there when the
Israelite went out. The Israelite has been out after
somebody else’s money ever since.
It is set forth on the authority of the Church that
a man should not marry his grandmother. He doesn’t
want to, but he feels so virtuous because he hasn’t
done it that he has two extra beers.
When you feel bad in the morning never drink
more than a pint of the dog that bit you. Pro-
bably you had a quart of him the night before, and
even a dog isn’t a tank.
Always think twice before you act, except when
a motor-car is rushing at you. Then jump three
times before you think.
Don’t be found dead in the same paddock with a
Freetrader, because his remains are bad company.
Also, don’t be found dead in the same paddock with
a Protectionist because it is a pity for him to be
dead. In either case you will be dead, and that is
the only sort of investment that pays no dividend.
“Rosna’s” note from Britain:—
King George and Young Solemnity, otherwise Ed-
ward of Wales, have gone down to the sea to put the
seal of their approval on the Australia, the first of
the Commonwealth’s Dreadnoughts. The British
Admiralty’s gift to the Commonwealth, Rear-Admiral
Patey, acted as showman-in-ehief, and the 50
per cent, good Australians in the crew were alarmed
to see him drop on his knees, while the monarch
amiably prepared to cut off his head with the cap-
tain’s sword. It turned out, however, that his
Majesty was merely giving him a knighthood to wear
on stormy days. Next day the Admiral invited Mr.,
Mrs. and the Misses Ausitralia-in-London to tea on
board, and 600 members of the family, in two special
trains, went to Portsmouth to have it. The usual
miles of red carpet, bunting, ices, strawberries,
cream and floriculture were spread around—while a
band played rag-time, having meanly forgotten to
learn the “Song of Australia.” Later, Shy Commis-
sioner Reid recited an original monologue, accom-
panied by bland inquiries from the jolly Australian
end of the crew regarding the health of a certain
dry dog, now deceased.

the gaol. He has been removed to quarantine.

NEW ARRIVAL (cheerfully, to convalescent):

better off here than we are in Darling-
hurst, you know!"
The Maori is theoretically a “noble sav-
age,” but he isn’t sanitary, and despite his
occasionally upstanding figure he has qply a
shred of constitution. Even dried
doesn’t build him up. All the recorded
deaths from small-pox during the recent
epidemic have happened among the Maoris.
Henare seems to take everything useless that
comes in his way.
TEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, the futility of afternoon-tea. . . .What a sham '
What a waste! — Bitter cry of “Mrs. Philistine ” in
B. 31' 7/’ 13.
Tea, tea, afternoon-tea—
Everyone’s anxious to brew it for me !
Whv this abuse of it ?
What is the use of it?
Oh, thou futility—afternoon-tea !
Tea, tea, afternoon-tea,
Wowsers may like and absorb greedilee :
Their gossip goes with it,
Scandal arose with it ;
No milk of kindness is stirred with the tea.
Tea, tea, afternoon-tea—
Poisonous cup that is frequent and free.
Only to think of it—
Sane people drink of it,
Till their digestion goes B-U-N-G!
Tea, tea, afternoon-tea—
Tannin’ and talk, and a “spoon,” too, maybe !
I’ve got a down on it.
Why do I frown on it?
It drives me always to soda-and-b.
VICTORIA.
T. CAKE.
THE WORST OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Owing to the Balkan war, the price of special
Turkish cigarettes has been doubled in London.
—ltem of cable news.
Into the heavy-scented shop
Where dwells Raftopolus the Greek,
There strolls a neatly-tailored fop
To sniff the aromatic reek:
“How’s this, Pheidippides?” he cries,
“What swindle’s on, I’d like to know?
My bill from you is twice the size
Of what it was a month ago!”
“Oh, Mistaire Snook!” says beaming Raff,
With deprecation in his eye,
“You always talka to maka laff—
I getta not the dam supply !
My farmers senda me no more
Da gooda leaf for cigarette;
I tella you, this Balkan war
She breaka half my business yet!
“Bulgaria go upside-down !
There was five brother plant and plough
Da landa near a little town,
But ajla dead and buried now !
Ivan and Stephen, George and John,
And Yakoob —what you calla Jeem —
First thing I know, poor Yakoob’s gone ;
I think da crows get hold of heem !
“How can I maka cigarette ?
Now, Mistaire Snook, you look abouta !
Stephen, he got da bayonet,
Da Turka dug his stomach outa !
I lika not da charge I make;
But how to help it? Tell me this,
If silly Ivan go and break
His foolish head at Kirk-Kiliss’ ?
“And all da leetle farmer chap
That sella me da leaf they grew,
They never stoppa from da scrap,
And they are dead and buried, too !
My customaire, he is my friend,
I lika not to treat him crook ;
Da awful war, she ought to end,
By Cripes, I think so, Mistaire Snook!”
So, on the smiling little farm
Of George and Steve and Bill and Jim
Fell blood and flame and deadly harm,
And fiery lust, and slaughter grim ;
The devastated vilayets
Are voiceless all from town to town ;
And eighteenpenny cigarettes
Have leapt in price to half a crown.
N.S.W.
A. M

Geelong. Tuesday.—For having
thrown kisses to some young women
walking along the street, on Saturday
evening, a young man was fined 20s. at
the City Court to-day. The young
women complained to Constable Par-
menter of the conduct of the youth,
who was taken in charge.—News item.

lians are the champion meat-eaters of
the world.

MOSTLY MELBOURNE.


ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Lddyson : The Hogans and the Dwyers
never did agree, and when, after many trials of
strength and mutual tribulations, the Dwyers won
the suit that took Hogan’s bit of “crick” and half
his land from him, Hogan sold out the remainder
and emigrated from Waddy with his small belong,
tngs. That evening Hannan called upon Tim Dwver
clicking a sympathetic tongue. “’Tis a great
shame,” said he. “Did ye hear it? That bla’guard
Hogan s bin puttin’ the big curse on yeh, Tim. He
shtood on th’ hill wid th’ gr-reat book befure him,
n wid two candles burnin’, V he shpoke th’ curse
on yeh, Tim Dwyer. ’Twas a big, black curse, ’n’
im never wantin’ to hear th’ likes iv it, so I’m
not, him crossin’ himself th’ while, ’n’ talkin’ th’
terrible wor-rds.” Tim Dwyer said nothing in
answer, but he was impressed. Phinn, too, had seen
the ceremony, and had heard Hogan recite his
magnificent curse. Two days later Tim Dwyer en-
tered the kitchen, grey-faced and tight-lipped, and
sank into the corner chair. “’Tis worrkin’,” said
he. “Th’ black curse is workin’ on me. Pitty’s
dead. ’ Pitty was the red cow. Shortly after that
Tims second son, Jimmy Dwyer, fell from a tree
and broke his arm. “’Tis the curse iv Dan Hogan,”
said Dwyer. And when the fire went through Tim’s
crop, and burnt his milking shed, he crossed him-
self and accepted the blow as inevitable. Sorrows
came down upon Tim Dwyer. His wife died. His
best plough-horse broke its neck against a tree.
The blight got at his spud crop. The rheumatism
took him across the back, and lamed him till he
bent his forehead to the ground. Then came the
widow Ryan with matrimonial symptoms. Tim
Dwyer had a fearful dread of the “red widdy
woman,” and seeing her coming across the stubble
would throw his hands to Heaven, and whisper
aloud, like a man stricken and helpless, “Th’ curse !
Th curse! It is th’ curse.” The time soon came
when Mrs. Ryan talked openly of “the day when
we’re t’ wed, Timmy dar-rlin’,” and Tim only
groaned in reply, and whispered: “’Tis th’ curse!”
Tim had been six months a widower when the red
widow said to him, “I’m thinkin’ Chewsdee fortnit
will be suitin’ me fer th’ weddin’, Tim Dwyer—-
Chewsdee fortnit at two iv th’ clock.” “Th*
curse!” whispered Tim within himself, and that
very day he put on his old funeral suit, and went
out on a pilgrimage. He was five days tracking
Dan Hogan. He came back, looking a new man,
erect, firm-lipped, the old fire in his grey eye. The
red widow ran to see what had happened him all
this time. “Was yeh off buyin’ fer th’ marryin’,
maybe, Tim darlin’ ?” she said. “Never a ha’p'orth,”
answered Dan, cold as the headstone of a saint.
“Well, well, ’tis all right, no matter fer what ye
vvint, Timmy; we marry the Chewsdee cornin’!”
“We do not, Bridget Ryan,” thundered Tim. “We
do not marry Chewsdee cornin', nor never a Chews-
dee. ’Tis all a mishtake, all a mishtake from th’
first. ’Twas a misapprehinsion killed me cow, ’n’
broke me b’y’s leg, ’n’ burrned me crop, ’n’ took
off me wife. ’Twas me brother Mike Dwyer Dan
Hogan cursed, ma’am, an’ ye can go ’n’ marry him!’’
“Souter Johnnie”: Re recent Bulletin
par. If the bomerang was used by the blacks in
the Cooktown district in “Wal’s” youth, then he
must have collected them all, with one exception,
and taken them with him when he left. At any
rate, the exception was the only one I saw among
the blacks during several years’ prospecting and
pilgrimage in the whole peninsula from Cape York
down to the West Normanby goldfield (90 miles
south of Cooktown as the crow flies sometimes and
the boomerang seldom). The Black Brother who
owned and mishandled it was a stranger from the
Mareeba district, and he couldn’t throw it worth
sixpence. In fact, I believe he stole it or bought
it by a process of swap for a gin or a jew’s harp
or something equally useless, an" ueglected to steal
or buy the art, knack or scien' of wielding it.
IN THE PROPER ORDER.
Captain Mary Booth, daughter of General Bram-
well Booth, was sleeping in the room next to that
in which the intruder was discovered. Learning
that he was driven to crime by hunger, she promptly
took him to the kitchen and set before him a good,
square meal. When the meal was finished 6he knelt
down and prayed with him, and insisted on the man
praying for himself. —Cable yarn.

lake up the collection."

“Olla Podrida”: Re Momba station (B.
19/6/’I3). The future owner may have elegant
diversion if he likes. The present manager has been
known to come to Wilcannia and White Cliffs
(both cities about 40 miles from the station home-
stead) driving mules one day and horses the next,
and riding horses and mules the following week;
and he once defied convention by coming to a ball
astride a camel, with his dress duds in front of
him. Now he rattles through the townships in a
motor-car. On the station, reputedly the biggest
in N. S. Wales, one can see camels in waggons,
camels “packed,” and camels in buggies, while the
boundary riders ride camels, mules and horses, and
the buggy boy rides a billy-goat. Also the station
bookkeeper has been experimenting with an aero-
plane, and tried to “break in” an emu to a go-
cart. S’truth, and city people talk about the mono-
tony of life out back!
* * * *
“F6ng-shui” (from Shanghai) : The
methods of Chinese fire-fighting are worth watching.
Fire broke out last week within our walled city,
which contains in the space of a small-sized Sydney
suburb 500,000 persons. At once 499,000 of them
got busy with the loot. The remaining
thousand, in firemen’s helmets and with
the help of a single tub of water, pro-
ceeded to put the fire out. The tub was
kept full by the aid of coolies, who were
paid at the usual rate per picul of water.
The chief inspector 6at by the tub check-
ing the coolies by the usual system of
wooden tallies. Tallies having run out,
the coolies refused to deliver any more
water. Fire in the meantime had con-
sumed a whole crooked row of houses and
was passing on to deal with the famous
willow-pattern tea-house, when a foreign
brigade arrived on the scene and quenched
the blaze. Whether ten or a thousand people got
burned to death it would be quite impossible to
find out, as no note is taken in China of little
things like that.

“Coola” : Bill and I reached the dance in
a state of suppressed unfriendliness, due to the fact
that Bill had let fall our beautiful bottle of ryebuck
on the hardest boulder along the track. I was
doing a perish about nine o’clock and wishing Bill a
happy new year or something, when he gave me
the office from the door to join him outside. “There’s
a plant somewhere,’’ he whispered. “I seen two
blokes do a flit behind a tree over there twice in the
larst half-hour, and when they came back I nicked
round and smelled them. And you can leave it to
me, it’s the right smell.” Bill was correct, but
after a few visits we differed as to the tree. How-
ever, I left it to Bill’s bushman-craft, and he was
certainly marvellous. About midnight we could
only crawl to the vegetable, but Bill found it.
While we were crawling, I saw a lot of shadowy sort
of blokes crawling likewise, and investigated. There
and then Bill's bush work went up the pole. There
was a bottle behind every tree I
“Souter Johnnie”: One Allen, Mayor of
E. Fremantle, recently went for a trip as far as
Darwin, and on his return gabbled a yarn that from
what he had seen he was strongly of opinion that
so far as the more tropical portion of the N.T. was
concerned, the White Australia policy was fore-
doomed to failure. If White Australia is foredoomed
in the N.T., then it is also foredoomed in North
Queensland and Northern Westralia, and if it is
foredoomed in all these places then the Mayor of
East Fremantle may as well send me his chain of
office to tie up my dog with. N. Queensland is
being soundly, if slowly, settled with white people,
who are doing very well, thank you, and I can honestly
say, after a residence of several years there and
several others in the N.T., that 1 prefer the climate of
the latter. In no part of the Territory is the climate
so really tropical as the Queensland coast north of
Capricorn, and that is just where the most pro-
gressive towns in that State are growing up, and
where the native-born are holding their own with
any in the Commonwealth, or out of it, for that
matter. From April to September inclusive, if the
Mayor of E. Fremantle can sleep out without a good
thick rug (at present I am using three!) and enjoy
it “in the more tropical portion of the Territory,”
then I’ll buy him a new hall.

THE CATTLEMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Th’ slow, sweet-scented cattle-boats shove up and
and down th’ coast —
Up an’ down th’ Nor’-west coast fer stock at Kim-
berley ;
An’ I ’ave “snared” a job at got no call
ter boast;
Fer cattleman’s th’ last job on this rotten earth
fer me.
Shovin’ up, shovin’ down—
Fairly makes yeh sick.
Bloomin’ ’eat it gets yeh beat —’twould bake a
blessed brick !
Up an’ down, down an’ up,
Makin’ squatters’ pelf—
’Oo would be a cattleman, if ’e could ’elp ’isself?
I guv Fremantle all me “dough,” an’ ’ad a lurid
spree,
Did a flamin’ perish till me ’eart was nearly broke ;
And w’en th’ d—d boss-cattleman came up an’
“worded” me,
I took th’ job ’e offered ; but I’m sorry now I spoke.
Shovin’ up, shovin’ down,
There’s no ’angin’ back,
Gotter work, yeh cannot shirk—th’ boss keeps on yeh
track.
Up an’ down, down an’ up.
Life ain’t wot it seems—
’Oo would be a cattleman, except in blanky dreams?
Th’ stoke-’ole staff an’ deck ’ands, they will ’ave
their little jokes,
Thinks we ain’t no class at all —fit subjects fer their
mirth;
It wouldn’t do fer them ter mix with common
cattle-blokes—
For we are wot th' orfficers call “scrapin’s of th’
earth.”
Shovin’ up, shovin’ down,
Runnin’ round with ’ose,
Slingin’ ’ay from day ter day, with chaff in eyes an’
nose;
Up an’ down, down an’ up,
Cattle from King Sound—
’Oo would be a cattleman, with good jobs ’angin’
round ?
You’ve gotter know yer proper place, an’ ’umble be
and meek,
Gotter learn: “There's tons ’o your sort kickin’
round ashore.”
An’ all ter earn yer bloomin’ grub an’ thirty bob a
week—
Gawd knows we ain’t no union mob, an’ cannot arst
fer more!
Shovin’ up, shovin’ down,
See th’ buzzin’ flies
Droppin’ in th’ tucker-tin and bungin up yer eyes!
Up an’ down, down an’ up,
Strike me deaf an’ blind !
’Oo would be a cattleman if ’e was sound of mind ?
We don’t know much about the sea. We ’ear of

“B.C. Manoora”: “Bulloak” draws the
long bow when he talks about 1200 natives having
gathered at McGrath’s Flat, at the mouth of the
Murray, for corroborees. I was living there over 50
years ago, and the greatest number assembled in
those early days would not be more than 200 or 300
—and even 200 or 300 make a grand corroboree. At
that time I was Sub-Protector of Aborigines, and
issued stores, etc., to Black Brother; so knew the
number of mouths he could muster to the last frac-
tion.
* * * *
“Eddyson” : IJomm, and Raill, and Miggs,
and Mulcahey, met and assembled in Anderson’s bar,
agreed unanimously to a policy of harassment for
Stanley. They had no very ardent objections to
Stanley, but he was new at the Band, and had
taken Raill’s job when Raill was fired for being
intermittently drunk and always disorderly. Be-
sides, Stanley was a man set against sociability and
drink, and given inordinately to the cultivation of
cabbages, a person who took a fatherly interest in
melons and regarded passing loads of manure with
a sort of poetic rapture. Homm, Raill, Miggs and
Mulcahey did not purpose using violence towards
Stanley. The idea was just to cover him with
public ridicule, and heap contumely on his head
when opportunity offered. Raill was first of the
adventurers to find an opening. There were no wit-
nesses, and no wholly unprejudiced person could
say how it happened, but it is certain that Raill
was presently found digging Stanley’s garden, and
it was obvious he was in no fit state for unwonted
exertion. His eyes were blackened, and there was
an ear like a cream-puff. Raill offered no explana-
tions, but he worked hard. There was never a more
disgusted man than Mulcahey. The spectacle con-
verted his contempt for Stanley into a vigorous
hostility. He seized the earliest subsequent oppor-
tunity of expressing himself. Mulcahey did not
start in Stanley’s garden till the day after, but
evidences of the fray were still strong upon him.
He wa| even more painfully burred and hit-up about
the head than Raill had been, but he dug hard.
Homm and Miggs hung about on the other side of
the fence, and questioned Mulcahey. They betrayed
6ome evidence of anxiety, but' Raill did not inter-
fere. He kept his distance, being satisfied in his
own mind. Mulcahey was silent under cross-examin-
ation for nearly an hour. Then his patience gave out;
he dug his spade into the soil. “Hell t’ yer souls!”
he sai<), “d’ yeh see that?” He pointed to an eye
like a pitch ball. “D’ yeh see thim teeth that’s
missin’ ?” He yanked his lip down. “D’ yeh mind
th’ shwellin’ t’ me ear? Well, Stanley agreed t’ let
me off th’ rest iv it, if I’d dig in his gar-r-den for
wan day, ’n’, blarst yeh, I’m diggin’ in his gar-r-
-den!” Homm and Miggs were quite satisfied. The
crusade of harassment went no further.

THE WNADERER : “Gad, it’s marvellous what
they can do with them machines. Fancy
goin’ backwards full speed, upside-down!”
“Eryander” : I have made special study of
the dingo, as being one of our few non-marsupials,
and can back-up “Pick” and “Tarago” (B. 17/7/’l3)
in labelling the beast harmless to man. Was sur-
prised once on a Gippsland track to find a big yellow
brute tailing me up about 50yds. back. He ske-
daddled when I stopped. I covered another mile or
so before I looked back again, and found that I had
now a respectful retinue of half a dozen warrigals.
They vanished into the scrub before I could throw
up my Winchester. Then I suddenly understood.
The' Irish terrier I had with me was a lady. I
camped by the track-side that night, and had nearly
dozed off when a furious outcry from Biddy aroused
me to the fact that sundry dim forms were perforat-
ing the gloom with whispered invitations to come
into the garden, Maud. I lay still until a more
daring bloke than the rest ventured up to within
about 6yds. of the fire. Then I fired three rapid
shots at him as he bolted. The third bullet got
him, and, though I remained awake till daylight,
I saw no more of the other gay Lotharios.

“Gouger” : My mate and I made our way
overland from China Camp (N.Q.) to the Mossman,
along a blackfellows’ track, over country not
crossed once in two years by a white man. On the
sea-washed mouth of the Daintree River, nine miles
from the nearest of the half-dozen scattered settlers,
we came to a well-built galvanised-iron
hut. Drawn up anyhow among the man-
groves was a 20ft. centreboard sailing
boat, without mast and hatches. We
pushed into the deserted hut, and were
surprised to see two good cane-hottomed
chairs and a wire mattress. Ye gods,
and in as wild a spot as possible on the
N.Q. coast! Our blackboy said a fisher-
man had lived there. Then we were awe-
struck by the sight of two broken tum-
blers, two good stoves, a school slate and
the remnants of an expensive breech-
loader, with reloading gear complete. We
demanded further information from our
black boy, and got it. The fisherman was
a Japanese, and had gone away. Fisher-
man? Most fishermen I know are con-
tent with a box for a chair and a bag for
a bunk, and then barely make tucker!
On looking some more, we found a num-
ber of fine paint-brushes, well used, with
some revolver and gun cartridges, and a
broken watch, with a -finely-chased case.
Outside the hut was a fishing-net, thrown
on the ground, and rotten. Even a black
boy knows a fishing-net costs a lot of
money, and the fisherman who leaves his
net to rot on the ground is unique—
especially the fisherman who fishes many
miles away from the nearest tiny port,
which, moreover, has its own kanakas in
the business. We suspected our fisher-
man was fishing for channels and reefs,
and marking the whereabouts of the
buoys and beacons that are dotted inside
the Barrier Reef. And when, among a
lot of knick-knacks never used by a fisher-
man, thrown together in an old box, we found a Lee-
Enfield military bullet, minus the shell, we sus-
pected harder than ever.
* * * *
“Ivory” : I upend myself to defend Binghi,
the tracker, from “McNorth’s” sarcastic remarks
(B. 19/6/’l3) that said Binghi is able to track a
man through long grass, scrub and (save us!) a
maize-patch just as any amount of white stockmen,
etc., would have no difficulty in doing. I have seen
some fine white trackers in my travels, and it took
a top-noteher to follow a trail through long, dry
grass. And as for scrub, well it sounds right enough
in theory to be focussing your optic on to the broken
twigs, but I should be long sorry to be lost in a
scrub and be dependent on any but a real top-notch
tracker to find me. Where “Me” and many others
fall in is in thinking, because every blaekfellow
they test is not a first-class tracker, that none of
them are. Tracking is a profession in the tribe, and
there are comparatively few experts at the game.
As for Binghi’s capabilities as a spear-thrower—
well, I’d as soon face a revolver up to 60 yards as
a well-nourished nigger behind a spear. As a final
spasm I may convey the information to “McN.” that
the best tracker, as a rule, is Mrs. Binghi. It is
she who trails the wily gohanna and other delica-
cies. Mr. B. supplies the assault and battery when
the time is ripe.
“Celia Loid” : I see some kind old lady is
worried about the fate of Fido and Floss, and
12cwt. of other dogs lately shipped from Sydney to
the Solomon Islands for trade with the natives.
Having trodden the paths of darkness with our
Black Brother in the dog trade, I’m passing the
lady the tip that she can put in her biggest worry
about these pups whenever she has time. Their
fate is a rotten one from the day they fly the Blue
Peter at Sydney. I have never yet pushed up
against a man, black, white or brindle, who could
give me any reason why a dog’s tooth should be
considered useful or ornamental; but as sure as
these dogs are traded to the nigs, they are out to
lose two teeth. Only two are reckoned of any
value. The operation of extracting the prizes is
most interesting to everyone but the tyke. After
the animal has been buried in the sand up to his
neck, with a good chunk of wood jammed well back
into his mouth, the two canine teeth are carefully
removed —hauled, bumped or dug out with any old
thing that won’t spoil them ; but I never saw them
worry as to how much dog they spoilt. After this
performance the teeth are carefully bored and
strung, and represent so much spot cash (about
2s. each). The dug-up dog is just a village cur,
and hasn’t much use for any teeth, as his diet con-
sists of vegetable refuse and a little rotten fish
occasionally. About six months of this and he is
bald from nose to tail, with a hide on him that
would make a fellmonger envious. Some are more
fortunate and escape—to the alligators. But these
are the exceptions.
