“Paff” : Met him in a North Queensland
copper town, where he had been sent for “experi-
ence.” AVe were having tea on the hotel verandah
when he strolled in. The waitress offered him a cup
of tea, but he replied: “No, thanks, girl; I—haw
detest that —haw—beastly condensed milk.” She
then offered to get him some goat's milk, if he
would hold the goat. We watched the fun. After
considerable trouble they caught the .goat, which
was of the large AVilliam variety, and the Johnnie
held it by the horns whilst the girl went to get a pail.
After struggling with the embittered male for about
10 minutes, and there being still no sign of the girl
he called to us. “Have you seen —ah —that girl?
I can hardly—haw—hold the —haw—vicious animal,
and it —haw —emits such a—haw—disagreeable
odor.” I thought that the joke had gone far
enough, and told him to let William go. I also
explained other things. He confided to me after-
wards : “What a—haw—beastly juke to play on a
fellow ! And she seemed such a —i:aw—respectable
girl.”
From Brassey’s ‘‘Naval Annual” (1913)
The Mother Country can no longer bear the grow-
ing burden of protecting at sea the whole Empire
and its Ocean trade. Expenditure on Army and
Navy has more than doubled in 20 years. In con-
sequence of the concentration forced by Germany’s
challenge, the China battle squadron has disappeared ;
Japan is undisputed mistress of the Eastern Seas!
In the Mediterranean 10 years ago we maintained a
powerful fleet, which has ceased to exist. In the
West Indies, in the waters of South America, and
other regions where we have vast commercial in-
terests, the British flag is hardly ever shown.
And Australia, which lies in “the Eastern
Seas,” of which “Japan is undisputed mis-
tress,” continues to be tremendously inter-
ested in its penny postage stamp, and its
polo, and the eternal struggles of one small
politician to show the infamy of another
small politician and his utter unfitness in
some small way for small office; and it
dawdles over its transcontinental railway,
and raises Cain at every proposal to put on
an extra tuppence of National taxation;
and it sends its work to the foreigner so
that the foreigner may have fighting men;
and it bleats of Empiah and arms itself
with a new box of voice jubes so that it
may howl “Rule Britannia” louder than
ever. Also what scrap of work it pronoses
to attempt on the Port Darwin railway is to
begin at the north, so that when an enemy
takes Port Darwin he will have so much the
more railway to carry his troops
south, whereas if work began at the south
the Australian would have so much more
railway to carry his troops north. This
arrangement arises from the fact that if
work began from the south the Government
would have to commit itself to one of two
routes, and it can’t commit itself without
losing votes somewhere. And the uniform
railway gauge hasn’t arrived. And the
country says it can’t make its own locomo-
tives, but must send the job abroad. And
a great many Lahorites object to immigra-
tion lest we should have men here to defend
the country. And many people of all shades
of political opinion have “conscientious
scruples” about defending the country at
all. And incidentally Brassey’s “Naval
Annual.” which takes no special interest in
Australia and is a cold record of events,
remarks that “Japan is undisputed mistress
of the Eastern Seas.”
■*-
One of the first things that Victoria’s Wil-
liam Watt discovered when he arrived in
England, six months ago, was that the
marketing of Australian products in Lon-
don was on an entirely wrong basis. It
was borne in on him that the system needed
decentralising. To that end he has an-
nounced his intention of asking the States
“to unite in promoting one or two shipping
services from the Commonwealth, to Liver-
pool, Manchester, Bristol, Hull, etc.” Eng-
land is a very small country, with a large
number of railway systems. London is a
very big port, with a vast and splendid col-
lection of the most modern facilities for
distributing imports. Indeed, if this were
not so, a big proportion of the inhabitants
of the Freetrade-blighted land would be in
a bad way as regards the securing of the
bare necessaries of life. In short, the de-
centralisation of trade is not a crying need
in England. Victoria — Watt’s own State
-—presents the obverse side of the picture.
It is a much larger place than England. It
has but one railway system. And practi-
cally all the State’s exports and imports,
as well as most of the locally-produced
goods that are afterwards distributed
amongst the inhabitants, are deliberately
crowded, by that system, day in day
out, into the more or less out-of-date and
indifferently-eouipped Port of Melbourne.
If Watt really wants to advantage his
State, he will leave the Socialistic business
of promoting Australian shipping services
to Bristol and Hull alone, and, instead,
lavish his energy and any money that he has •
to spare, or that he can induce others to
spare, on the really urgent business of
opening up the starved ports along the Vic-
torian coast. That would do more good to
Victorian trade than a whole fleet of State
steamers trading out of Port Phillip to
the effectively decentralised regions of Eng-
land. But Watt, of course, knows this
already. The only thing that debars him
from doing the right thing in the matter is
the fact that his Administration, like so
many previous ones, practically relies for
its life on the vested interests of Mel-
bourne.

TO STOP FALLING HAIR.
Falling hair —the forerunner of baldness
—is nearly always due to the presence of a
parasite known as the dandruif germ. If
your hair falls out the chances are ten to
one that the dandruff germ is the cause of it,
and you cannot do better than to get your
chemist to make you up a bottle of the old
reliable formula, consisting of 3oz. of Bay
Rum, loz. Lavona de Composee and i dram
Menthol Crystals. Apply night and morn-
ing, rubbing briskly into the scalp with the
finger tips. This preparation, which enjoys
a world-wide reputation as a hair grower
and dandruff destroyer, is popularly known
as Lavona Hair Tonic, and many chemists
keep it prepared ready for sale. Lavona
Hair Tonic is claimed by many to restore
grey hair to its original color as well as
making the hair grow, and destroying the
dandruff germ.*


A “SPLENDID OFFER."
LONDON, W’ednesday Evening. —An anony-
mous sympathiser has offered to advance the
fares, where necessary, in connection with
the New South Wales widows scheme of im-
migration. The first contingent will sail
during October.
The New South Wales Immigration Department arranged to send out to the
State small batches of widows of the artisan class, with one child each; also tea-
shop waitresses, if they were prepared to accept domestic service.
It is now known thut this “anonymous
sympathiser" is no other than Mr. Tony
Weller, whose views ujK>n “Widders” are
notorious.

lhe vigorous Grant llervey’s volume of
patriotic verse,_ “Australia Yet,” will be pub-
lished by Lothian, of Melbourne, within the

Flickered out, in Maoriland, George
Henry Swan, aged 80, for 15 years mayor
of Nanier and three years M.P. for the
same restful town. He was one of those
rare birds, a brewer with a soul above
dividends ; but there is no need to get ex-
cited and raise a monument to him. He
has several, chief amongst them being the
Marine Parade, at Napier, a boulevard pro-
bably unsurpassed for beauty on any part
of the Pacific coast-line.
Angus, M.P. in the S.A. Assembly: “It
was reported that Mr. had resigned
from the leadership of the Labor party
owing to ill health, but I am glad to see
that he looks quite as well as usual.” “Oh,”
said the sarcastic Peake, “he lets conceal-
ment like a worm i’ the bud prey on his
damaged cheek.” But, as a matter of fact,
Verran has really been unwell, though he
would probably have been displaced in any
case.
Naumai: P. C. Webb, who succeeds the
late Speaker Guinness as member for Grey
in the Maoriland Parliament, belongs to the
Federation of Labor crowd (identical with
the Industrial Workers of the World, who
have their home in the Yankee country).
They oppose compulsory military training,
and advocate a general strike in case of
war. Which means that they propose to
sit down and let the invader take every-
thing he can find. And that idea will suit
the invader down to the ground. He will
take everything. Incidentally he will per-
haps hang AVebb on suspicion, thinking
that a man who professes such impossible
ideas must have some great conspiracy or
device or ace up his sleeve.

Last week s cable news that the Earl of Rocksavage
had married Miss Sybil Sassoon in a registry o&ce
must have caused London Society more thrills than
a dozen murders The extraordinarily good-looking
hen to the Cholmondeley Marquisate has figured m
the hmelight, through no fault of his own, for the
years. He is one of the best polo-
p ‘ jers in the world in fact, he has represented
. n ,^f in t severa l times against the United States. lie
is likewise a champion at tennis and at a number of
other games and pastimes. On the other hand, he is
chronically broke. It is his misfortune that he is
the idol of the upper middle class London “knut”
a grim infliction. His manner of dressing, phrases,
habits, and so forth, are copied by his worshippers
with slavish fidelity. The Sassoons are outrageously
wealthy Indian Jews. If they haven’t a powerful
dash of the tar-brush in them, they are grossly
libelled by their complexions, eyes, etc. Most of
their money comes from textile mills in Calcutta,
The first Sassoon to burst on London Society per-
formed the feat in the latter part of Queen Vic-
toria’s reign. He took Dorchester House in Picca-
dilly, the immense mansion which had been let to
the Shah of Persia (who left it in such a state of
filth and decayed meat that a question arose whether
it wouldn’t be cheaper to burn it down than to
clean it), and which was inhabited latterly by an
American millionaire. Ed. VII., then Prince of
Wales, fraternised with the somewhat discolored plu-
tocrat, and socially he was a made man. According
to the cable, Miss Sassoon is to get an allowance
of £25,000 per annum, and a lump sum down of
£50,000. This would, of course, have no influence
whatever on Lord Rocksavage. Love regards, mere
lucre as so much superfluous dross.
A FOWL WHICH CAME HOME
TO ROOST.
Britain’s champion sleeping-man died the
other week of cold and exposure, due to a
fall into a river—-when napping, it is sup-
posed. The shock of the cold water woke
him up, but as soon as he had clambered out
he fell asleep again and died of his damp-
ness. Deceased, Arthur Mann, was the son
of a Derbyshire J.P., and a
much-travelled person. His
life appears to have been mainly
spent in looking for a country
where he wouldn’t fall into a
cataleptic sleep, but he never
found the right climate. He
had a way of collapsing sud-
denly and remaining practically
dead for weeks at a stretch.
Once, in America, he nearly got buried by
mistake. Seemingly he was quite dead when
he was buried, for the police had kept him
under surveillance until decomposition had
made considerable progress.
A parson named Brough, who has hung
out his clerical shingle in India for a num-
ber of years, lately told the people of West
Maitland (N.S.W.) that King George did
“a magnificent stroke of genius” when he
visited India, and that he is known there as
“the Sympathiser.” Well, sympathy is
cheap enough. There have been rulers
called “the Perfect,” “the Bad,” “the Mad,”
“the Devil,” “the Promiser,” “the Peace-
maker,” “the Pig-Dealer,” “the Unready,”
“the Thunderbolt.” the Sudden.” “the
Lame,” “the Well-Beloved.” “the Terrible,”

Long-legged,” “the Drunkard,” “the Flute-

“Sympathiser” more or less doesn’t matter
among the crowd. Some day there may
even be a King nicknamed “the Hen-
Stealer.”
Chief Justice Way (S.A.) has been seri-
ously ill, and his friends were very anxious.
He was born just three months before the
first shipload of settlers arrived in the State,
and he has been Chief Justice for 37 years.
His father was a Bible Christian parson
who never received more than about £l5O a
year, and the son still remains a good Metho-
dist and till lately was always ready to pre-
side at a tea-meeting if so desired. Another
hobby is the breeding of long-woolled sheep,
and as there is more money in that, he now
owns a good deal of city property. Before
he went on the bench he was a law-partner
of ex-Senator ’Siah Symon.
Owing to the increased cost of living the
salary of the Clerk of the Vic. Legislative
Council (the successor to the immortal
Jenkins) has been raised from £9OO to
£H)()0 a year. The late Jenkins got more
than that, but he was an oleaginous iden-
tity with a capacious beard.

, A most respectable educational publication is the
Australasian Vacuum Review, prepared every
quarter by the technical department of the Vacuum
Oil 0 O j n Melbourne. If its first intention is to
make “every salesman and other employee” of the
co. wise t° the virtues of the goods they deal with,
and how t 0 h the trade its matte r is none the
i ess educational all round. And the review breathes
a spirit of fair dealing-, anyhow. C. A. Moser, man-
aging dire ctor of the Vac.,‘is about the most popular
and pat ernal boss in Melbourne. Himself a clean-cut
cosmopolitan Yank of the Hanoverian type, he runs
the show with a strong leaning towards Australian
business capacity in the staff. Moser, seemingly, is
the schoolmaster who impresses on the undermasters
and pupils the system of organisation and manage-
ment under which he was trained, and for which he
reckons that he can find just as good material in his
adopted country as in the place the co. radiates from,
His policy of encouraging local industry in brains
by checking the importation of heads of departments,
has endeared the amiable disciolinarian to the em-
ployees. “They all love Moser - .” Characteristic of
the man is his article on “Modern Salesmanship” in
the March issue of the Vacuum Review. His pre-
cepts are clean: “Very few businesses can endure on
the one-sale principle. Therefore no man or house
can afford to sell to a customer but once. In other
words, the basis of commerce is confidence—faith on
the part of the buyer in the salesman, in the firm he
represents, in the goods he sells. The satisfied buyer
is the best advertisement,” etc., etc. The immediate
cause of this commercial-minded Review is shown in
an extract from Printers’ Ink at the end of the
number: “Nine points of the battle is to have an
intelligent disciplined army of workers at your back,
The big concerns make a practice of keeping up the
post-graduate education of their salesmen by means of
literature and conventions, and more and more of the
lesser concerns are making use of booklets and ‘senti-
ment’ to the same end.”
F . R Suttor> p res ident of
the N.S.W. Leg. Council, should
know a thing or two about poli-
tics. He represented Bathurst
in the Leg. Ass. for many years,
having been elected 12 times.
Then he decided to try the Leg.
Council. After a spell there
he resigned (1891), and was
re-elected to the Assembly.
Changing his mind again, he
was re-appointed to the Council
in 1900, and has been President
practically ever since. His
father before him represented Bathurst and
Roxburgh Counties (in the Ist to the 6th
State Parliaments inclusive), his brother
represented East Macquarie in two Parlia-
ments, and his uncle, John Bligh Suttor,
represented East Macquarie in the sth and
6th Parliaments. And all four were at one
time or another in Leg. Council. Frank
Suttor, the last of them, was born in 1839,
but is still a sprightly chicken at 74.
Erstwhile Crown Prosecutor Gurner, who
has just completed a 12 months’ commission
as an acting Judge in Melbourne, made his
task pretty miserable owing to his peculiar
sense of fairness and toleration. The mere
fact that juries disagreed at the fate of two
or three a week, necessitating his Honor’s
presence at the court till 8, 9 or 10 o’clock
at night, did not ruffle him in the slightest.
Neither did he ever weary of the tall stories
that were told by prisoners and counsel—
which all goes to show what a considerate
old soul he is. But Judge Gurner’s methods
had their disadvantages; for instead of
clearing away doubts his Honah manufac-
tured them in large quantities, and the
always-present 12th juryman, filled with
either recalcitrancy or mere cussedness,
fastened on to them like a limpet. The
whole paraphernalia was repeatedly hung
up in consequence. It is a great rule in the
criminal courts: “Find out which of the
12 jurors is the twelfth juror, and kill
him.”
From a man who campaigned with him: —
If “Bill” Lyne had taken reasonable care of him-
self, he would have lived to be a hundred. But
even his rare physique and stamina couldn’t stand
the strain he put on them. When out campaigning
in the Hume he would arrive in town in the morn-
ing, knock about all day, put most of the steady
consumers of liquor under the table, address a
meeting for two or three hours at night, and then
(still fresh and full of “go”) drive off through the
bush for 30 or 40 miles to get an early start next
day—or take train to Sydney or Melbourne, if occa-
sion required it. As a “local representative” Bill
was for 30 years the champion. People in all sorts
of out-of-the-way places had come to realise that if
they wanted a post office or a policeman or a road
or a culvert or anv other old thing, the member
for the Hume was the man to get it. If you come
across a bit of good road in the wilds of the Upper
Murray and remark upon it, your host is sure to say:
“Yes, Lyne got us that.” In fact, if you take
away the things that Lyne got in most parts of the
great Hume electorate, and more especially near the
border, there is practically nothing left but the
indigenous wombats and the rest of the landscape.
In an election the big-bodied, kind-hearted “bullock-
ing” Australian got a most remarkably-diversified
support. Labor organisers worked enthusiastically
to secure his return: and rich squatters, to whom
Labor is anathema, lent him motor-cars and horses.
And how he did stick to his friends! It was quite
characteristic that once he held up a great public
work —a big hospital—because there was opnosition
to_ the appointment of a nurse whom Bill—then
Minister for Works—had recommended for a place
on the staff. The Board ignored the Minister’s
recommendation at about the time that the hospital
was being completed. Thereupon Bill promptly in-
timated that if Nurse was not appointed, he
would not take over the building, and there would
be no subsidy. Behind the scenes there was indig-
nation and uproar, but “the tumult and the shout-
ing” didn’t disturb Bill, and the nurse got her
billet. Incidentally she was about the best nurse the
district ever had, and she was not related to Bill
even in the 170th degree.
South Australia runs to substantial com-
fort all round rather than big fortunes in one
hand, and the Andrew Tennant estate, just
proved at £550,000. is the first to top the
half-million since the Angas probate of ten
years back. There are three sons and three
daughters to share it.

CRESS WELL,
of the Austra-
lian Navy.
“Jimmie Pannikin,” otherwise Donald
Fraser, recently achieved his 50th year; but
the accumulation of time seems to lie very
lightly on his shoulders. Neither is his eye
dim, nor his step devoid of the hefty spring
of youth. It was in 18S5 that his first con-
tribution blew into the editorial desk of Tub
Bulletin office. The Bulletin office has
been shifted since then, but the stream of
“Pannikin” contributions followed it, and
threatens to go on till long after the present
staff has died. In 1886 “J. Pannikin” went
forth to the Great Scrub; and most of his
time since has been spent in its treeless
glades.
Died at 77, the Rev. H. H. Britten, for
many years head of All Saints’ Anglican
College, Bathurst (N.S.W.). Some 30
years ago Britten was the central figure of
one of the most famous of the Ma State’s
court cases. While he was rector of St.
Anne’s, Hunter’s Hill, some of the church
property caught fire. An influential
parishioner—brother of a present-day Judge
—accused the parson of putting a match to
it, and he stood his trial on the charge.
Proceedings were made very warm ; but in
the end there was a triumphal acquittal and
general parish rejoicings.
HENRY KENDALL.
He was ambidextrous; I have seen him change his
pen from one hand to the other, and so continue his
writing; he explained to me that his right wrist was
weak. —Letter to Sydney Telegraph.
Ambidextrous, was he? Well, what of it?
So's our Religious Editor. Neither does he let
his right hand know what his left hand is
doing.
KNIGHTHOOD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sickened and spent, with swimming head brought
low,
The combat fearing;
Assured in your despair you shall not know
The victors’ cheering,
Were it not well to let lax effort go,
And, limply lying,
Take craven counsel of your soul, and so
Try dying?
Not yet! Fierce drums awaken in your breast,
And beat to call you
On to the charge, the shock, the battle-test
That must befall you.
Unhorsed and shaken, seize your sword anew,
Though spent and dying,
And then, with strangling heart and straining thew,
Die trying !
victoria.

During the past half-year the strike of deck-hands,
etc., occurred, and that and the subsequent increase
of wages lead the directors to announce that “the
profits have not been sufficient to make adequate
provision for depreciation of steamers.” But, to
put it quite plainly, nobody with any knowledge of
accounts takes this co.’s statements seriously ; and if
the public says “Bluff!” to this last remark, the
directors have only themselves to thank for it.
And, anyhow, they have been too greedy to get any
sympathy. Since Dec., 1905, the capital actually
got from shareholders has been £134,986. During
the same period the co.’s landed property has in-
creased from £88,408 to £131,091; cash balances,
less liabilities, have increased from £18,700 to
£35,310; and about £15,000 has been put into
wharves, waiting rooms, etc. These three items re-
present £74,293 of the new capital, leaving only
£60,693 of the capital to represent new ferries.
lor that money the co. has acquired nine new steam-
ers besides horse-punts, and has made large pay-
ments on account of three other vessels. The outlay
on this lot has been approximately £180,000; and,
as shown above, only £60,000 of that has been pro-
vided by shareholders. The balance has come out of
the pockets of customers, who, of course, have also
provided the dividends, which have been equal to
about 14 p.c. on the actually paid capital. There is
another wav of putting it. The present paid capital
is £324,986, of which amount the shareholders in
the present co. have actually paid £214,986, in-
cluding the capital ( £60,000) in the original co.
The present net assets, apart from ferries altogether,
amount to £194,444. In other words, the co. has
assets apart from ferries representing all but
£20,542 of the capital which shareholders have put
Ho the business. This means that the public, be-
siaes providing the dividends, has paid for the whole
of the ferries except as to the sum of £20,542.
.hat is the history of the past 15 years. This
paper will do no guessing as to the history of
the next 15 years. So far the co. has
seemed to actually thrive on threats and fore-
bodings. When the Labor party came into power it
looked as though something might really happen.
But nothing has happened; nothing happened even
when the co.’s mismanagement led to the hanging
up of all harbor services for nearly a week, and
when even the daily papers were driven to curse the
co. It is no use prophesying about a co. which is
so plainly under the Devil’s protection.

Dec., T 2.. 32,412 120 „,, 30,000 960 1,452 72T16
June, T 3.. 42,393 120 ~ ~30,000 937 11,456 83,572
If George Swinburne, one of the new Interstate
Commissioners, can make as big a success of Aus-
tralia as he has of this co., of which he has been
chairman for very many years, this paper will shout
him a halo when he dies. Unfortunately Australia
didn’t get quite the same start as this co. got. The
co. was given 28 years in which to make practically
as much money as it could squeeze out of Broken
Hill. Certainly, a limit of a sort was set on the
price to be charged for water ; but as the price of
water for most purposes has been kept at ss. per
1000 gallons, and as it costs no more to let water
run into a reservoir at Broken Hill than anywhere
else, the co. has been on a splendid wicket. No
doubt there were risks at the start; nobody knew
then that Broken Hill was going to be that miracle,
a mining field enjoying greater prosperity 25 years
after its birth than at the beginning, and showing
more ore reserves on its 25th birthday than on its
fifth, or 10th or 20th. And least of all, nobody sus-
pected that the smelters, which used very little water,
would be superseded on the field by mills, which use
a very great deal. So the co. has had a life gaudy
beyond all expectation. But the end is drawing near ;
of its 28 years only five are left, and when they run
out it will have to hand the works over to the Gov-
ernment and get nothing in return except a cordial
invitation to go and wind itself up. At its birth,
or, rather, shortly after its birth, it had a capital of
£200,000. Subsequently, as the table shows, it de-
cided that, as its interest in its principal asset was
fast closing in, it ought to pay out some of the sink-
ing fund which it had accumulated. It therefore re-
turned £150.000 (15s. per share). But, though
there was only £50,000 capital left, the reservoir,
etc., continued just as fine money-making appliances
as ever, wherefore in the past half-vear (a very
droughty one in the Far West) a profit of no less
than £42,393 was made. It is impossible to say
exactly what the co. will be required to hand over
to the Government free of charge, but, roughly, at
present it has £95,000 net, mostly in cash, which
will not pass to the State —except, as to some little
of it, on a valuation. That is 9s. 6d. per share.
Then it has five years to go ; and what it can do in
five years can only be guessed at from the fact that
in the past five years it has made £265,000, or
265. 6d. per share. Certainly it may not do so well
during the next five years. The chances are it will
not. Broken Hill may hot boom quite so vigorously ;
also the Government has another reservoir under con-
struction, and, though work on it is now dawdling,
water may possibly be running through the mains in
two years from now. Still, that is not going to
knock this co. out ; it will still have the bulk of
the mines’ custom, which is worth about 10 times
as much as the town supply.

Winchcombe, Carson, Ltd., formed in
Sydney, July, 1910, to take over the business of
Winchcombe, Carson and Co., Ltd., pastoral property
and products salesmen, as from April 1 of that year
(the deficit to start with consisted of goodwill less
reserves transferred from the old co.) :
Towards De-
June, Profits. Dividends. Reserves. ficit.
1910 £31,729
1911 ..£20,214 6 p.c. =£11,212 £9002 22,727
1912 . . 17.893 6 „ „ 12,345 5548 17,179
1913 .. 17,834 6 „„ 12,345 5489 11,690
This co., which formerly issued reports everj' half-
year, has dropped the practice—a bad one with a
concern which reaps its principal harvest only once
a year—and now sensibly makes up its accounts
only once in 12 months. In the table the evidence
of the previous bad habit has been wiped out, and
all is forgiven. But things are not as good as they
should be. The prospectus on which the co. was
floated set out that profits for five years had aver-
aged £20,774 p.a. ; and as the public put £lOO,OOO
more into the business, the profits, instead of shrink-
ing, ought to have grown. It is certainly up to
somebody to explain why a business which in the
old hands showed a profit of £20,774 a year on a
capital of £105,755 can now do no better than
£17,834 on a capital of £205,755, and, moreover,
instead of improving, does a little worse every year
than it did in the year before. Still, though the co
does worse, The Bulletin is by no means prepared
to say that it does badly. The profits for the past
year, the worst of the three, were equal to 84 p.c.
on the capital; and though 84 p.c. isn’t anything
tremendous at a time when you can get 7 p.c. on
good, solid bank-stock investments and over 8 p.c.
on many good-looking industrials, yet it is only fair
to remember that .just upon half the capital of this
concern is in freehold land and stores ; and one can’t
reasonably expect a big return with that kind of
security. Further, a great proportion of the £55,000
which has been put into freehold during the past
two years has gone to Brisbane, where the co. has
but lately established itself; and no doubt the
accommodation there is a long way ahead of the
business so far. For the present, therefore, The
Bulletin wouldn’t expect the new capital to produce
much of a return. At the same time, it certainly
should not be costing money, for it isn’t as though
the new premises carried a heavy mortgage. The
co.’s total liabilities are £83,766, and apparently
this, or most of it, is represented by advances against
wool and produce and on stock mortgages, etc. This
miscellaneous heap has now grown to £147,739. If
the co. makes losses in the future it will be through
these advances. Pretty well every stock and station
produce firm sets out with the best intention in the
world to stand out of this business ; but keen com-
petition for selling commissions drags them in, just
as it dragged the Melbourne breweries into tied pubs ;
and when drought happens along it is hard work to
get out. For this reason it is satisfactory to see
that the co. began with a comparatively modest 6
p.c. div., and that figure still allows of a substantial
lay-by. As the amount accumulated hasn’t yet any-
thing like replaced the goodwill or paper assets in
the business, and as the risks are apparently steadily
increasing, a lot more laying-by should be done before
the dividend is shifted up at all.

This co. started in 1909 with a capital of £BO,OOO,
of which no less than £46,235 was represented by
goodwill and other inedible and intangible assets:
and The Bulletin didn’t like it. Fortunately the
co. fell right into a boom, and it was just the sort
of co. to make money out of such conditions. Wages
were being put up, and, instead of taking a bit of
lunch to town, Willy shouldered into Sargents with
the best of them. And Bill's missus also, being un-
able to get “help,” told Bill that he had better have
something in town, because he wouldn’t be able to
get anything except bread-and-jam at home. So be-
tween one thing and another Sargents have been
making a lot of money ; and though the co. has paid
a steady 10 p.c., surplus profits have piled up and
considerably more than half of the old paper deficit
has been thus wiped out. Of the present capital of
£lOO,OOO, all that is not represented by tangible,-
assets is now £17,169. In other words, whereas at
the outset only Bs. 6d. in the £ was represented bv
anything that you could smash or sit on or drink
out of or fall over in the dark, now there is 16s. 6d.
in that category. Whether the tangible assets are
really worth 16s. 6d. in the £ The Bulletin cer-
tainly doesn’t know. The balance-sheet says they
are; but it depends to a great extent on the stock-
taking. For instance, furniture, fittings and plant
are valued at £38,195 ; and there is no way in the
world of checking that except by going round and
counting the cups and finding out whether enough
has been written off them out of profit to counter-
balance the extra loss in value through having five
chips taken out of them where last year there were
only two chips. Profits in a business of this sort
may be rather easily over-estimated by neglecting
to write off enough for depreciation. However, trade
undoubtedly keeps good; and though it won’t con-
tinue so good when the aforementioned Bill is more
often out of work than he is at present, yet the co.,
having less of its capital represented by unproduc-
tive goodwill assets, will be in a far better position
than it was when it started. A co. of this sort, de-
pendent so largely on management and the caprice
of the public, never can offer a gilt-edged security;
but it can make very fine profits while things go
well with it.

This co., after piling up profits gloriously for
some years, has lately been launching out vigor-
ously, and has been and still is spending a heap of
money on new carbonising plant, gas-holder, vertical
retorts, and extensions of. mains. The cost has
been provided partly by a new issue of shares,
partly by the issue of a few more debentures. As
to the new share capital, the table shows that, as
usual with gas cos., some of it fell into shareholders’
pockets in the shape of a bonus. For many years
the total paid capital had been £52,500; it now
stands at £88,286. (There are 120,000 ordinary
shares of 10s. fully paid, 72,883 pref. shares of
10s. paid with a few exceptions to 75., and 7117
pref. shares of 10s. fully paid.) Liabilities, includ-
ing debentures, amount to £21,200; liquid assets,
including stock, are set down at £19,040. On
account of the construction works in progress, the
figures are undergoing large changes; and no doubt
there are still more changes ahead. It may be that,
on this account, the future cannot be fairly judged
by the past; still, things have been so remarkably
good with the co. that ■it does not seem possible
that the introduction of more modern plant will
drive shareholders to the poorhouse. It is much
more likely that trouble will come by way of Par-
liament. Queensland gas cos., like those of N. S.
Wales, have hitherto been under practically no legis-
lative restraint, and if they keep going that way
they will have far more luck than those of England,
to which Right-thinking Persons mostly look for
their precedents.

That last line doesn’t look any too well; the dis-
closed profit was only £951 in excess of the 8
p.c. dividend; and if there was only that much of
a margin last year, what are things likely to be in
the future? For, though the construction boom
has eased off, many large works which had been
commenced are being completed, and, on the whole,
there has been far more activity than there will
be under normal conditions. In fact, in many direc-
tions Sydney has built considerably beyond its normal
requirements, so there is going to be less than a
normal demand for some builders’ requisites. But
there is this point to consider: how well such mer-
chants have done during the past few years their
accounts are hardly likely to suggest; if they are
wise (and most of them have been long enough in
the business to know how erratic it is) they have
seized the shining hour to write down various items
so hard that they won’t require much attention in
the dull times. And this co. has done something
more than that: it has unloaded a lot of its freehold
property during the boom. Follow these compari-
sons :

Thus through the sale of properties and the sale or
writing-down of machinery, plant and stock out of
undisclosed profit, liabilities in the past seven years
have been reduced by £73,321, while no less than
£49,470 has been added to the cash and book debts.
The position has therefore, to all appearances, been
very much improved indeed. What proportion of the
£81,647 of “cash and book debts” is
cash, The Bulletin doesn’t know; but if,
as it suspects, the book debts have been conserva-
tively valued, then the liquid assets represent just on
one-half of the paid capital, and properties (which
were written-down severely some years ago) stand
for a half of the balance. There are very few trad-
ing and manufacturing cos. with assets like that;
and the accounts, taking them as a whole, give the
impression that if the directors had wanted to do
it they could have made the year’s profit show up
much better than it does. The capital is £165,000
in 15s. shares.
The Australian Drug Co. (Sydney) :
Re.
June, Profits. Dividends. To Reserves, serves.
1911 ..£4766 5 p.c. =£3750 £lOl6 £10,252
1912 . . 3419 5 „ „ 3750 9,921
1913 .. 8572 10 „ „ 7500 1072 10,993
That is a bit more like what was expected. The
concern, which was making money, but was terribly
handicapped by want of capital, fell into some of
the strongest hands in Sydney. This was about three
years ago. Of course, they drove a good bargain.
For goodwill they paid nothing whatever. On the
contrary, the vendors had to pay brokerage on the
shares which Adam Forsyth (8.,P. and Co., Ltd.)
and his friends parcelled out among themselves, and
this commission had to be handed over to the co.
and put to reserves ; and the reserves already in the
business had to be left there. There was certainly
not more than one other co. formed or reformed
during the Sydney industrial boom on such favor-
able terms for the new shareholders. Results for the
first couple of years were, nevertheless, poor. In the
first 12 months the new capital couldn’t be employed to
advantage, and in the second the old factory was
congested while the new one was being built, and
much of the new capital was still earning nothing.
During the past year the co. seems to have got into
something like the stride it hopes to maintain ; and
the div. is consequently sprung suddenly to
10 p.c. But it isn’t like Adam Forsyth to pay a
10 p.c. div. unless there is very good evidence that
the business is capable, as a general thing,
of providing more than £1072 in excess of the
amount required to pay that dividend. Adam
Forsyth generally wants to see a better margin
than that. The present share capital is £75,000,
and there are still £25,000 of 5 p.c. debentures
outstanding. In addition there are current liabili-
ties totalling £24,569. The assets to represent this
£124,569 include £72,130 of stock (“valued at
cost or under and fully insured”), £47.691
of book debts, and £20,106 of freehold and plant.
Of course, nearly everything—profit included —-de-
pends on the valuation of the stock ; but, assuming
that that is right, the practicallv-new co. makes a
capital start on what is apparently a very profit-
able business, and, instead of paying for goodwill,
actually got a bonus for coming into it.
Quirk’s Lighting and Engineering,
Ltd. (SvdneyV— This co. was formed to take over
the Australian business of Quirk and Co., manufac-
turers of petrol or air-gas machines. The profits had
been large compared with the capital; but as the
latter was only £9500 even for the last year of the
private firm’s existence, and as 25,200 shares were
offered to the public while the vendor’s little lot was
24,800, it looked as though the co. was taking con-
siderable risks. Plenty of things can show a good
profit on £9500 which can’t carry £50,000. How-
ever, if the vendor demanded a large price, he was
prepared to make the bulk of it more or less contin-
gent on the success of the business. He asked no
less than £35,328 for goodwill and patent rights;
but payment was to be made as follows:—By 14,300
shares issued at once; by cash, £1028; by £2OOO
payable within five years, after the profits have been
“such as will enable the directors to pay a 10 p.c.
div.” : by 10.500 when the co. is “in a position to
pay 10 p.c. and a sum equal to 7i p.c. on the esti-
mated value of goodwill shall be provided in
duction of goodwill” ; and by 7500 out of the future
issue of reserved shares. In addition to this n av
ment for goodwill, the vendor has received £547"
for tangible assets. Apparently if the co. pays on N
one div. of 10 p.c. and has 7* p.c. of the book-value
of the goodwill left over, the vendor will be at once
entitled to the 10,500 shares mentioned above Also
if, getting short of cash, it issues its 25 000 re’
served shares, 7500 of them must be handed to the
vendor. In other words, another £7500 (in shares')
must then be paid for goodwill, though the issue of
more capital may be due not to the growing value
of the business but to its failure to pull throueh
without further capital. The vendor’s intentions
may have been good ; but, altogether, the form of
the prospectus was loose, and the plan of payment
adopted is open to serious objection. The position
at present is that the co. has a paid caDital nf
£26.900 (14,300 vendor’s shares fully paid and
25,200 shares paid to 10s.) ; and it owes £6878
On the other side it has plant, book debts and cash
amounting to £22,666. For their 10s. per share
the contributing shareholders have thus at present
6s. Id. in net tangible assets. So far the co
has done well. For the past year, after making
liberal allowances for depreciation, it shows a profit
of £4417, out of which it pays an 8 p.c. div -
£2152. But as soon as it does a little better and
pays 10 p.c it will have to pay out £2OOO in cash
and thereafter carry also another 10,500 of vendors’
shares; and if it grows a bit larger and issues
17,500 more shares it will have to hand the vendor
still another 7500 free shares. A business ought to
have excellent prospects to be worth all this. For-
tunately, there is a lot of energy behind it, and the
finances seem to be very carefully handled; but
the business has to a great extent to make itself
and when a business makes itself it seems hardly a
fair thing that it should pay away so much to other
people who only started to make it.

A dividend in two consecutive years—this is Afflu-
ence indeed! True, half the capital was written off
a few years ago, so the 24 p.c. is really only lj p.c.;
still, when a man has got into the habit of getting
nothing at all, even 1J p.c. is liable to be upsetting.
Unfortunately, though a div. is paid two years run-
ning, the profit shown for the past year is less than
it was in any year of the previous three. It is a dis-
mal business. Queensland grows just about the best
wool in the world, and there isn’t anything in good
tweeds except wool; yet in the last year for which
figures are available Queensland imported £176,000
of foreign-made woollens, and £6500 of foreign-
made flannels, and £35,000 of piece-goods “combed
or fluffed on one side to resemble flannel,” besides
£lO,OOO of miscellaneous rugs and such-like. And
probably nine out of 10 of Queensland’s Laborites who
wouldn’t handle a bunch of bananas at a wharf if it
had been handled somewhere else by a non-unionist
declared their union principles to that effect in pants
made by sweated foreign non-unionists; and though
the local Laborite insists on the Australian manufac-
turer paying fair wages for decent hours, he doesn’t
insist on the shutting-out of the foreigner's goods,
though he knows that the foreigner doesn’t pay fair
wages or work decent hours. Worse still, he buvs the
foreigner’s sweated goods, and thus does the little bit
he personally can do towards shutting up the local fac-
tory and starving the local factory-hand. And the fool-
capitalist votes for Joe Cook and then wonders why
he gets only 24 p.c. on half his capital, and nothing
at all on the other half. In the case of this co. the
capital left in the business ( £23,339) is inadequate,
and the shareholders have to lean up against the
bank for an overdraft of £17,479 ; but what can it
do ? There is no hope, in present circumstances, of
getting more capital. So the concern has to follow
a cheese-paring policy, and carrv insufficient stocks
and generally give an impression that it is in a
ricketty condition. And this means that it isn’t at-
tractive to labor of the best sort, for labor of the
best sort won’t go into a line of business which looks
liable to snuff out. The new Interstate Commission’s
•l°b (s to look at these matters from a purely com-
mercial point of view.. Obviouslv the present Pro-
tection isn t enough to attract either the necessary
caDital or the necessary labor to the business, or to
save even the capital that has been put in the busi-
ness; and the sooner it tells Parliament how much Pro-
tection is-necessary ther sooner the present species of re-
fined cruelty will end. Meantime the Laborite who de-
mands preference to unionists and buys pants in
which most likely not one unionist had a hand had
better consider what sort of a colossal humbug he is.

When the co. started the public held 17,000 7 p.c.
cumulative pref. shares and the people who had
been in the old proprietary co. took 5000 ordinaries
all £1 each. After the ordinaries had got 10
p.c., the prefs. were entitled to come in for a
further helping of profit, if there was any left. In
1911 it was arranged to make all shares of the same
denomination, and at the same time another 8000
were issued at a premium which brought in £BOO net.
Thus the capital is now £30,000. Being well-handled by
men who know how to watch costs, and being, more-
over, blessed with a fair tariff, the co. has been able
to show good results on this moderate capital; and
the jobghas been made all the easier by the fact that
the capital was all real. There wasn’t any goodwill
loading for tbe business to carry. The labor supply
seems to be the worst worry, the felt-hatters having
made a pretty close corporation of their craft, and
shut down too hard on apprentices, with the result
that there are not enough tradesmen coming along to
keep pace with the growth, or possible growth, of
the trade. However, as said before, the business is
bandied by men who know what figures mean, and
don’t attempt the impossible. The verv clean little
balance-sheet shows no liabilities beyond £1384 on
current trade accounts; on the other side, land,
buildings and Diant stand at £11.870, stock-in-trade
at £18,840, and book debts and cash at £7793.
Depreciation appears to be amply provided for,
which is another way of saying that profits are no
doubt really what they are said to be.

Manly Electric Supply Co. (N.S.W.)
was formed in Sydney in April, 1911, to take over
the business of the Manly Electric Light and Power
Works, Ltd. The prospectus did not disclose what
the profits (if any) of the vendor co. had been ; the
frequently-misleading practice was adopted of put-
ting forward estimates purporting to show what a
fine thing could be made if more money was spent on
plant. But, though profound silence was kept re-
garding the past profits (if any), the goodwill was
valued at £2488. With plant added, the cost to the
new co. was £10,500, payable £6500 in cash and
£4OOO in shares —-which didn't hint any too loudly
at the vendors’ faith in the thing they were selling.
The public was offered 11,500 shares, but only 8120
were taken, and £B3O is still unpaid on these.
Matters have gone this way:—
Writ- Deficit
ten (Goodwill,
April, Profits. Dividends. Oft. etc.).
1911 .. —.— £2927
1912 .. £1247 6 p.c. = £332* £4OO 2412
1913 .. 1370 7* „ „ 847 500 2389
shares only.
Evidently remorse or something of the sort impelled
the vendors, having taken very nearly the whole
ot the money subscribed by the public, to forego the
first year’s dividend. Of course no dividend at all
should have been paid. As a matter of fact, on such
skimpy applications no co. should have been formed.
As it was, the first year ended with an overdraft
of £2457. During the past year an attempt was
made to raise the wind by a debenture issue; £2400
was thus got in, and there are other debts (including
overdraft) of £2131. Yet another dividend is de-
clared ; but since it is probably impossible to pay it
in money, it is to be distributed in shares. Mean-
time the business has been seriously hampered, most
of the estimates with regard to the new plant having
gone wrong; yet the turnover has increased, and
there is room for a big further increase. But
whether the co. will pull through is another matter.
If it does, without a serious writing-down of share
capital, the management will deserve three cheers.
The debenture-holders are, of course, on a different
footing to shareholders. Total liabilities now are
£4531; and apart from goodwill and such-like
(rubbish, probably), there seems to be certainly over
£lO,OOO worth of assets. The shareholders’ best
hope of making their shares worth anything may
be to put up or get others to put up the necessary
capital on debentures (7 p.c.). That may save the
shares, and the debentures ought to be all right unless
the management is particularly rotten.
Stockton Borehole Collieries, Ltd.
(N.S.W.).: —
June, Revenue. Outgoings. Loss. Divs. Deficit.

This co. was registered at the beginning of July,
1909, to take over the property of the Borehole
Collieries, Ltd., which had been formed three years
previously to work “Black’s” colliery, theretofore in
the hands of the Sneddons, who still have a big say
in it. It started off with insufficient capital; there
are 60,000 shares of 10s. each, and 6s. of this was
regarded as paid up. In part payment £15,000 of
8 p.c. debentures were issued. Later on it became
necessary to increase this debt, and there is now
£22,815 outstanding; but the interest has been
reduced to 6 p.c. On top of this the co. had to
hire coal-waggons, and that is always a costly busi-
ness; so, though it hasn’t earned interest for its
shareholders, it has had to earn interest for other
people. Further, the Borehole seam hereabouts is
rather thin, and it took a lot of development to
work up to an output of even 500 tons a day at the
end of 1911; and this development is charged up
with the outgoings shown above. On the whole, then,
and especially considering its heap of labor troubles,
the co. hasn’t made a bad start; though until the
Northern men get out of their habit of throwing in
their tools on the smallest provocation, the person
who invests in Newcastle coal invests chiefly
in uncertainty and worry, with no pros-
pects of gaudy dividends to compensate. Apart
from the debenture debt mentioned above, the
Stockton Borehole’s liabilities are £2017, while the
more or less liquid assets (excluding stores) run to
about £2500. » * *
At the meeting of the Zinc Corporation
in London chairman Govett supplied some figures
which the annual report overlooked. Of the 85,000
tons of zinc concentrate produced during the year,
55.000 tons were delivered at the average spelter
price for the year (viz., £26 3s. 4d.), and 35,000
tons were credited with the arbitrary price of £23
for spelter. The figures don’t tally. The 55,000
tons is evidently a misprint for 50,000, as this is
the agreed tonnage on which payment was made at
the average price for the year. The excess produc-
tion above that amount isn’t finally paid for until
perhaps 12 months later, as the buyers have the
option of taking it on the average of either the six
months or the full 12 months after the close of the
year, according to their view of how the tricky
metal market is likely to jump. The buyers have
thus a strong trump card to play when the occasion
demands. As spelter is now down somewhere about
£2l, there is the chance that the 1913 average may
be less than £23; if so, the co. may need to take a
cut out of the current year’s profits to square the
deficiency on the 35,000 tons of zinc concentrate
reckoned in at £23. Last year metal prices were
very high, and the co. received £16,000 as surplus
from the preceding period. It sold its 36,108 tons
of leady concentrates at the solid figure ot £lt
15s. 10d. per ton of lead. These prices are, ot
course, not going to last for ever, and anyone who
bases a purchase on the 1912 results is likely to get
sore about it before he has finished.
Because the Broken Hill cos. are very reticent
about their selling contracts, it is difficult to find
out what a person should certainly want to know
before taking a plunge, and that something is wfia
is going to be the difference in the profit when metal*
are on their normal level. There was a bit of skir-
mishing around this prickly subject at the meeti g
referred to. Govett declared that with spelter at
£23 and lead at £l3, there would be just enough
to pay a 6d. dividend—after, of course, the modest
pref. shareholders have had their 20 P-?- P ,a * ~
extra £1 for each metal or £2 for either \
mean an extra Is. dividend. H. C. Hoover also
a confession that to make a profit of £150.0
co. required a combined price of £39 per t
lead and snelter. AVith spelter at £2l and leadl also
at £2l, the extra £3 in price would give another
£63,000 to the co.
t^ gh Sfu T lc,e , on ? ma - v have a Shot at the vexed
nenfrof thus ’ In deal,n & with a decent-grade lead con-
centrate one may calculate to get a full recovery of
„!,. le ? d anc [ silver; after that, he must deduct a
realisation charge of somewhere about 80s. or 85s.
per ton of concentrate. Unless the zinc in this con-
centrate is unusually high (above 8 p.c.), it may be
disregarded. Then as to the zinc concentrate. First
oi all, eight units of zinc are put into the buyers’
pocket, after which he grabs eight units of lead and
ve of silver. In the Zinc Corporation's zinc con-
centrates there is not 8 p.c. lead, so he grabs all that
there is of that metal. Then he charges something
to smelt and realise on the concentrate, the amount
depending on the price of the spelter. Evidently the
principle is that the higher the price the more the
producer can afford to part up. The charge ranges
from £5 to £6 per ton of concentrate, or a trifle
more if spelter is very high. After all these deduc-
tions and charges the buyer is prepared to pay for
about 95 p.c. of the remaining zinc, half of the re-
maining lead (if any) and half the remaining silver.
So it is not difficult to see what sort of a cut Aus-
tralia loses by foreign smelting. Taking the Zinc
Corporation chairman's figures as to metal prices,
and allowing (say) 85s. as the realisation charge on
the leady concentrate, the Zinc Corporation
would have received £330,000 from these
concentrates, estimating that silver (the price
of which was not mentioned) averaged 2s. 4d.
per oz. The zinc concentrate (with a smelting and
realisation charge of £6 per ton) would bring in
£292,000. These two total £622,000, which com-
pares with £644.000 shown in the accounts. But
the £644,000 includes the £16,000 surplus from
the preceding period, and also a credit for 6000
tons of zinc slime saved from re-treatment by the
liorwood process. The slime may be worth £1 to
£2 a ton. The calculation is, therefore, pretty
right. Then this much further can be calculated:
if metals were to average in the future the same as
in the last 10 years, the yearly net profit, after
depreciation and redemption, would be £93,000, as
against the £225,000 actually reported.
As to that redemption. For every ton of tailings
used up, ss. is put aside, and forms practically a
fund for the return of canital, although, as pointed
out the other day, the accounts don’t show that it
is earmarked for that purpose. Something like
£60,000 or £70,000 goes into this fund every year.
At the end of the last financial year (December 31)
the co.’s surplus of liquid assets over liabilities was
£224,900, out of which £67,412 was payable in
dividend. The co. had six years’ life in the dumps
and another two years or so in the tailings to be pro-
duced from the South Blocks mine, which the co.
owns. In six years, setting aside ss. a ton on the
dump tailings, the co. should accumulate, say,
£350,000. This, with the surplus on December 31,
after allowing for the dividend, would total about
£520,000 ; and the capital is £672,261. The
directors’ idea is to play with the fund and try to
bring it up to £572,261 by the end of 1918; in
fact, according to the official report of the meeting,
they want to pay back not only the pref.’s £246,000,
but also “the original 20s. per share on the 653,000
ordinary shares.” Of course nearly half the latter
capital was written off long ago, when things looked
very gloomy; and there are probably few of the
shareholders left who actually paid the 20s. It
would be far sounder to treat the fund as a sinking
fund and allow it to accumulate in solid investments.
But that is far too slow for the Govetts. So, hold-
ing out the bait of a “return” of 20s. per share on
ordinaries and prefs. alike, though the ordinaries are
now only 10s. shares, the managing directors are
going to regard themselves as “an ordinary invest-
ment trust, with the ordinary powers of investment
and of underwriting issues which are exercised by the
successful investment trusts.” And by the unsuccess-
ful ones, too, it may be added. For instance, the
start at the old Sunny Corner doesn’t seem any too
promising.

The capital was only £12,500, so the mine doesn’t
owe the shareholders anything. Last year the co.
had the usual water-deficiency troubles, and of
late it has had to shift a lot of overburden ; but the
water supply is now good, and as the ground just
ahead won’t be so troublesome, there should be some
more dividends. Liquid assets are already a few
hundreds ahead of liabilities.

Ivanhoe (W.A.) is rejoicing over its
apparent good fortune at 2720 ft. As stated here (B
24/4/’l3). the advent of graphitic slate knocked the
remnants of its main shoot to smithereens in the
then bottom level at 2570 ft. This slate had also
made its presence felt in the two levels above, but
le good patches of ore were nevertheless opened
up at those points. At 2570 ft. there was absolutely
no good ore. The opinion of Dr. Maclaren that the
skte would pass away in depth, and that values
would make again, had a cheering effect on t .
otherwise depressed management, and as 7ft. of 4/ .
or° has lust been exposed in the main crosscut at
2720 ft., it looks as if this opinion is going to prove
correct. # *

Westralia’s export of minerals other than
gold for the half-year ended June was valued at
,£ 112,265, or about double that for the same period
in 1912. Copper, tin, silver and silver-lead showed
substantial increases. Most of the tin sent away
came from Greenbushes, which has livened up a bit
lately as a result of suction dredging and sluicing.
Increased production at Whim Creek and elsewhere
helped to swell the copper returns, and a revival at
Northampton was responsible for the larger lead pro-
duction. The figures for the remaining half of the
year are expected to be better still.
Somebody must be a liar. In their recent
report to shareholders for the half-year ended May,
the directors of the Royal Oak, at Hancocks (W.A.),
stated that “if the State battery had been available
for regular crushings as required by the management,
the necessity for calling on shareholders to provide
further funds would have been obviated.” But an
official of the Mines Department says that no trace
can be found on the file in the Department of any
application by the Royal Oak for the use of the mill;
and as the mill has been idle a good deal of late
it seems certain that all the ore that the co. may
have been able to gouge out could easily have been
crushed.
Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has been dished
in regard to the Chaffinch lease. Some months ago
it induced the warden to recommend its application
for forfeiture; but the Minister has allowed the re-
gistered holder—a woman —to retain possession on
payment of a fine of £5O. An Adelaide syndicate
has been formed to do some boring on the property
on the off chance of picking up the continuation of
the Bullfinch main ore channel or something similar.
Marvel Loch (W.A.) has decided to take
The Bulletin’s advice and give its mine best. Low
returns, increased arsenical contents and a deluge
of water are the reasons for the decision. The
co. now proposes to go nap on the Comet option, in
which “several fine bodies of ore” are said to have
been found. Enough gold may be taken out to pay
for the property ; but as the shoots are more likely
to snuff out than go down, shareholders’ chances are
still uncertain.
Eastern capitalists are nibbling at por-
tions of Block 50 on the Hampton Plains (W.A.).
Several areas are being prospected in this locality,
and some look like turning out fairly well. One has
a big formation, from which a recent crushing aver-
aged 6jdwt. over the plates, with 4dwt. in the tail-
ings. Hamilton and other notable Italgoorlie mining
men are said to be favorably impressed with the pro-
spects of the field, but so far nothing good enough
for a big co. has been unearthed.
* * * *
Black Range West (W.A.), the Sydney-
owned “position” claim which always looked as
though it must hold something, is feeling better than
ever it did before in its long life of hard struggle.
What its present find is like can be judged best by
the fact that 930 z. of smelted gold has been cleaned
up from 1001 b. of specimens.
For putting up £150.000 of working
capital the London group, which had an option
over the Mount Oxide (Q. 1, was to get 275,000 shares,
and the present co.’s little lot was to be 200,000.
The Londoners have been squeezing, and apparently
they have scored to the extent of 25,000 shares. Any-
way, it is announced that a deal has been completed,
and that the new co. will be loaded with a capital
of £500,000. Fortunately the mine has been respond-
ing very well indeed to development -work, and the
money seems to be in the property.
In July Mount Morgan produced 9268 o!?.
of gold, which was more than the total output of
Charters Towers and Gympie (88260 z.). Charters
Towers treated 6807 tons for 56170 z., and Gympie
10,123 tons for 32090 z. The average value of
Towers stone was £3 Bs. 6d., and that from Gympie
only £1 6s. 7d. per ton. The Scottish Gympie
supplies more than half the tonnage at Gympie. Its
June return was 5488 tons for £5323. To try to
improve matters the Scottish intends to cyanide its
tailings; some steel vats have been purchased from
Mount Leyshon, and a tube mill has also been added
to the equipment. # .
One of the kind friends of the Long
Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.), writes that a loss was
made over its last crushing. Of course; and it must
go on until the Board gets more stopes open, lo
cheer the new co. on the road, specimen stone has
come into the stopes over 3225 ft. ; but a few patches
don’t tally up like the old persistent seams of gold
that brought wealth to the Pearsons. The co. ought
to use some of the State bonus in working south at
1000 ft. towards the old South mine.
Britannia United, Ballarat East, has got
its sinking done, and will soon be pushing out into
the slates to look for the patches that £ av e kept it
going for half a century. Meanwhile the old hands
quietly gather in the loose scrip, knowing that with
cheap' work and tribute parties, very low yields bring
in fair divs. , *

Prospecting over about a tenth of the cave area
has, it is alleged, exposed 100,000 tons of guano,
averaging in nitrogen p.c., in phosphoric acid
12 p.c. and in potash 2 p.c. The Queensland Gov-
ernment’s standard puts prices per unit at nitrogen
165., phosphoric acid 45., and potash 65., which gives
a total value of £6 10s. per ton. The mining will
be all pick work ; treatment is covered by drying and
grading and transport to the rail by a light tram-
way three-quarters of a mile long. The Common-
wealth guano bonus has not been considered in the
question of value.

of lib. of tin oxide per yard to an average depth
of 41ft. —a very payable proposition if the ground
is suitable for bucket dredging.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. 10d. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow ifiin. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these [2

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
inquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured.*

Victorian Government Railways. The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.* ,
