A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 11, 1913.
My Dear Abini, —
The new spring garb is fearful and won-
cJeriuJ. It follows no line in drawing, and
is flat where it ought to be round, and
bunched like a wrinkled jelly-bag where it
ought to lie as smooth as a glove. Colors
that make the African macaw look like a
tame understudy flaunt their gorgeous tints
with the wild disorder of the paints on a
youngster’s first palette. The sash is a for-
midable rite of the new frocks. It usually
hangs anywhere and falls down in front, or
over one hip, or in its old accustomed place,
the back breadth of the skirt.
Generally speaking, if the new models
tell the truth, the sash has nothing
whatever to do with the color scheme
of the parrot-tinted frock. It adds
another vivid dab and makes the
final scream in a gown which is bright yel-
low or scarlet for a start, inlaid with emer-
ald green (as something to go on with),
then hitched up with rosettes of violent
purple, and finished off with a few sunsets
or a new moon or two. The draping is
apparently the result of blind chance. Pre-
sumably some great man-dressmaker in
Vienna or Paris goes to a Futurist show.
He returns home and has an attack of
nightmare. He falls off his uneasy couch
on to a mad lobster that he has put there on
purpose. He rolls off the lobster, seizes the
quilt or the top sheet, flings it over the towel
rail, and drapes it to some likeness of the
worst Futurist picture that Paris can pro-
duce. Then he shoots it with a gun loaded
with paint. This is as near as I can get
to the origin of the spring fashions. Hogarth
missed a good deal by dying in 1764. Art
hadn’t properly begun to shriek then. It
was merely moaning in a minor key.
South Sydney is bounded on one side by
the smells of Botany. On the other angle it
has a lumpy hill to fence it off from Ken-
sington goff links. Anywhere else on the
horizon straggles Zetland, Waterloo and the
chimney stacks of Bedfern. About 80,000
bodies inhabit this thickly-populated area,
which is now the proud possessor of a new
and handsome hospital. Governor Strick-
land turned a gold key in an obliging lock
that opened something inside the building.
Joynton Smith, M.L.C., made several
speeches, and remarked as a preface to one,
that “no less a person” than Baronet
Cooper was present, at which the Baronet
winked facetiously at Mrs. Baronet. She
was clothed in all the pomp and circum-
stance of velvet brocade and waved a Reck-
itt’s blue plume on her handsome hat. Wil-
liam Cooper handed over his signature to
the amount of £5OO to the hospital on Satur-
day, and gave in the first place the three
acres of sandy sod on which it stands. He
is. a slender, elderly man with the typical
millionaire appearance of being just able to
keep the wolf from his palace gates. Ex-
Premier McGowen was not allowed to hand
out his favorite anecdote, “I gathered gee-
bungs on this spot as a boy” in peace. Sec-
retary Fred Flowers, M.L.C., interjected,
“You must have been web-footed then —
this place was a swamp.” Great anger
ensued, and the ex-Premier looked as if he
would demand his colleague’s head on a
dinner waggon or something. Lady Edoiine
drove out in her pet victoria and Avas
assisted to her place of duty on the
platform by one of the Cranbrook Kinder-
garten. Archdeacon Boyce attended the
ceremony in the usual gaiters and saucy
hat that go Avith his cure of souls. A few
hundred local inhabitants were present at
the fray. A bevy of small schoolgirls
squeaked like young mice in a tuneful wel-
come to his Ex.
The National Council of Women put on
its spectacles and best hats and sallied forth
to State Government House the other after-
noon. The purpose was to hold a meeting
to scan the reports of various overseas’ mem-
bers of the clan. The Strickland ladyship
was in the chair. Help Talbot and Col.
Campbell hovered discreetly on the horizon
and fled before the “Road to Ruin” and
“Worst Man” discussion was handed round.
Rose Scott sat at the official table, and
Mrs. R. McAnderson plied her fountain pen
as recording sec. As I have never yet been
able to unravel exactly _ what the National
Council achieves, I see its thousand resolu-
tions and innumerable minutes through a
dense fog. Mrs. Henry Dobson, who repre-
sented the Nat. Council at a recent parley
in Paris, fonvarded a voluminous re-
port, heavily embroidered with good works.
Temperance, hygiene, the importance of the
woman’s vote, and a general crusade against
microbes stud the account of the French
Congress. An osprey or two waved at this
meeting, and I noted that the gathering
didn’t pass a resolution exterminating the
wearers of this fascinating but murderous
Huff.
The committee of St. Vincent’s Hospital
Hall is backed up by a formidable array of
non. secs, and hon. treasurers. The president,
Mrs. Mayoress Cocks, holds the fort at
meetings which palpitate with new ideas.
Imagination reels before the inter-suburban
complications of 14 fancy sets. Hon. secs.
Mrs. O’Gorman Hughes, Mrs. Kenna and
Mrs. H. Kendall are officially supported by
non. treasurers Veronica Mort, Mrs. R.
Innes and Mrs. Bullmore. The handsome
Brennan girls (Edgecliff) are lending their
decorative appearances to the tableaux
which the R. E. O’Connor damsels are dedi-
cating to the galleryites. Mrs. Ronald
t orbes, who is built on delicate Watteau
lines herself, has stage-managed the Watteau
set which includes many scions of our old
nobility. A.D.C. Forbes forsakes his tartan
trews for the occasion, and will Watteau
round the wild grand chain. Mrs. Sep Levy
is going to dine and wine the Watteau set
on the evening of the ball, August 26.
St. Martha’s Home (Leichhardt) catches
the Young Idea (female persuasion) and
stuffs it with vital information and needle-
work. Martha, who was troubled about
many things, was not, I believe, the
lady after whom the island and whaling sta-
tion of Martha’s Vineyard was named. She
can hardly have been, for she is not men-
tioned as having whaled even once. But she
must have been the patron saint of lace-
making, for the girls who are trained in the
sunshiny home at Leichhardt turn out lace
as. gauzy as a butterfly’s wing or a poli-
tician’s promise. Last week, St. Martha had
a garden. f§te, and filled the hungry with
good things and sold the thirsty soft
drinks from innumerable stalls. Miss Strick-
land declared the fair to be well and truly
open, and the John Toohey dame (a buttress
of the Cause), Mrs. O’Gorman Hughes,
Mrs. Carmichael, Mrs. Lane Mullins, Mrs.
Porter and Mrs. Veech were amongst those
who also ran.
A few official personages were invited to
eat a scholarship dinner at the Tech. College
the other evening. The scholarship
girls are the bright particular stars of the
cookery department which is surrounded by
a firmament saucepans. Teacher in
charge, the bright-eyed cook lady A. M.
Monro, glides behind the schnapper a la
Marquise as oresiding angel. Minister Car-
michael, Under Sec. Board, Acting-Super-
intendent Nangle (Tech. College), and
others were bidden to the feast.
The Alliance Frangaise will mingle light
refreshments with an account of the Right
Way to handle irregular verbs, on August
Iff. Governor Strickland has given his vice-
r°yal word to be present at the ceremony,
which is the last of the present season. The
committee extends the glad hand to anyone
who. contributes 2s. 6d.. and is able to say
Oui. People who can’t say “Oui” are
barred, I believe, and told to'go out into
the wilderness and abide there for 40 days
while they acquire the essential mono-
syllable.
The-annual corroboree of the Kindergarten
Union will be held at State Government
House one afternoon next week. Lady
Edeline Strickland presides at this gathering.
Ihe business includes the usual heavyweight
items that garnish balance-sheets and £ s. d.
General Sec. Ellen Desailly is at present
chasing the uttermost farthing to its long
home in the audited account, and will sub-
mit same, without prejudice, to the assembly.
Mrs. A. C. Buchanan, of this town, and
three daughters, are still scampering through
foreign hat-shops. The two younger dam-
sels, however, have to temper French mil-
linery with Continental tongues, as they’re
booked for a boarding-school.
The Warwick Armstrong pair have been
poised on the wing in this city for a day
or two. Little Mrs. Warwick, in peri-
winkle blue the other morning, had the
latest remark in feathers waving on top of
her toque. The remark consisted of two
long, thin plumes standing straight up in
the air and rising about a foot above the
head. Some invisible support prevents this
frail structure from falling down dead, so
it shakes its phantom fronds like the ghost
of a note of exclamation. It’s the very
newest thing in millinery, but it has to be
handled with care.
A granddaughter of the late John Cecil
Read was married the other day to Henry
Horn, who belongs to a North Sydney family.
The bride’s father was the late Mr. Charlie
Read, whose male parent was Governor of
Darlinghurst Gaol for a good many years.
A large contingent of the Read relatives,
including William A"ero, of that ilk, assisted
at the ceremony.
The Oliver Osbornes, Mr. Jack McMaster,
the Frank Chisholms, of Goulburn, and the
Tindales, who are Armidale folks, are in
town as I pen this line. This sort of thing
cheers up the town considerably.
Various extensive properties in our best
suburbs are changing hands. Elystan, the
palatial mansion at Woollahra that used to
house the late James Marks and his kindly
wife (she has also crossed the River StyxL
has been sold during the past week. The
name of the buyer hasn’t floated this
way at the moment of gumming up the
present screed. The Spencer Bruntons have
been anchored at Elystan for a while. They
will move on to Glenyarrah presently, having
bought _ its picturesque chimney pots, its
walls, its terraced garden and all that is
within its iron railings from Alderman T.
H. Kelly.
Several local engagements are alleged to
tremble in the background at the present
crisis in Bulgarian blouses. A young man
who is something moderately tremendous in
the whisky trade (to put it another way, he
is a small giant in the business) is said
to have plighted his promise to a Society-
maiden in her early prime. A bachelor
pianist of considerable wrist power is also
suspected of an understanding with a well-
dowered damsel pending her parent’s ap-
proval. These events belong to the unreli-
able rumor species and can’t be more than
whispered in cold print.
Rose Bay Club goffers have drawn up a
new code. This is to restrain lady associ-
ates who are smacking the elusive ball with
raw inexperience from trampling down the
turf on the male golfers’ ground. A dark
suspicion that Woman is getting the whip
hand in these links has fallen like many
whales and avalanches on the committee of
ways and putts. Woman, in future, will be
firmly requested to keep her place—except
on special occasions—or tell her age.
The Petersham Choral Society, with S.
Vost Jansen in charge of the baton, is going
to produce “The Shop Girl.” J. C. William-
son Ltd., who hold the Australian rights,
offer no objection. August 16 is the open-
ing night.
Dora Ohlfsen will remove her picturesque
presence from this town on October 1,
weather and other unforeseen accidents per-
mitting. She has recently achieved a fine
medallion portrait of the Admirable King-
Hall, which is going abroad for its bronze
top coat. It catches a brilliant impression
in profile of this mariner’s horizon-searching
expression, a characteristic of most naval
big-wigs who command the lurching gait of
a few thousand tons of J. Bull’s ironmon-
gery. A study of the just-departed Admir-
alty lady has gone off in sections to be
bronzed in foreign parts. During the com-
ing winter in Rome the artist is going to
button herself up in her largest overall while
she prepares her wares for 1914 exhibitions
in Venice, Paris and London. She is a
great grand-daughter, by the way, of one
George Howe, who made his mark here in
1803. He was born in the West Indies, had
a few snacks of ink on the London Times,
then sailed for Sydney in 1800. Three
years or so later he published the first num-
ber of the Sydney Gazette and N.S.W.
Advertiser, at a net profit of £6O a year.
This was the princely salary with which the
Government of the day rewarded its first
official printer.
In spite of the groans of the 1913 house-
wife, Mrs. Hunter Street had to pay 2s. 9d.
before she called a pound of butter
her own in 1820. Bread was sd.
for a 21b. loaf. Tea was almost
priceless when shipping fell down in
green seas and wallowed into port a month
or two overdue. Howe, who died in 1821,
lived long enough to see the staff of life
ascend to sixpence per 21b. cottage loaf.
Still he got away from the evil that was to
come. In 1839 the Cornstalk housewife
paid for a 21b. loaf, and the ordinary
41b. loaf of commerce cost Is. lid. Butter
averaged half-a-crown per lb., and potatoes
were exactly twice their present price.
Cheese was Is. Id. per lb., and eggs about
2d. per egg. And in those joyous Freetrade
times a laborer acquired some ss. a day, and
general servants could be leased for £i4 per
annum. People lived then simplv by doing
without things.
The cable reports that several noted com-
posers, some well-known contactors, and a
few singers (with Caruso as a special note
of indignation), intend to form an anti-
chatter combine. Any person found guilty
of carrying on a conversation during the
trills of the operatic aviary will be removed
from the theatre. During the Butt-Rum-
ford and Nordica concerts here, the rustle of
programmes, and the cackle of the perpetual
talker went on without the slightest public
protest. A new species of usher, with the
chest measurement of a 6ft. policeman,
might be employed with enormous success.
He could also attend to the feet of those
persons who invariably kick the chairs in
front of them, by way of letting off their
superfluous animal spirits.
The Butt-Rumford team sang and fiddled
to a huge audience on Saturday night. The
Town Hall was full to the organ stops, and
the stately Clara had to continuously bob her
head to adorers on the dais, who had hardly
room to move their knees. The Butt voice
was in majestic tone-power, and the air was
thick with encores. Garbed in raven plum-
age, the mighty contralto kept her back hair
tidy with a few hundred-pounds -worth of
diamond bandeau. Her breathing apparatus
moved another decoration of the same gems
to fling out quivering blue lights from her
massive bosom. Alice Elgar’s heroic song,
‘The King’s Way,” and the classic “Crea-
tion’s Hymn,” were the solids of a fine mo
gramme. Gluck’s haunting ”Che faro” was
the only weak snot in the Butt warble the
other night. Mrs. Clara takes the legato
passages too roughly, and sends Orpheus
stumping through Hades on a wooden leg
whereas I imagine he glided amongst the
shades in velvet slippers, or went about shod
with air. Kennerley liumford repeated his
brilliant attacks on Alfred Hill’s vital Maori
lyric, “Waiata Poi,” and the torrential vocal
rapids from “The Barber of Seville.” Mrs
Butt’s elegant husband is singing up to his
best form since he quaffed Queensland’s sun-
shine. The tired little spots in his voice-box
noticed earlier in his present Australian
tour, have vanished. Solicitor Alfred Nathan,
who cultivates a melodious voice of his own!
ambled round to the artists’ den to shake
the Butt-Rumfords by the hand during the
smoke-oh interval.
Sydney’s Mayoress, Mrs. Cocks, has sent
forth cards to view Madge Titheradge at
afternoon-tea on August 18 The party
occur in the Mayoress’s official lair in the
Town Hall. If you miss the lift take the
marble stairs, or anything else you can put
your hands on, provided the municipal
Orderly Garrick isn’t looking. If you find he
has been looking and that trouble is about
to arise, the only thing you can safely take
is your leave.
Evelyn Beerbohm, who married the beau-
teous Elsie Anderson of this port, rises to
the surface with much applause as a lord-
ling in “The Butterfly on the Wheel.” Mrs.
Elsie and her Evelyn are located at an
Elizabeth Bay flat.
Now that the “Butterfly on the Wheel”
has settled down into her stride at the
Royal, the feminine spectator can dissect
the best parts of the dialogue. As com-
ponent parts of the truths that every woman
knows, the girlish friskiness of the Butterfly
becomes the choice wickedness of vicious
tongues. Madge Titheradge is uneven in the
torture scene, when Waller, as Robert Fyffe,
turns the legal screw, and she sheds her
emotion more copiously at later perform-
ances than in her first weep in this town.
Most Nice Girls, however, could bear a good
many of Mrs. Grundy’s spiteful asides with
fortitude, if they owned Madge’s Butterfly
wardrobe. The elfin night robe in which
she curls up her toes on the sofa is a mix-
ture of Oriental allurement and Western
discretion. Bacon and eggs desecrate the
breakfast scene in which the Butterfly tells
a few pale cream lies to her husband, in a
foam of white lace tied up at the waist with
a bow which is one of the seven deadly sins.
Proper feeling sends the reckless little flirt
to the Divorce Court in penitential black,
edged with the comfort of ermine. An
emotional white plume sticks straight out
behind the small black hat like the tail of a
moulting chicken. This decoration nearly
kills the pathos of Madge’s high-tension sobs.
It waggles like the plumage of the fowl just
mentioned when it totters on the back fence
before dropping into the next vard with its
beak full of maddened squawk.
The Women Painters’ show was torn open
by Mrs. Chief Justice Cullen the other noon,
r rancis Suttor, M.L.C., dropped a few pre-
liminary remarks, and the deed was done.
Mrs. Hedley Parsons dominates the oils
landscape section with a Turneresque
effect in sea and sky that floats round the
phantom shape of a ship in the offing. This
is indexed “Out of the Mists.” Only sec-
ond to this fine canvas is the same artist’s
Evening, Newcastle Harbor.” Violet
Teague’s “Boy with the Palette” is another
piece of brush work of unusual merit. Lilian
Chauvel, improved out of sight, with a large
splash of rose-tinted hill and water, “Break-
fast Creek” (Brisbane), is forgetting the
artificial style that marred her earlier daubs.
Ethel Stephens’ Bowl of Asters is a glowing
tribute to the Stephens’ way with still life.
Florence Rodway is half a staircase higher
than her everyday form in a clever picture
labelled Italian Girl.” Edith Cusack ex-
mbits her best work in a vital portrait study,
Giovanni Ferrago.” It is incredible but
true that the hand which painted this bold
and virile picture is responsible for a crude
canvas m lead grey, called “Evensong.” C.
Nevill-Smith shows a small study in sad-
eyed wallflowers and a portrait of her
brother, musician Nevill-Smith, of Maori-
land. Gladys Owen is dipping her brush in
gold and sunset shades to paint the lazy
clouds of “Early Morning.”
A small fire raged with great fury at the
Women Painters’ show for about three min-
utes on Friday afternoon. The cause, which
is generally diagnosed as the same in all
these flares, was a methylated-spirit lamp,
which spat out blazes like a small Etna.
Ruby Winckler’s decorative panel had the
misfortune to have nothing but a rag of
d £ a P er y between its back and a fierce tongue
of flame. A yawning hole in its middle
was its testimony to the tragic lamp. The
Fire Brigade savers stamped in after the
event. Their disgusted expressions, when
the inevitable female explanation, “Just go-
ing to boil the kettle for tea,” leaked out of
an agitated eye-witness, was a study in
male scorn framed in shining brass helmets.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

THE CALLING VOICE
With Eight Illustrations by Lionel Lindsay.
A Story of the Australian
By E. F. CHRISTIE.
PRICE, 1/-; POST FREE, 1/
Bush.
The power of the calling voice of the Bush is splendidly illustrated in this book, where a young
fanner marries a city girl and takes her to live outback ; but, although he provides her with every-
thing she can wish, her desire for “society” induces him to try residence in the Big Smoke. An
endless round of hollow pleasures soon satiates them, however, and the call of the Bush becomes so
insistent that they succumb, and finally find happiness in the country. .
This book is' full of humor, and some of the society people introduced are worth their weight in
gold to our readers. Indeed, their antics in the country turn the laugh against the town so completely
that, for once, the rustic scores. .

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 11, 1913.
My Dear Yaerie, —
The Glaciarium carnivalled last week, and
G.-G. Denman stayed from beginning to end,
and trotted round complimenting nice girls
on the success of the various sets. He also
bestowmd some flowers .on instructress Miss
Fischer. The McEvoys, Frank Maddens,
O’Haras, Cleggs and Pinschofs all helped
to make pretty pictures. Lesly Madden was
Queen of the Butterflies, in the Butterfly
Pantomime. She skated primly, waving large
butterfly wings, and (in pantomime) re-
ceived the Fairy Prince and presented him
with an illuminated address. It was all
terribly alluring, and the John Madden dame
watched with great satisfaction. The mothers
were packed into special seats, where
they chatted and gloated over their various
darlings.
When the Homoeopathic Hospital was
raising the wind to help build a surgical
ward for its children, the Children’s Hospi-
tal was proudly opening its completed pav-
ilion, which contains the Edward Wilson
ward, the Frederic Poolman w T ard (mostly
built with the money of these two dead bene-
factors), and the Ann Higgins operating
theatre. The pavilion has balconies and
verandahs, the floors of which are on a level
with the wards, so that the beautifully bal-
anced cots can be -wheeled out into the
a.ir at any time without a moment’s discom-
fit to the little patients. G.-G. , Denman
brought his two children along to help with
the opening. The president of the committee
(Mrs. Turnbull) later on conducted the
assembly to tea. Mr. Fanning, the Edward
Wilson trustee, joined forces with dozens of
other people who had given substantial sums
and helped in other ways. Amongst them
were Mrs. J. A. Levey and Mrs. Sumner.
Everything to do with the Pierrot Ball,
in aid of the Homoeopathic Hospital, was
consistently in the picture. Ticketed pat-
rons w r ere allowed to look on from
the balcony; but no ordinary human being
who was not properly pom-pomed and
ruffled was allowed on the floor. The only
exceptions to the rule were a few hostesses
and the Excellency in attendance. Thou-
sands of big black paper balls hung from
the roof, and the lights were enclosed in
square orange silk lanterns. Some discreet
Pierrot-Pierrette washing hung on gilt lines
decorated with gilt pegs; it consisted of
long black stockings and short white socks,
all of which danced lightly when the
draught swayed them. The Gaud Mayoress
was sick and unable to attend for the hand-
shaking ; but a very richly-jetted, black-
satined Madden dame acted in her stead,
and received the G.-G., who seemed to be
twinkling cheerfully at the unusualness of
the charity spree. The quaint dress suited
most of the Best maidens.
Dr. Sehlesenger’s daughter Dorothy led
the Pantaloon Pierrots, who wore black,
with green crescents and ruffles. Jennie
Brenan’s many sisters made up an English
Pierrot set. and Miss Hyams’ following was
French. Thirza McLachlan’s team was
powdered and otherwise black and white.
Mrs. Heymanson put a dozen pale blue and
black frockers into the picture. All hands
carried sticks with gold and silver bladders,
and even the most sedate ventured to be
playful w’ith them. Theatrical folk arrived
about midnight and infused some fresh
spirit into the revel. Five o’clock in the
morning saw the end of Folly, and, unless
you lived close by, you got home just in time
for a bath and breakfast.
The Co-operative Craftworkers—a severely
practical band —have started business behind
a large plateglass window in Centreway.
Locally-made rugs hang about in a care-
lessly luxurious manner reminiscent of Asia,
and some examples of the pottery that the
Finlay brothers design, mould and bake, are
beautiful incidents in the decorative scheme.
Floor coverings, hats and cushions, all made
out of raffia, are also to be seen —and pur-
chased. Finally, artists are sending in real
pictures, constructed entirely of paint, can-
vas and imagination. The place is to be
run on business lines, and it is hoped to
make it a good and brisk market for local
artistic work. Those in authority encourage
the various workers to try and do the sort
of stuff that sells. Even misguided doers
of fancy work are given a chance.
All the country people hurried out to Caul-
field, on Saturday. There ' were Mani-
folds, from the Western District; Chirnsides,
from Werribee; Grices, from Mornington;
and Robertsons, from beyond Ballarat —all
of them with swift horses to watch and
shout for. Both G.-G. and G. attended —
and the lucky G.-G. drew the astrachan coat
for the day. A large and fashionable con-
gregation of aides attended them, and helped
to fill the vice-regal coop while the silken
jackets were glimmering round the course.
They looked so undistinguished that they had
almost escaped from the course before the
band noticed their absence and blew some
Anthem after them. Sporting medico O’Hara
wore the biggest check motor-coat on record,
and looked like two of himself. Mrs.
Pursey Chirnside looked blue but haughty
in white cloth. Sister Miss Fenner wrapped
herself sensibly in the family ’possum rug
cut into long strips. Norman Fenner
brought his fair wife along, wrapped in black
furs. Mrs. _ Alawa Buchanan held a bet-
ting book tightly and sported a pheasant’s
feather on end in her tam-o’-shanter. Some
women towed three-foot lengths of feather
through the wind. The Millers clanned to-
gether with the Curries and lunched cosily.
Russell Clarke, dowm from Rupertswood,
had his Tasmanian wfife in tow. The wind
made everlasting fun of the newest hat idea,
which consists of a flower, or a bow, or a
long stem standing straight up. Such things
needed wires to keep them upright on Satur-
day. Victor Hood, who is still visiting, kept
his glasses levelled at the galloping. The
vice-regal enthusiasm for the running horse
is drawing the Sex to haunt meetings that
used to be ignored by all dressy women. But
then it is easy to popularise horse-racing,
anyhow. Some poor, misguided women, de-
ceived by a mid-day burst of sunshine, went
out in spring frocks and, thus lightly armed
against pneumonia and doctor’s bills, met
the chill blasts of Caulfield heath. A visit-
ing Englishwoman was heard to moan for a
beautiful set of furs left in moth-safe storage
on the other side of the world. Someone —
the usual, bothersome “Someone” who knows
everything—told her that people never wore
furs in Australia.
Womenkind must have suffered badly from
damp feet on Bullawarra’s Steeplechase day.
Fur coats were associated w r ith chattering
teeth, bluey-purple faces, and gossamer-clad
ankles. Everyone shivered. It didn’t seem
to matter how heavily a woman was furred —-
the thin shoes and hosiery served to let in
the cold and make her as wretched as the
rueful visions of spring, with V’s exposing
their poor chests. A few tulle hats were
noticed. They looked about as appropriate
amongst the furs as a golf outfit in a ball-
room. One of our newest matrons flashed
late upon the scene in a paralysing Paris
creation, or mistake. Her furs did their best
to screen things from the gaze of the curious,
but the outfit from Paris was the sort of
thing that defied half measures or steps.
The lady wearer limited her stride, but, all
the same, everybody who looked could see
how far her skirt was slit up. These things
should be worn courageously, or not at all.
At a recent charity show some Personage
was booked to sit amongst the judges—all
of them social dames. Now the story goes
that, having failed to settle by friendly dis-
cussion which dame should sit at his illus-
trious elbow, the aforesaid females first of
all lured one of their number from the room
with a telephone call, and then drew lots!
As for the rumor that the first prizewinner
subsequently sold her seat by auction —
there is no truth in it. Anyhow, there doesn’t
need to be. The story is good enough with-
out it.
The Arts and Crafts of Victoria, which
institution was boosted by the domesticated
Carmichael lady, and patted on the back of
the blouse by successive vice-regal dames,
is going into new quarters at the Audi-
torium. Very soon, Governor Fuller, fresh
from the snow slopes of Buffalo, will in-
augurate the new venture, which will wear
the industrious sign, “Craft Depdt.”
The Women of Melbourne (that is to say,
the representatives of a dozen august club-
ettes) stood blinking on the steps of the
Auditorium, while a savage east wind threw
dust and road grit at them. But they hung
on bravely, for Nordica was nearly due. The
star had just endured the train journey from
Sydney and was suffering a few of the un-
pleasant after-effects of vaccination; but,
despite all this, she arrived becomingly
flushed, wrapped in a creamy-white ermine
coat, and underneath an osprey curved like
a cockatoo’s crest. This vision was straight-
way closed in upon by the swarm of women,
and enough fluttered after the first honey-
bringers to fill the big Auditorium. Then
someone in the dim distance smothered the
beaming visitor with flowers, and made a
speech which was full of blossoms and frag-
rant sprigs of English. Meanwhile a great
moan of admiration went up from the lis-
teners and lookers. “Isn’t she lovely!” went
round the hall like a wandering breeze. The
compliment was really won by Nordica’s
clothes, for only a little of her face was
visible. The singer, in replying, spoke on
feminine matters. She is a big-hearted
worker for the Sex’s comfort and well-being
—and thereby hangs a lot of her popularity.
At the same time she isn’t exactly a Pank-
hurst.
Nordica’s concert, on Wednesday last
week, was beautifully padded in the musical
sense. Tenor Dufault, at whose feet Mel-
bourne was ready to fall, having met and
worshipped him before, easily reconquered
the place with his first song. Violinist Hold-
ing, of the scarlet lips, has the dash of fire
in his bow that brings success, and he tri-
umphed in his turn. Pianist Simmons, who
adjusts his very round eyeglasses, flings out
his coat-tails from his plump form, and
tickles the piano persuasively, won all the
way. His are the sort of accompaniments
the" average singer yearns for and dreams of.
So Nordica, the Supreme Frocker, shone re-
splendently in a very melodious setting. The
much-jewelled star made her glittering en-
trance through the red plush curtains that
big Clara Butt almost demolished. She
wore a white dress cascaded with crystal
waves—or waves of crystal. If the under-
skirt had been sand-color, she would have
looked like a bay beach on a brilliant sum-
mer day. Outsize diamonds flashed in her
ears, and an emerald as big as a watch
glowed in her hair, while a bloated pearl
partly obliterated one finger. Half a peck
of diamonds, distributed round, dazzled the
beholder, and a curv'-i osprey boomeranged
out of her hair. After her first song, ushers
delivered scarlet and white blossom at her
feet- Some patriotic lmll-decorators had put
blue-gum bi.-.o- :es on the screen behind her.
There was some young spring foliage on
them —little fat, round leaves that outlined
themselves against the white wall and sent
refreshing breaths of gum scent stealing
through the hall. Seven Government Housers
sat and listened in an attentive semi-circle
on the balcony. G.-G. Denman, who headed
the party, was attended by Help Barttelot,
Secretary Guise and Chamberlain Nevill, all
of whom were lapelled in blue. The Bart-
telot dame sat with Mrs. Guise, who wore
purple- Denman looked bored —or perhap •-
it was his dreamy concert expression. A
dark-haired matron of some social import-
ance wore a blood-red feather in her hair.
Hugh Buckler, of Sydney’s Little Theatre,
had one wrist bound up, and his fair wife
alongside nestled snugly in a fur coat. Soci-
ety folk were fairly plentiful in the balcony
and body of the hall, but there was room for
more. Lots of people—many of them irrita-
bly nursing sore arms—hate to expend half-
guineas in the middle of the week. Society,
it appears, must have Saturday to show off.
A year or two ago, the cohorts of the rich
threatened to appropriate Friday night for
their show off, but, finding that the houses
were not always crowded on a Friday, they
changed their minds.
Nordica was audaciously gowned in cerise
and scarfed with purple for her Saturday
night concert. She “blossomed in purple and
red,” as Tennyson puts it, and mostly sang
as sweet as pie, in a ’Murkan way of speak-
ing. But when she came to “Angels Ever
Bright and Fair,” the prima donna’s diction
suddenly degenerated into Yankee cockney,
and the charm of the voice fell on irritated
ears. Not once, but several times, her appeal
to the angels was “Tyke, O, tyke me to thy
care,” until one began to think of dog shows
and the various kinds of tykes one has met.
Kaiser Willy, of Germany, has a favorite
scent, and, ’tis said, insists on the imperial
box being perfumed with it whenever he
visits the theatre. The representative of the
odor is now threatening to sprinkle Her
Majesty’s with it, for the first night of the
Quinlan Opera Co. A distinctive imperial
scent is rather a good idea. But it has its
drawbacks. The bomb-thrower has got a
nose as well as the loyalist.
Yolinin, the high-marking dancer who
enthuses football followers, is pictured in
the latest-arrived London Graphic dancing
with Lydia Kyasht, a beautiful Russian of
renown. Volinin is honorably mentioned as
a new man who had caught on and was
pretty nearly worthy of the exalted com-
pany of Kyasht. The paper’s respect for
Kyasht is almost overwhelming.
Anonymous letters are the ruling curse
just now in leisured circles. Tearful threats
of sooling on detectives are broadcasted by
the distracted victims ; but nothing is heard
to fall. Matrons who are up to their eyes
in charity schemes regard all but their most
trusted friends with the cold eye of sus-
picion. The latest disturbing missive was
received by a dame of great eminence. It
was written on the notepaper of a women’s
club and was unsigned, of course (or where
would the anonymity come in?). It accused
the dame’s daushter and another lady of
bazaar kleptomania, and when it was passed
round amongst the Paris hats, shrill cries
of indignation ascended in gratifying quan-
tities.
Jeff Stuart, one of the most popular Naval
Depot boys and a dancer of the “heavenly”
grade, has had a transfer. Melbourne girls
are looking alarmed and asking if there is
any danger of him ever being transferred to
another State. These are the sort of
national sacrifices that Society (capital,
please!) looks forward to with shudders.
There is a matrimonial boom in St. Kilda.
The latest maid to wear the ring is Ruby
Kennella, daughter of the John Dixons, of
the Esplanade. Her fianc4 is Rupert Min-
nett, of North Sydney.
There wms a duel, or something of the sort,
in a cafe, the other night. One of the two
youths involved is tall, while the other is
about up to his shoulder. The skyscraper
one played a practical joke on the little
one; and the little one, instead of taking it
calmly, bounded in wrath and hurled his
clenched fist at the joker’s nose. A scene of
unparalleled fury and devastation followed,
food and eating implements falling all over
the place. The little man triumphed more
or less, but the proprietor was the real
winner. Both contestants had to pay him
for the damage done.
Jack Manifold, son of the Willie Mani-
folds, of the numerous Camperdown clan,
was married last week to Barbara Grey-
Smith. Ivy Clarke, daughter of the late
Janet, helped the Grc.v-Smith sisters to
bridesmaid the and a small Manifold
child carried the bride’s train. The feath-
ered legions of Society were kept from roost-
ing on the ceremony by strategy. The wed-
ding was performed in the secluded
chapel of the Church of England
Grammar School, of which place the
bridegroom is an old boy, and the
bride’s father a still older boy. Jack’s father
has bought him a new place, which is handy
to the parental palace in the Camperdown
district, and there he will go on the land, as
all the Manifolds have done since the keen-
eyed great-grandfather spied out the fertile
corner of a then trackless wilderness.
Marian Manifold, daughter of squire Ches-
ter, looked on cousin Jack’s marriage as a
sort of dress rehearsal for her own, which
will excite the clan very shortly. Chester
bought a property from brother Eddy to give
to his daughter. The place includes a fine
old stone house, and the proud papa is having
it “done up” and generally renovated in readi-
ness for the bride. Fiancg Adams was for-
merly a mariner and ploughed nothing but
the angry deep. But latterly he has been
jackerooing, under specially favorable cir-
cumstances, and “learning the land,” i n
order that he may be worthy of his Manifold
bride.
Percy Duffus, of Dalgety’s, died last week
aged 33. He was essentially a man’s man
and was, therefore, admired by a host of nice
girls. He was an artist to the finger tips
and, who i not worrying over the day’s wool
tallies, was ever ready to work wonders with
a piano. It was seldom that the talented
amateur could be held down to a set per-
formance ; but there are many friends who
will never hear the music of “Bohbme” or
“Butterfly” without conjuring up delightful
memories of the good fellow and fine musi-
cian who is gone.
__ The new Professor of History at Melb.
Uni. has a wife who owns a famous mother.
Mrs. Ernest Scott was of old Miss Besanfi
daughter of Mrs. Annie, writer and lecturer’
thinker and theosophist. Ernest and his
wife came to Australia in i 892 for the good
of the then-young husband’s health. He
reckoned on staying two years, and proposed
to fill in that time on the Herald. His ill-
health has gone, but he hasn’t.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Appendreena Dramatic Club staged “The
Liars” at the Walkerville Hall on Saturday night.
Mrs. Geoffrey Burgoyne (better known to Club mem-
bers as Miss Lovie Oldham) and Mr. Latter played
Lady Jessica Nepean and Christopher Deering re-
spectively ; and did well, like the old stage-hands
they are. Mrs. Burgoyne swathed her slim height
in some graceful frocks, and Miss Ward and Miss
Robin were others who dressed like artists. Mr.
Clement May made Gilbert Nepean a quite impos-
sibly unpleasant husband, and Mr. Reg Sabine was
also extremely offensive. The cheerful Mr. Talbot
Smith provided the humor of the evening, and ex
cellent and likewise articulate humor it was.
Amongst those “in front” were the David Wilkies,
Mr. and Mrs. Morice and Miss de Lissa, Miss Coles!
Mrs. Belt and the Fred Levis, Dr. and Miss Plum-
mer, and Mrs. Angas Johnson.
Miss Williams had a score or so of her friends to
tea at “the Quack” —I mean the Queen Adelaide
Club on Monday. By the way, the Club is enlarging
itself. At present there are days when the dining
room proves all too small, and hungry members are
sent empty away—and as you doubtless know, my
dear, an empty member is one of the most passion-
filled objects on earth.
The Royal British Nurses’ Association is to benefit
by a ball at the Town Hall on August 19. The
Governor and Lady Bosanquet, a naval band and a
naval guard of honor are among the threatened
attractions.
Pianist Elsie Hall is still instructing small Prin-
cess Mary in the art of scales and exercises. She
fills in her spare time by playing at concerts and
the haughtier sort of At Homes.
Miss Eva Bakewell and Miss Joyce Waterhouse
have left for a week’s ski-ing and bob-sleighing at
Kosciusko.
Mount Braecken, the ill-fated Hays’ house at Vic-
tor Harbor, is to be transformed into a swagger
private hotel. It’s an imposing-looking place,
perched on the hillside overlooking the ocean, and
was gutted by fire just before Mrs. Hay and her
daughter sailed in the Waratah. Since then it has
been deserted, save for a faithful peacock, which
will now probably remain to be irritated by flippant
and irreverent boarders.
Mrs. Mitchell, who died at Fitzroy on Sunday,
was the youngest daughter of Mr. and Mrs. R. Barr
Smith. She married Professor Mitchell some years
ago, and leaves two children.
The moan of the disillusionised was plainly audible
at the Adelaide Police Court last week. A youth
went bail for a strange young woman. She has since
faded gently away, and on Saturday the amount was
estreated. The value of the experience which the
trusting lad thus gained was £lO.
Mrs. Gregerson gave a small dance at Hindmarsh
to welcome home Mr. D. Evans, who has been in
Queensland for some time. Wattle blossom and
asparagus fern bedizened the hall, and supper was
served on an almond-blossom decked table. Amongst
the dancers were Dr. and Mrs. Evans, Mrs. Lloyd,
Mrs. Arthur Coles, Miss Coles. Miss Jean Wigg, the
George Gunsorns and Miss Pauline Schomburgk.
The big Victorian gaol at Pentridge has
got another advertisement, one that justi-
fies a householder living anywhere near
Pentridge in buying a large, vicious dog.
Some young Victorians were playing foot-
ball near the gaol recently, and one of them
kicked the bladder over the wall. It hap-
pened to be Saturday afternoon, when all
prisoners are locked up, and there are no
warders about, so there was nothing for it
but for the youth to clamber over into the
enclosure. He got the ball and kicked it into
the street again, and then climbed back.
No one gives the usual emphatic denial;
instead, the excuse offered is that, whenever
there are prisoners about, that wall is alive
with warders and other deadly weapons.
Still, even that isn’t too comforting; for if
anyone can clamber over the fence on Satur-
day afternoon, and then jump back again,
without being seen. Bill Sikes’s chance of
escape seems pretty good—so far, at any
rate, as the last obstacle is concerned.
From “Rosna” in London :
Just wedded in this village, Esther McCulloch,
grand-daughter of the late John Wagner, of Mel-
bourne, and Baron Albert Sadoine, of Brussels. The
ceremony occurred with undue flourish at the
Bavarian Royal Chapel, in Warwick-street, the bride
being upheld by her sister and a Fairbairn damsel,
while the bridegroom was supported in his hour of
joy bv a brother baron. The list of congratulators
included Melba and the Grey-Egerton dame, some
Bucklands and Metcalfes, and Count This and
Baroness von T’other. The wedding presents hav£
gone on to Australia, and the pair will follow theip;
in September, sailing thence to China, where thait
vague thing known as an appointment is waiting for
the bridegroom.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Is a busy
Burglar too.
While the bear
Holds up the light,
Chunder makes
The sharp steel bite
Through the safe
That guards the gold
(“Filthy lucre”
1 am told).
Chunder's conscience
Does not hurt;
He is only
Stealing dirt.


POLITICAL
POINTS
Speaker Morton and Ex-Speaker Willis
handed a joint opinion to C. G. Wade (leader
of the Opposition) last week in N. S. Wales
Assembly that must have stung like a boil-
ing adder. Willis, referring to the present
of £2OOO which was subscribed in 1910 “as
the price of Wade’s allegiance to the capital-
istic class,” said he had read that it was pro-
posed to send the hat round again. “This
mercenary.” he exclaimed, “allowing the hat
to go round.” Whereupon the faithful Fitz-
patrick asked if “this person” were entitled
to use offensive terms towards an hon. mem-
ber. Speaker Morton told Fitzpatrick (1)
that he must not refer to Willis as “this
person” ; (2) that Willis was “perfectly in
order.” Which would appear to show that
while it is officially considered bad taste to
call Willis a “person,” it is good enough for
Wade to be called a “mercenary.” Verily,
Henry is getting some of his own back.
Premier Jack Scaddan’s one hope of con-
tinuing to hold power in Westralia is that
the Liberals and so-called Liberals will
quarrel among themselves. Colebatch, an
ambitious young member —he who, as a
pressman, was once chased out of the re-
porters’ gallery—wants to oust Wilson from
the Opposition Leadership, and Wilson natu-
rally doesn’t want to be ousted. Then so
that some places other than Perth may
scratch out a little public money, a Country
party is incubating, which means another
probable split in the Liberal camp. If
Scaddan plays his cards well he may be
able to go on borrowing money and doing
other foolish things for a considerable time
after next election.

“B.”: Frederick Page, M.L.A. (N.S.W.),
the lanky Laborite who represents Botany,
has been scapegoated for his opposition to the
recent Referenda of the Australian Labor
party by losing the nomination for his old
seat at the State elections. Which is right
enough as far as it goes, but when it is
limbered up with the treatment handed out
to Premier Holman, who also deserted the
cause, the party appears ridiculous.
The visions of “Henry Ilorsecollar” :
The curse of the politics of south-eastern Europe,
where the war has been, is that too many nations
have possessed the country at one time or another, and
too many of these nations aren’t dead. The Greeks
and the Macedonians (who were Greeks of a kind)
owned this region early. The Romans stole it, and
it was ruled from Rome, wherefore the present
Italian, because he owns Rome to-day, thinks that he
has some dizzy sort of claim on the place. It be-
came Greek again when the seat of Roman empire
was shifted to Constantinople, and it took to owning
Rome; but the modern Greek (who is now largely
Goth and miscellaneous) and the modern Macedonian
(who is very hard to define) have ceased to regard
themselves as one people, and they put in separate
claims. Italy has at least three claims apart from
the old Roman ownership: (1) that the Roumanians,
who occupy part of the country, are alleged to be
the descendants of Latin settlers; (2) that Venice,
which is now part of Italy, owned a considerable
area of Greece for a time, as well as a bit of terri-
tory further north ; (3) that there was a temporary
Lombard Kingdom which occupied a big stretch of
the country, and Lombardy is now part of Italy.
France has a sort of claim, if it liked to press the
matter, because it once owned a great part of
Greece, and governed it well while the show lasted.
The Bulgarians got nearly the whole blessed place.
So did the Servians. They both reckon that their
ancient empire should be restored to them, on which
point the Greeks and Macedonians (at least they are
called Greeks and Macedonians) frankly differ from
them—at the point of the gun. Austria has a sort
of claim as the heir of the holy Roman Empire^ —
provided the new German Empire isn’t the real heir.
The Turks claim on the ground of conquest, but it
is reckoned that they have been disqualified through
inabilitv to hold their conquests. Apparently what
the place requires is a capable new master with no
very strong local traditions —someone who can start
fresh. Seeing that the Normans came, in part at
least, from Norway, and a piratical sort of Norman
King who ran a sort of pirate kingdom in southern
Italv was once a sort of boss in Athens, perhaps
Norway had better take over the whole insane
caboodle and use it as a fishing station.
There is peace again in the south-east of
Europe, save where Abdul Fez, the
Turk, keeps up a flicker of warfare north of
Adrianople. The smaller wild cats have.all
signed a treaty of peace, and the only reason
for supposing that they won’t depart from
it next week is that they are very tired and
ruined cats, with their tails in splints, and
it will be years before the fur of three of
them grows properly again. Now £he Great
Powers have only a few casual little jobs
left on their hands. They have to suppress
Abdul Fez, the Turk, without falling
out among themselves through mutual sus-
picion. They have to organise the new
kingdom of Albania, and to find a reliable
king for it (the case calls for a really tre-
mendous sort of king, yet he will have to
live in a mean town, and his salary, until
things look up, will be about 2d., paid
irregularly). They have to prevent the
score of shrieky little tribes within the
Albanian borders falling out among them-
selves, and to prevent them falling out with
their neighbors, and their neighbors falling
out with them. And then, provided Russia
doesn’t happen too. conspicuously over the
border into Armenia, by way of bringing
pressure to bear on Abdul Fez, and
Austria doesn’t begin expanding too promi-
nently in the new kingdom in the interests
of peace or something, and Italy doesn’t
get too mad over its dream that it ought to
recover the bit of territory in the Balkan
peninsula that used to belong to Venice, and
Britain can get out of its habit of stealing
Turkish land in a friendly way whenever
the Turk is “up against it,” everything in
the garden will be heavenlv. On the other
hand, there is still a nossibilitv that every-
thing in the garden will be hell.
The contrast afforded by the contemporary
sittings of the Maoriland Parliament and
the “Unity” Labor conference is anything
but helpful to up-
holders of the Party
Government curse.
Attended by 400
delegates, the confer-
ence concluded its
business in nine days,
while Parliament,
after sitting longer
than that, had not
passed or even dis-
cussed a single mea-
sure. Charges of
spoils to the victors
and much delving in
old Hansards is all
that Maoriland has
got during the first
fortnight of the
present session for
the £1 per day which
it pays its members.
From a N.S.W. pressman who had to sit
out the Vic. no-confidence debate: —
The debate manufactured by the Argus and put
on the market by McLeod brought out three things—
the resourcefulness of Premier Watt, the weakness of
his followers, and the smallness of the Labor party,
mentally and numerically. The dark, clean shaven
Watt towers over everybody else, and he knows it. He
has a fine command of language—vitriolic, persua-
sive and denunciatory—and reminds one of Holman,
of the Ma State, though his utterances are more
vigorous. When this has been said, praise has been
exhausted, for the Victorian Liberals have no other
tall poppy in their garden, while the Labor party
has none “to equal Griffith, Trefle, or even Edden. It
looks now, after McLeod’s crab-like crawl-out, that
Watt will run the Victorian Parliament indifferent
as to whether he satisfies the Argus or not, despite
the paper’s ardent desire to fill the job of king-
maker, once occupied by the Age.
IMPENDING JUDGMENT.
A remarkable political development of late is the number of disgruntled politicians and would-be can
didates who have “ratted” on their respective parties and launched forth as “Independents. "
THE HARITUALS : “Traitors! Cowards !” THE DESERTERS : “Bah! Fools!”
Prime Minister Cook is supposed to be a
strenuous Freetrader, yet two of his three
nominees on the inter-State Commission
which is to report on tariff matters among
other things—are strenuous Protectionists.
One of them (Lockyer) is a Collector of
Customs, who knows a great deal about im-
portations; and the other (Swinburne) is
an engineer, business man and company
director, who knows a lot about Australian
industry. Piddington, the Freetrader, is a
lawyer, and is supposed to. be full of bottom-
less research about the intricacies of the
Constitution, but when a question of tariff
reform arises the two Protectionists will
be the Men Who Know. It is inferred, in
consequence, that Prime Minister Cook has
no intention of wrecking his job in the in-
terests of the Freetrade superstition.
THE NIGHTMARE.
Melbourne, July 30.
Merchants and manufacturers are complaining of
the further increase in inter-State freights by the
steamship companies. In the past the practice has
been to make small advances of 5 per cent, or less,
but the latest addition is one of 15 per cent., making
a total of 20 per cent, in the last six months.

May last.”
It is charged against politician Levy of
N.S.W. that he declared the sins of the
Holman Government to be “as thick as the
autumn leaves that strew the brook of
Vallambrosia.” That is the trouble of using
unfamiliar figures of speech. If Levy had
said “as thick as rabbits at Jamberoo” he
might have been on safer ground.
By the Parliamentary reporter:—
The gentle art of stirring up the other man by
unpleasant interjections has resumed its course in the
N.S.W. Assembly, and decent members sigh for
the touch of the vanished boot of Willis, the strong
kicker. Ever since Willis called in the police to
give an ex-Minister of the Crown the frog’s-march
out of the Chamber the Liberals have hated him —just
as any Towzer hates the foot that keeps him out of
the dining-room. The Laborites never loved him,
and loved him less than ever after he had one of
their party emptied out suddenly when he got his
back hoof “over the traces. The serene impartiality
of Willis commanded the respect of everybody outside
the House, and roused the hatred of everybody inside
of it. Somewhere in the small hours of Friday morn-
ing Hollis was addressing the House, and Cohen, of
Petersham, interjected, and finally he rose to a point
of order and inquired if Liberals could not be pro-
tected from blackguards. Later on, in the lobby,
he got a concussion that quite recalled old
times —the old times of Parkes and co., when
hon. members dragged each other all over the pre-
mises, and there was great howling and much bat-
tered belltopper in the land. Still, there is this to
be said for the N.S.W. State Legislature: Never did
it witness the spectacle of a large hon. member try-
ing to seat a small hon. member on the fire, and a
prompt hon. member shooting the aggressor in the
interests of peace. And one of the most aristocratic
Legislatures in Europe once saw three peers mixed
up in a scuffle of that sort.
D. Levy, one of Gregory Wade’s tame
satellites in Ma’s Assembly, was raging at
Holman the other night for not having con-
tradicted a statement which had been attri-
buted to Holman by the newspapers, if, as
Holman alleged, it misrepresented the facts:
Levy: If that statement as it appeared in the
newspapers was not correct, why did the hon. mem-
ber not contradict it ?
Holman: Will the hon. member permit me?”
Levy: No, I will not.
That’s what they call strategy in Ma’s am-
phitheatre.
A new member who will be more uncon-
ventional than Meehan of the Mulga was
elected to the N.S.W. Assembly last week.
At least that is what it amounted to when
Jabez Wright got the Labor nomination for
one of the Barrier pocket-
boroughs, vice Griffith.
Jabez is just about the
prickliest porcupine among
the old Broken Ilill
irrepressibles. Broken Hill
Laborites view employers
with deep suspicion. Jabez,
who for years has had almost
a monopoly of the burying of
Broken Hill, is the excep-
tion. He was the first Labor
mayor in Australia, and if
the Speaker wants him to
wear a coat at the opening
of Parliament he will have
to argue the point first. And
if Jabez gives in on that
(assuming that he ever gives
in on anything) he will cer-
tainly baulk at a collar. But
he will work and he will
talk, and he will make things
a lot more uncomfortable for
the traitors and drifters of his own side
than for even C. G. Wade.
How they hate Socialism, these farmers!
At one day’s sitting of their annual conven-
tion at Maoriland this is all they would
have to do with the wretched thing:—
The conveyance of agricultural lime from the
nearest available source of supply upon the railway,
any distance, free.
Freight on manures to be the actual cost of haul-
age.
Excursion rates on the railways during winter for
the benefit of farmers.
Encouragement by the Government of the impor-
tation of pedigree stock by reductions of freight and
guarantee charges.
Cheap telephone service to settlers outback.
Government imported labor, preferably British
youths, who will thankfuly accept a few shillings
(about 7s. 6d.) a week.
Government veterinary surgeons to be made avail-
able in all country districts at a small fee.
Inland mails to have priority over passenger ser-
vice.
If the honest farmer ever did decide to go
in for Socialism what a whale, and what a
burning elephant, he would make of him-
self !
Tom Slattery has again come into the
public eye in N. S. Wales, this time with a
claim against the Government. In the early
’9o’s Tom was Minister for Mines and Agri-
culture. In Aoril. 1892. his chief, George
Dibbs, went to England on a mission, which
yielded a knighthood, a big cigar and a his-
torical Damn. The expenses of the trip
(£lOOOl were paid from the Treasurer’s ad-
vance account. To reimburse this account,
the sum was placed upon the Supplementary
Estimates, and it was only when the latter
were tabled that the House knew the money
had been advanced. Members took it
badly. Slattery thereupon gave his
cheque for the thousand. Friends of the
party subscribed and handed him about half
the amount; and now Tom, who isn’t the
affluent attorney that he used to be ; wants
a Select Committee to inquire into his claim
for the balance.
A NIGHTMARE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If there be truth in half the tales
Of the man with the holy chiv,
Then good-bye, Sunny New South Wales,
I’ve no desire to live.
He says I’m brother to Ah Foo Sin
And Monkishuma too,
While Tommy Tanna’s my next of kin!
Shoot me, somebody, do !
I dreamt one night I dwelt a space
In a world that is yet to be ;
In a crowded mart I saw a face,
And the creature looked at me.
He was white —in the way the Chinese are,
But his almond eyes were blue!
He told me I was his grandpapa !
Shoot me, somebody, do!
He walked between two baskets slung
On the ends of a long bamboo ;
His father, he said, was Sum Thing Hung,
And he came from Sum Where Foo;
His mother (oh H —1!) was a girl of mine!
(That dream must not come true) ;
Lest I be father to such a line,
Shoot me, somebody, do!
N.S.W.
W. G. D.
