SPORTING
NOTIONS
Bullawarra, by winning the Australian
Steeplechase in fine style, brought off The Bul-
letin’s “double.” Last year this paper, with its
straight-out selections of Guncap at Flemington and
Uxbridge at Caulfield, got one leg in for each of the
big jumping “doubles.” Last month it got half a
leg in only with the tip of United Kingdom and
Leah Kleshna for the G.N. Steeple. But the Caul-
field selections were successful in both cases. Ballista
started at 10 to 1 for the Hurdle, and Bullawarra last
Saturday went out at 7 to 1 for the Steeple, so the
“double” even at s.p. was an 80 to 1 chance. Let
it be counted to The Bulletin for past righteous-
ness when its future occasional tips prove sinfully in-
correct. Such great efforts of prophecy are apt to
leave the local oracle in a state of exhaustion for a
while.
Acceptances for the Australian Steeplechase boiled
down to 13, but they all went to the post. More-
over, eight of them completed the course—a good
average for the 4-miles race, which entails 26 leaps
into the air. Leah Kleshna made the running after
the first mile or so, until the very last obstacle had
been cleared. The mare was not rushed to the
front; she simply jumped her way into the lead,
gaining quite two lengths at each fence by her
lovely spring. At one point she was leading by a
dozen lengths. In the latter half of the long jour-
eny only Vanguard and Bullawarra looked at all
likely to catch her if she didn’t fall. And Leah
Kleshna never falls. The mare was still in front of
Vanguard as they made for the straight after all
the timber was done with, but then Bullawarra came
from third place into the lead, and the race was all
over. The 12st. 11 lb. horse won by four lengths, and
Vanguard (list, lib.) finished nearly half a dozen
lengths in front of Leah (12st. lib.). The time was
under Bmin. It was not a record, but only
three times in 27 years has the time been under
Bmin., and considering the heavy rain over-night and
again on the morning of the race, Bullawarra's per-
formance ranks with the best. Needless to say that
the great horse was well ridden by McCabe, who
steered Obi to victory in the G.N. Hurdle last month.
No animal that has been ridden feebly or inju-
diciously can win a 4-rriiles steeplechase unless the
rest of the field toppled over.
Sea Prince, the alleged dead bird for the Caulfield
Cup, had a go at Caulfield on Saturday, and per-
formed very indifferently. This form would have
gone for nothing as a guide to the English horse’s
Cup chance had the stable not expected him to run
much better. Two or three weeks ago Sea Prince
met with an accident, and got considerably cut about.
This race was accepted as a test of how much the
mishap and subsequent stoppage in his work had
mattered to the chestnut son of Persimmon. Appar-
ently it had mattered a lot, or else the much-glorified
steed is not a handy horse to manage when he gets
badly placed in a field. Sea Prince will have to run
again in public ere his reputation for brilliancy is
restored. Anyway, the popular Victorian double for
the two Cups—Sea Prince and Uncle Sam —looks
very sick. The tales told of giddy Uncle are quite
discouraging.
The other day the record price of
£45,000 was paid by J. B. Joel for the Persimmon
horse Prince Palatine. There were certain condi-
tions with the sale. Pilkington, the previous owner,
says the horse must not be exported from England,
while Joel stipulated that should the Prince suffer
defeat in the Goodwood Cup (which he did a few
days later) the price was to be reduced to £40,000.
Even at £40,000 the price is a record; the highest
hitherto was the £39,375 which the French sport
Blanc paid for Flying Fox. Diamond Jubilee
fetched £31,500, Cyllene £31,500, Ormonde
£31,250, and Jardy and Val d’or (two of Flying
Fox’s get) brought £30,000 and £28,000 respect-
ively. #
Sydney lacrosse came to the end of its
second round on Saturday, and the result of the
various upheavals didn’t alter the position of the
top-dogs in any way. The only riot on which any-
thing depended was that between Mosman and Birch-
grove, and by making a draw of it with a goal each
Bircbgrove lifted itself into the semi-final. Eastern
Suburbs continued its devastating course, and devoured
University by nine goals to two, while Petersham
jumped on Burwood by four to two. An ozone trip
to Manly evidentlv agreed with Sydney, which slew
the Villagers on their own seaweed by 14 goals to two
—the greatest murder of the afternoon. The clubs now
left in the semi-finals are Eastern Suburbs (27
points), Sydney (21), Petersham (17), Birchgrove
(15), and nothing short of a deluge or an earth-
quake will deprive Eastern Suburbs of the premier -
ship. #
Sydney baseball semi-finals on Saturday
were worm and gall wood, so to speak, to the lead-
ing clubs in the premiership scramble. All lanes,
highways, backstreets and bridle-tracks led to Peter-
sham on Saturday, and were flooded with about 3000
barrackers marching to watch the fray. The sur-
prise of the day was the crushing which Petersham
inflicted on the" North Sydnev-Mosman mob. _ At the
end of the seventh innings Mosman led by nine runs
to seven, and then Sheol broke loose in the last two
biffs when Petersham amassed the gigantic score of
13 runs to Mosman’s one, and so came out on the
roof of things by 20 runs to 10. As a curtain raiser
Leichhardt smote Lane Cove to fragments by nine
runs to two —a good, solid drubbing. For the rest,
Waverley slew Burwood by 32 to three —which is aw
unspeakable downfall, and is the record walloping
for the season in first grade. University climbed
laboriously on top of Paddington with 14 to 10. The
semi-final stands thus: Mosman-North Sydney, 11
wins; Lane Cove, 10; Leichhardt, nine and Peter-
sham seven. So the premiership appears more doubt-
ful than ever.
The revived Amateur Athletic Association
in S.A. has started to justify its existence by holding
a cross-country championship. Amateur running is
a sport in which Adelaide has been strangely uninter-
ested in fact, this was the first local event of its
kind. Of six teams three were from the
Y.M.C.A., which has a big membership of new-chum
Britishers brought up on the game. Their first team
ran first, second, third and seventh. University was
a moderate second. Flynn, an Irishman, did the five
miles of racecourse and grass-land, with a lot of
fences, in 28min. 35sec.. and Soutar was only 9sec.
behind him.
Although the French Grand Prix was run
off on July 12, the results have been announced only
last week. Formerly the first past the post was the win-
ning car, but this year it was a reliability contest as
well as a race, and that took some working out. The
result was a victory for Continental tyres, which
have now carried off the honors two years running.
The ability to get rapidly over the face
of the planet Mud per boot is a national asset in
time of war, and Maoriland has some promise and
hope in that direction. It found it out about a
fortnight ago, when Lieutenant Brown, of Nelson,
slithered over 113 miles in 31J hours. What sort
of country he did it over is not mentioned, but
Maoriland roads, excepting in Canterbury, are gener-
ally pretty dreadful. So if the rest of the Maori-
land army is anything like Lieutenant Brown, in-
vaders will have to bring motor-cars to hold their
own with it.
* * * *
The billiard tournament at the Victorian
Club, Melbourne, went to one of the local bookies
this year. W. Gerrard was the annexer of the a 100
prize. He and his opponent in the final heat were
both in the middleweight division —95 and 85 start
in 250 up —but neither of them played much above
ordinary “mug” class. The runner-up, who made a
22 break early in the game, subsequently struck a
bad patch and scored 13 only in 10 successive tries.
Gerrard, who “amassed” a 22 break in the last
stage of the game and thereby paralysed the other
fellow, had previously gone eight times to the table
without scoring at ali. The betting crowd at a
Victorian Club billiard tournament gets a lot of fun
for its money.
By overthrowing South Sydney on Satur-
day at Sydney Cricket Ground in the presence of
over 25,000 people, Eastern Suburbs Rugby League
team put the result of the premiership competition
beyond all doubt for the third successive year. The
Royal Agricultural Society’s Shield is now the perma-
nent property of the club, and so is the President s Cup.
The premiership is its for another year. Of 12
matches played during the season, it has lost only
one, to the boots of Newtown. It has 24 competi-
tion points to the 19 of Newtown, the 18
of South Sydney and the 14 and 10 of
Glebe and North Sydney respectively. Balmain,
Annandale and Western Suburbs are nine, six and four
respectively. What little chance South had of defeat-
ing the premier team was spoilt by over-anxiety,
which caused the players to lose chances when they
happened. East Sydney team, on the other hand,
never played more coolly. In the first half the game
was very even, and East led at half-time with nine
to seven. After that, South’s nerves cracked up,
and it never scored a point, while East scored a try
and added the points, and so won by 14 points to
seven. Annandale, as the prophets said, has struck
form and got the confidence in itself which its plucky
play throughout the season deserved. After the shak-
ing-up it gave North Sydney the previous Saturday,
when it scored 20 to the Hillmen’s 18, everybody ex-
pected it would defeat Western Suburbs, and it did
.—by 18 to nil, chiefly through the brilliant work of
the back machine. Glebe found itself altogether too
slow for Newtown in the second half. At the inter-
val the scores were six all, but the Glebe boiler had
apparently burst, and while it added three points
Newtown piled up 22, and won by 28 to nine. North
Sydney revenged itself for its defeat by Annandale
the previous Saturday when it fell on Balmain and
chawed it up by 19 points to five.
THE “CHOSEN ONE."
Referee Snowy Baker decides Saturday's
argument in favor of Mat Wells (Colienland).
Hughie Mehegan is astounded and dissatisfied.
The Sydney Soccer struggle for the Raw-
son Cup raged more furiously than ever on Satur-
day, four matches being played off to the glorifica-
tion of Balmain and Newtown mostly. Both these
olubs maintained the advantage already gained.
Balmain met Annandale and fell upon it, beating
it by two goals to one. Newtown did the same to
Sydney by the same figures. Northern Suburbs
came down from its hills and smashed up the swamp-
men of Canterbury by a goal to nil. Glebe was
the bright particular star of the day—it fell on the
iron-workers of Granville and chewed them up with
five goals to three.
* * * *
Owing to the fact that Queensland and
the Ma State XV’s were playing an inter-State
match on Saturday the Rugby Union contented itself
with one club match as a preliminary to the star
item. So St. George and North Sydney rolled on
each other cheerfully in the presence of about 1500
people. It was a most surprising sort of game, in
which North galloped over the Illawarra men in the
first half to the tune of 13 to five. St. George seemed
to have literally no hope. Then came the second
half, and North Sydney started with a burst of splen-
did confidence. But some magician had apparently
waved his wand over the dispirited St. George team,
and changed it altogether, or filled it with steam of
something. For all in a flash St. George became ag-
gressive, and continued so. North Sydney did not
seem to wilt, but St. George boomed, and piled up
14 points, while North Sydney failed to scrape any
more together, and so the saint that killed the large,
mythical animal won by 19 points to 13. >
The inter-State struggle was interesting,
the teams being most evenly balanced. The parties
led alternately in the scoring till a few minutes before
the whistle. ” Then Queensland got one point ahead
—and stayed there. Queensland opened the scoring
with three for a try. N.S.W. equalised it. and then
added a full goal, and so got a lead of five points.
Queensland crept up to six to eight, and then, adding
a try, was nine to eight. N.S.W. added an uncon-
verted try, and led with 11 to nine. Just here
ball burst, and with the new sphere N.S.W. bounded
up to 16 to nine. Queensland, with a try, made the
nine into 12, and then added a full goal and led
with 17 to 16. N.S.W. came with a fine charge and
lifted the score to 21 to 17. The shadows were
growing long, and Ma’s chances began to glow, but
there was another sudden onslaught by the Maroons,
and Queensland's score was 22 to 21. The minute
were running out and the Ma State team made tie-
mendous efforts. A series of wild battles took place
on the Queensland line, but the visitors managed to
pitch the Cornstalk crowd back over the whiting
every time, till the whistle went and relieved the
agony. . *
Though two games will complete the Vic.
League first found, it is still impossible to name the
four clubs which will battle out the finals. Roy,
’Wood and South are sure of places, and any one
of the three may grab the double chance that goes t°
the leading team; but either St. Hilda or Carlton
may scramble into fourth place. On Saturday Roy
encountered South in a high wind, and a fast, open
and willing scuffle ensued, with 'Rov always on top
by surer and smarter handling and better method.
For the first three-quarters the Red-and-Whites fum-
bled and blundered and when the last term started
they were 22 points behind. Then their marking
and combination improved, and by hard, steady plug-
ging, they piled on 20 points to ’Roy’s one. Only
time saved the latter from defeat —59 points to
South’s 56. On the day’s play, the Collingwood of
a month ago would have trounced either team ; but
’Wood has struck the internal trouble that spells
disaster. Consequently the Magpies only beat Var-
sity with difficulty the Week before, and on Saturday
the Saints stamped them flat—B2 points to 44.
Unless ’Wood can come to a working agreement of
some sort, it will win no more games this season. It
took Carlton all its time to hang on to its “final
chance, its opponent, Richmond, being still a propo-
sition to be reckoned with ; but it managed to make
63 points to 61. If ’Varsity, which collided with
’Don, had played throughout the season as it is doing
now, it wouldn’t be where it is ; and neither would
’Don. The Students led until the last quarter, but
in that term the ’Dons piled on points at such a rate
that they ultimately won by a pair of them. There-
with vanished ’Varsity’s last chance of winning a
game this season. The Students have still South and
Richmond to meet, and they have Buckley’s chance
of beating either. Geelong was at home to Mel-
bourne, and, perceiving at half-time —one goal eight
behinds to Melbourne’s four goals one behind—that
something was amiss, bogged in with such ill-directed
vigor that it finished up with four goals 23 behinds
to Melb.’s four goals two behinds. #
In the Vic. Association games Brunswick
made its position secure by downing North —74 points
to 52—and thereby raised ’Scray to first place and
itself to second, while deposing North to third.
Next week, in the last game of the round, ’Wick
will doubtless do its utmost to gain first place by
beating ’Don, provided ’Scray draws or is beaten by
P’ran. If it does so, ’Town, which has a soft snap
in Brighton, will step into fourth place, and ’Don
will slide into outer darkness. This will serve Don
right, for on Saturday it concussed with the same
’Town, and retired with a punctured reputation—
’Town, 54 points; ’Don, 45. P’ran, which won its
first six games this season right off the reel, threw
its last chance into the dustbin by playing second
fiddle to the despised Northcote. An untidv and
unmannerly scrimmage ended in ’Cote gaining 49
points to P’ran’s 45. ’Scray stamped on Brighton
with 89 points to 43; and Port put together 16
goals to Melb. City’s three. There were also a num-
ber of behinds, but they don’t matter. Nothing
matters that happens to the Melb. Citizens.
As these later scrimmages grow keener casualties
become more frequent. Still, the barrackers behave
themselves, which is more than can be said of those
at the Scotch v. Wesley College scuffle on Fridav, at
St. Hilda. Here the lighter and smarter Wussleeans
held their own up to lemon-time, after which the
weight and strength of Scotch told and carried it to
victory by 59 points to 34. At the last change of
ends some ill-mannered Scot breathed “Wowser .
into the ear of a Wesley barracker, and a cheerful
little scrap ensued in full view of the respectable
fond mothers and proud fathers. #
The Parramatta-Sydney amateur road
race on Saturday, through clouds of choking dust,
cold winds and vast deserts of blue metal, was a
hideous affair for the riders, and was almost a re-
petition of last year’s event. In the 1912 race M.
E Burke took the first prize, and W. Corben the
prize for fastest time. In this year’s contest the
profits fled into the same pantries. Corben s time
this year, accelerated by the rear-wind, was 1 hr.,
25min., 35sec. —which knocked over two minutes
from the record put up by Andrew Sime.
* * * *
As the result of digging out 60 brethren
with £5 each, N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association
has joined with the Maoriland body in the spec, of
bringing along a team of Yankee athletes. One
half of the guaranteed £3OO will be disbursed in ex-
penses for the coming Yanks, while the other half
will be devoted to financing the N.S.W. Amateur
Championships meeting of 15 events, to be held
while the Yankees are with us. So, provided the
Victorian body will ante up a £l5O guarantee, the
Amurkan tour will take place. If Victoria falls
down dead in the effort, so will the tour.
* * * *
Please charge the glasses and drink the
health of referee Marre, of Brisbane Stadium. Marre
is a reformer, and has started in the good work of
suppressing the palming evil in boxing contests. The
practice of using the heel of the palm for walloping
with has become almost general among boxers
of late, and though referees have issued warnings on
the subject, brother Marre is the first to deal with it
properlv. On Saturday night last he disqualified Pri-
vate Palmer, of Brisbane, for persisting in the evil
course after being warned.
* * * *
The Mehegan-Wells fight filled Sydney
Stadium to overflowing on Saturday night, but did
not supply the enthralling struggle that was ex-
pected. The long-looked-for contest was a dis-
appointment, and distinctly devoid of excitement.
It was really a duel between a Hebrew racehorse and
an Australian land-crab of equal size. If the crab
could only get the horse where he wanted the steed,
the rest would be silence, save for the voice of the
referee counting 10. But the crab was too slow, and
when the horse wasn’t galloping wildly backwards
or sideways for safety, it was hugging the crab with
its forelegs and hanging on like the Devil. From
the very start it was obvious that Mehegan was the
stronger man and the harder hitter, and the ques-
tion was not who could win the fight, but how
Mehegan could catch the other fellow and get a
chance to fight. He was the aggressive party to the
disturbance all the time, and so much the superior
fighting animal (not boxer) that in a bang to a finish
he must surely triumph. No one realised it better
than Wells, and he adapted his tactics accordingly.
The beginning of his confession of faith read after
the first round was: “The chief end of man (that’s
me) is to keep his vulnerable end out of reach of
Mehegan’s salary hook.” Only three times did he
appear as the offensive partner—once when he was
rushing for a clinch and smote Mehegan on the ear
as he came in, crashed into the Australian and bore
him to the floor —but with innate courtesy dragged
him to his feet again immediately ; once, in the 7th
round, when, after landing on Mehegan's jaw with a
most remarkable right overhand wallop, he sought
to follow up the blow ; and finally in the 14th, when
he astonished Mehegan and the house by charging the
Australian into a corner and raining a shower of
lefts and rights with bewildering speed. Most of
the blows landed on Mehegan's forearms and gloves.
Several dropped on his face, but they made no im-
pression and left no mark. At last the slow, deliber-
ate Mehegan put one fist into action and punched
Wells off. But for the rest of the fight Mehegan
wandered round the ring, vainly trying to get within
range, and shooting blows that missed by about a
yard owing to the way the elusive Londoner tip-
toed gracefully away into safety.
When Lord Curzon was telling King Ned
about the tiger hunt, the King remarked: “Cut out
the sunlight glinting through the trees—did you
blow his blessed head off?” “Who won the fight?”
has been the question universally inquired. Honestly,
this writer doesn’t know. His impression is they
both lost it—one because he was too slow and the
other because he was too quick. The speed that
Wells showed in his backward galloping was de-
scribed by Baker as marvellous ring-generalship. It
rnay have been, but it wasn’t fighting; and most
people went there to see a fight and not a race.
Wells’s mad passion for a safe distance reduced the
scrap to a scrambling business, in which the only
hitting took place in and immediately before the
clinches; and that sort of fight is a dreary proposi-
tion. At in-fighting Wells had no show, although he
showed wonderful skill in blocking the vicious upper-
cuts that Mehegan jerked in quickly and ferociously.
But his performance was absolutely defensive; and,
apparently, because Mehegan made all the attack and
failed to make a success of his attack, and because
Wells, by rear-galloping and quick clinching, made
a success of avoiding a knock-out, and therefore
made good his defence, Wells won. Which means
that the only hope Mehegan had of beating Wells
was to knock him out, while Wells wasn't required to
knock Mehegan out. As boxers, there is no com-
parison between them. Wells is the finished article.
Mehegan isn’t a boxer at all —he’s a fighter, and,
apparently, a fighter can only beat a boxer by outing
him. This writer does not dispute Baker’s decision.
He was referee, and the referee must adopt some ideal
of how the fight should be fought before it begins.
And, anyhow, the referee is fate. Had the writer
been referee he would have given Mehegan the
verdict. Mehegan was never in danger, and Wells
was never out of it. Wells received the more pun-
ishment, and simply dared not stand up to his
opponent, who begged of him to come up and have
a real struggle. The most exasperating thing in the
scrimmage was the amazing slowness of Mehegan.
If he could have put on a spurt and kept up with
Wells, history had told another tale.
It was the easiest fight in the world to control.
Both boys were as cool as cucumbers, and after the
referee spoke obeyed at once. There was no sugges-
tion of hitting on either side once the voice of Baker
breathed over the arena. Wells has a mat of shaggy
black hair over his arms, breast, back, shoulders
and legs. He would be a perfect boon to a kilted
regiment. His nose bled throughout the disturbance,
but his face was not the least bit cut about. He
will never be an attractive spectacle fighting with
Mehegan, but with Holberg, the Dane, or our own
Herbert McCoy, he should be exceedingly good to
watch. Never was a man lighter on his feet or
quicker in his movements. Mehegan’s slowness is
a national misfortune; and he should set about
acquiring speed at once. Baker’s decision received
the endorsement of the bulk of the press, but was
hotly disputed by a large number of regular nng-
siders It was one of those affairs in which splendid
argument could be advanced for giving either side
the victory—all a matter of taste, in fact. But one
thing is certain, judging from the remarks heard
afterwards in trams, trains and boats —and that is,
that if Baker is wise he will referee no more fights
in his own Stadium. His popularity will suffer if he
does ; and just now it is a big asset to him. Public
interest is now turning rapidly towards the impend-
ing battle between Wells and Owen Moran, the date
of which, however, has not yet been fixed.
It is said that Wells was so delighted
with his victory that at the first opportunity he threw
his arms around the neck of the unemotional
Mehegan and kissed him. Ye gods! A photo, oi
Hughie’s face immediately after the osculation should
have been worth £lO. Wells is reported to have said
to a newspaper man, “It was my speed that beat
him!” Certainly his speed got him out of an awtui
lot of trouble, but it had as much to do with winning
the battle as the speed of the flying Jewish stock-
brokers of Johannesburg had to do with winning tn
late S’African war.

The Old Soldier: Where do these parsons
get their facts? Here is what Rev. A. W.
Brough, a missionary newly arrived from
India, told a Maitland (N.S.W.) audience
the other night:—
Fifty years ago there were no railways in India...
Fifty years ago there were no macadamised roads... .
Fifty years ago famine swept off the people by thou-
sands, and tens of thousands perished through great
droughts. Britain’s irrigation works had protected
65,000 square miles from drought.
In 1803, which is just 50 years ago,
the C.I.P. Railway was open from
Calcutta to Aligurh, except for the
break at Allahabad, where the great
bridge across the Jumna was un-
finished. That is to say, India had more
than 950 miles of railway in this portion
alone. On the west, Bombay was connected,
over the Bhore Ghaut, with Buna and
Baroda, and the line was being pushed on
into Central India. The Eastern Bengal
Kailway was also open to the Ganges cross-
ing—a matter of 200 miles. As regards
roads, the Grand Trunk Road, metalled,
and with the sides planted with fruit and
shade trees, was finished for more than
1000 miles from Calcutta to Rurki in the
early ’4o’s —say 70 years ago. The
65,000 square miles which are alleged to
be protected from drought are served by
two canals, the Ganges and Eastern Jumna,
which were begun in the ’3o’s of
the last century. In 1861-2 they had been
working for nearly 10 years. And yet there
was famine in the country that they served.

Our Oath.
Instead of a Bill to abo'ish the old ab-
surdity of oath-taking, a measure to sub-
stitute the Scawtish oath for the English
oath has been submitted to the Vic. Assembly
by members Mackey and Baird. They
propose to put the oath into a kilt, as it
were.
The person taking the oath shall hold the New
Testament, or, in the case of a Jew, the Old Testa-
ment, in his uplifted hand, and shall say or repeat
after the officer administering the oath: “I swear by
Almighty God," etc., etc.
If the person be a Mohammedan, Buddhist,
Confucian or any other sort of disbeliever
in both the Testaments aforesaid, he will,
presumably, swear in his own particular
way without regard for the alteration in the
Act. Also he will continue to be a liar or
otherwise, in common with all Gentiles or
Jews, according to how he feels about the
matter in. hand. What is the value of any
sort of oath imposed upon a witness in a
law case: Truth may be obtained from
many people by putting them on their honor,
or on their fear of being prosecuted for per-
jury if the\ tell lies, but the putting of
peonle on their religious belief in the sanc-
tity of an oath is a shocking waste of mum-
mery where the great irreligious majority is
concerned. Moreover, the people who think
they superstitiously attach sanctity to an
oath administered by a policeman or court
official, are mostly thinking a vain thing.
Perjured snufflebusters are at least as com-
mon as untruthful atheists, if not much
more so. What purpose, save the main-
tenance of an ancient fraud, is served by
swearing witnesses? To ask a man whether
lie understands the meaning of an oath is to
insult his intelligence, if he has any. But
to remind him, as he stands in the witness-
box, that he is in a place where he is ex-
pected to speak the truth, and where the
speaking of untruths lays him open to the
risk of being punished for perjury, is to
appeal to his reason. Which is all that the
Law cam forcibly appeal to. Useless to ap-
peal to the sense of honor or the super-
stitious fears of people who are neither hon-
orable nor fearful df hell fire—who tell lies
as readily and unconcernedly as they blas-
pheme—whose word in business dealings is
not worth the proverbial tinker’s curse. Yet
it is for them, and for the women who never
seem to know the difference between truth
and falsehood, that the legal form of oath is
supposed to be efficacious. Good citizens,
who tell the truth as a matter of course
where serious issues are at stake, don’t need
the solemn, sacred, blessed Bible oath. Bad
citizens don’t care a straw about it, whether
it be English, Scottish, Irish, Jewish or
Chowish.
The Hopeless Australian Sailor.
As recently as a couple of years ago,. when
that big section of Liberalism which is
chronically opposed to any new thing, was
fighting the local navy project with both
hands, all its teeth, and any other weapons
it could find, the argument was advanced
that Australians could never be turned into
seamen. We might buy. the shins, according
to these apostles of national self-abasement,
but we would only find the men to man them
efficiently by going to England. Then a
wholly Australian crew, under an Austra-
lian-born commander, gained the gunnery
championship of the fleet in these waters.
Incidentally, the same men won the highest
praise from the British authorities here for
their smartness and good conduct at sea.
The advocates of a hired navy fell back on
the contention that the competent Australian
mariner, or, for that matter, any other sort
of Australian mariner, was such a rarity
as to be hardly worth considering. Followed
the news that A.B.’s, identical in type with
those who had turned out so well previ-
ously, were being recruited in Australia as
fast as the naval people could wish. As a
final howl, our boys of the Pugdog Breed
claimed hopefully that, however good the
ordinary seamen might be at their job, and
however freely they offered, the locally-born
and trained officers would be absolutely
bound to fail. And now an expert, in the
person of Captain Chambers, the head of
the local Naval College, has come out with
the following concerning the lads under his
charge:—
I have no doubt at all about their futures. I do
not desire to train better material. They all
possessed a good education when they came to the
college, and to this has been added mathematics,
engineering, our chief subjects, physics, French, Eng-
lish literature, and seamanship. I have been asked
to compare -these lads with those I have had to deal
with in an English naval college, and on ships, and
am glad to be able to say that they are quite the
equal of British lads, and perhaps a bit superior.
Yet tfie local anti-Australian, whose custom
it is to pose as an Imperialist, will still
cling fondly to his doubts about the futures
of these youths. He will also assure him-
self hopefully that the supply of A.B.’s must
begin to fail as soon as the men discover
what a hopeless and discreditable service
the Australian navy is; and he will cheer
himself with the thought that, in any case,
as time goes on. the inherent lawlessness
and futility of his fellow-countrymen must
appear, with the result that the whole Navy
scheme will peter out in an atmosphere of
shame and repentance. 1 jacking consolatory
reflections of this sort, the existence of the
indigenous anti-Defence patriot would be
unendurable just now.

“A Meek Umbrella-bearer” : The aims and
objects of the Peace Society are absurd and
impossible. I realise that every time<
I gain the confidence of some fellow-sub-
urbanite and he tells me the story of how
he threatened to thrash the newsagent, or
punch the butcher, or throw the postman
over the fence. One mild-mannered man
solemnly told me, the other night, how he
placed the palm of his large hand over the
rage-distorted countenance of our Italian
greengrocer, and, in that manner, calmly
pushed him the length of the shop past nine
varieties of vegetables. When I asked him
why the greengrocer didn’t bite his hand,
he looked at me as if he considered I was
casting doubt on his battle saga. Another
neighbor, who recently had his house burgled
and couldn’t obtain a policeman with suffi-
cient rapidity, tells how he later rang up
the station and asked with biting sarcasm
what was the best place to apply to when
he wanted a constable. When the footsore
Hop at the other end of the wire showed
some annoyance at being asked foolish
questions the bitter householder Sternly
warned him not to be offensive, or he would
have the jacket _ taken off him, his stripes
removed, and his helmet trampled . on by
the official elephant. Only last night, a
man, who, according to his own account,
has threatened to flog hundreds, was sitting

“0.K.” (who is old enough to know
better) writes: — •
Now that the small-pox scare is abating, may I
be permitted to maunder some of my personal re-
miniscences as a “contact” long ago, when the
scourge was more dreaded in England than it is to-
day. My parental home was situated at the foot of
?o g the ate sm”n pox° n ho°spitaT at "Hampstead, 6 which™ is
beyond Highgate. So it quite often happened that
in my youthful walks up the hill the poor people
who were seeking the hospital aforesaid, with a
patient in charge, would hail me from cab windows,
or afoot, and ask me to ponh: out the cut
to the isolated institution where the patient was to
be placed. Thus I became familiar with the spotted
faces of small-poxed people. Once in a way a
woman carrying in her arms a baby wrapped in a
woollen shawl would ask direction to the hospital
and pull back the wrap from the child’s face to
show me how badly the little sufferer had got it.
When I mentioned these incidents to my people at
home they would bid me be wary of contagion, but
they never disinfected me or rushed me into quaran-
tine. In point of fact, there was no quarantining for
small-pox. So much for my experiences out of
doors. Now for a page of domestic history. As a
boy of 10 I slept in the same room as a brother,
seven years older, who had complained of headache
and sick-feeling overnight. In the morning he called
me from my little bed to come and see the funny
spots on his chest, forehead and thereabouts. I pu t

hind the half-opened door Two maid servants and
the terrlble cook remained also in the house, which
(I mugt admit) stood in an acre or two of ‘
an( j no dwelling-house within 100 yards
of it. And the patient’s nurse was the resident
nurse-maid, now relieved of the care of the baby,
She was supposed to be immune from contagion be-
caus(r she had “had it.” My brother’s attack of
small-pox was a severe one, but he recovered all
right, and was sent into the country to convalesce
before the absent members of the family (counting
the baby as one, although it couldn’t talk) returned
to the partially disinfected home. The nurse-maid,
the boss “contact,” went on earing for the infant
as before. Myself returned to the room where 1
had fdt the spots to assure myself of their pimpl
negg Everything went on as usual. Friends called
t 0 congratulate the family on not having caught the
disease, and a month later mv brother returned from
the country with his face showing all the red re-
mains of his departed pustules. These had not
Pitted his complexion because he had carefully
obeyed the instructions not to scratch 0 r rub them
wben they itched. My next experience of small-pox
“contacts” happened when I was a young clerk in
a warehouse in London. A fellow clerk mentioned
casually that a sister of his had worked herself into
a nervous state as the result of having travelled in
an omnibus with a spotted woman. She persisted
. r assuming that the spots were sma ll- po x, and her
re ] a ti o ns could not laugh her out of her fears. About
a fortnight later my fellow-clerk announced that his
sister had got the disease she was looking for, and
soon afterwards he came to the counting-house with
a black band round his hat. His sister had died. Did
we wori V about his contactitude? Did the disease
gQ any further in his home than it had gone in my
home, 10 years earlier? No. Did his sister's case go
to p rove that a duly vaccinated girl can die easily
enough of small-pox funk ? Yes. Needless to say, I
took no part in the Melbourne rush for calf-lvmph.

Sore Throat and Influenza
THE COMMONEST WINTER COMPLAINTS:
THEIR PREVENTION AND CURE.
When the days are cold and wet in winter, disease
germs have a much greater power over man than
they have in summer. The reason is that chill and
damp lower the vitality and render the body less
liable to withstand the attack of the germs and to
destroy them.
Although Nature has made a wonderful provision
for meeting these germ attacks by delegating to
certain particles of the blood the mission to eat up
these intruders, the warmth and moisture in the
back of the mouth and throat enable the germs to
develop with extraordinary rapidity. Each germ
divides into two in twenty minutes, so that, in the
course of a few hours, a single germ will have pro-
duced hundreds of thousands.
If the body is in a good state of health, the germs
are destroyed very soon after being inhaled, so that
they produce no ill-effects.
When, however; the system is below par or
weakened by any cause, the germs easily overcome
the defending forces in the blood, and we get the
particular disease they produce.
ten minutes they were all killed. In spite of
this, Formamint is not offered as a cure for
diphtheria, although it will undoubtedly prevent
that dread complaint and other serious germ-
diseases.
The Prevention of Diphtheria.
This fact is vouched for by many sanitary
inspectors. The sanitary inspector of High
Wycombe, England, writes : “ I know of no other
preparation so effectual in preventing infectious
disease as Wulfing’s Formamint. During an out-
break of diphtheria I have frequently given away
Formamint to those who have been in contact with
the disease, and no other cases have been removed
to hospital from the same household. Whenever
our ambulance is ordered out for the removal of an
infectious case, Formamint always accompanies
us.”
Many articles have appeared in the medical
papers pointing out Formamint’s supreme excel-
lence in the cure of sore throat. In The Prac-
titioner the Chief Medical Officer of one of the
largest Infectious Diseases’ Hospitals in the world
writes: “ I have never had sore throat myself since
I began to use Wulfing’s Formamint, although I
suffered periodically before.”
A writer in the Lancet states : “ I have had the
best results from the use of Wulfing’s Formamint
Tablets.”
It should be noted, however, that only Wulfing’s
Formamint produces such results. None of the
many imitations now on the market will do so.
How valueless they are is shown by a physician,
who writes that he “ has tried six substitutes, and
has not found one of them efficacious.”
Remarkable Concensus of Opinion.
The social testimony to Formamint’s value is as
remarkable as the medical. No preparation, with
the single exception of Sanatogen, which is made
by the same firm, has received such distinguished
testimonials.
The Right Hon. Viscount Massereene and Ferrard
writes: “I .have found Wulfing’s Formamint most
effective for the cure of sore throat, which quickly
disappears after using a few tablets.”
The Right Hon. Lord Glantawe writes : “ I have
been using Wulfing’s Formamint for sore throat
with most satisfactory results.”
The Right Hon. Lord Kingsale writes: “ I and
my family use quite a number of Wulfing’s
Formamint Tablets, and find them all you say they
are. I always carry a goodly number in my pocket
and have recommended them to many friends.”
As to their value in influenza, Sir George Alex-
ander writes: “Formamint Tablets have been my
constant companions during the winter, and I have
found them a dangerous enemy to the influenza
fiend.”
It is in this way that sore throat,
influenza, and bad colds arise.
Obviously the only means of cure
and prevention is to destroy the
germs in the mouth and throat.
Of all the methods hitherto de-
vised by Science for producing this
result, nothing can compare with
Wulfing’s Formamint. This is
unanimously admitted by the medical
profession.
Many thousands of doctors, in-
cluding several to crowned heads of
Europe, have written to the pro-
prietors stating that they invariably
use Wulfing’s Formamint for treat-
ing sore throat. The reason is that
nothing destroys the germs so rapidly
and completely.
This was first proved by a famous
scientist, who removed some virulent
germs of diphtheria from the throat
of a patient seriously ill with that
disease, and mixed them with a
solution of Formamint. Within

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
E.ll. J.: That list of Bulletin con-
tributors who “died young” grows rather wearisome.
Francis Adams wasn’t a scholarly Melbourne writer.
According to his own account he was born in Egypt;
and he was out here a short time seeking health.
Barcroft Boake didn’t die at Mosman; the
geography is all out. Harold Gray wasn’t “the old-
time ‘Giraffe,’ ” and he didn’t die “a sudden and
premature death.” He was ill for a long time be-
fore he passed out. Henry Cargill didn’t “drop
dead from drink in the streets of Brisbane.” He
died indoors, through breakage of a blood-
vessel, while working for a Queensland paper. Any
more questions on hand? Incidentally, what was
the age of the Saviour when He died? It is com-
monly set down at 37 years. Also, what were the
ages, at the time of death, of Josiah, of the infants
who were massacred at Bethlehem, of the child of
the widow of Nain, of Edward V., of England and
of Edward VI. ? Likewise of Little Willie in “East
Lynne” and Eva in “Uncle Tom’s Cabin” ? And
the idea of putting good old Evison among
the alleged early deaths is humorous....
James C.: A great many people can’t bear obscurity,
and if they can’t stand out of the ruck by reason of
special ability they resort to special eccentricity.
This accounts for many prominent atheists, vege-
tarians, nut-eaters, wearers of “rational dress,” Ul-
sterites, Turcophils, advocates of the Beast of Reve-
lation, anti-vaccinationists, Chidleys, and people who
carry a banner inscribed, “Excelsior.’ That is to
say, some people are by nature “rebels in the hills,”
and if there is nothing large to rebel about and no
convenient hills, they have to strike out for them-
selves. We notice, with all due respect, that anti-
vaccination is the hillock or ant-heap you are rebel-
ling on....Junis: You don’t even know enough of
the inkster’s art to use inverted commas where they
are wanted. And the whole thing is the dullest
possible catalogue of how one article went for so
much at the auction, and another for so little, and
one more for next to nothing. If a wild-looking
man suddenly brained the auctioneer with a smite
of furniture because he had stolen the wild-looking
man’s wife 25 years ago, and then shoved his re-
mains inside an eight-day clock, and went off with
the goods under his arm, waving a cruet round his
head to scare off possible pursuers, there might be
some trace of a story in it... . Gal: There is a hor-
rible sameness about the bush priest of the “charac-
ter sketch.” He is always Irish, always generous,
autocratic, short of temper, and a fast driver of
good horses. In short, he is a sort of holy roarer.. .
El Tigre: A rather confused anecdote and wants a
lot of sorting out The Curate: A somewhat
skimpy episode. Of course, we don’t expect every
tale to contain three railway accidents, a murder, a
person unjustly sentenced to penal servitude for life,
a heroine, an adventuress, a forgery, a battle, a
horse-race, a revolution and a whaling expedition
all in one column, but there ,are limitations... .F.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)

Jonathan B.: Declined. It is a poor and struggling
joke Smith: The pictures and the jokes were
equally a disgrace to the great Smith family. Do
you know that Benhadad, King of Syria, bore a
name that was the local equivalent of Smith? There
was, in Norse mythologv, the Smith of the Gods.
Furthermore, the smith’s apron was for many years
the national standard of Persia. And there were
many great and orominent Smiths, including one
Smittowski, of Poland, and Bobby Smith, the Lon-
don banker, whose descendant became Governor of
N.S.W. You are a discredit to a noble race.

TOBACCO
IT is an admitted fact that present tobacco
crops are eminently unsatisfactory, and
manufacturers who are compelled to
purchase at the present time cannot obtain
so good a tobacco as hitherto. It is owing
to this deterioration that many cigarettes
are degenerating in quality.
THE manufacturers of “Winfred”
Ci garettes are in a unique position.
They hold an enormous stock of the
finest selected tobacco purchased during the
past three or four years, so that they are able
to maintain, for some years to come, the
present high-class quality of their cigarettes
without increasing the price.
10 for 6d.
CIGARETTES
To be obtained throughout Australasia.

An Important National Question.
Settlement of
Northern Queensland.
Treasurer of Queensland has recently called tenders for the
1 erectlon of a ] arge, modern, and well-equipped Central Sugar Mill
at Babmda Creek, near Cairns, North Queensland. This will
now give wide opportunities for energetic young cane farmers, and
reliable field workmen, and (later) mill hands in the Sugar Industry.
The Mill is to be ready for crushing in JULY, 1915.
W ith adequate protection (and no industries in Australia are more
entitled to good protection than those natural to the Tropics, and carried
on by European labor) there are many other agricultural opportunities
for farmers and settlers, i.e in Coffee, Tea, Spices, Tropical Fruits,
Arrowroot, etc., etc.

Sydney, 11th July, 1913.
GEORGE J. COHEN, Chairman.
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
W. R. SAYERS, Accountant.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances, and counted the coin
In the Head Office of THE COMMERCIAL BANKING COMPANY OF SYDNEY, LIMITED, and have
compared the returns of the Branches, and have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance-
W. U DOCKER, , Auditor ,
The following resolutions were carried unanimously: , , a a ,
“That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated
amongthe Thompson Esq be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“That E S Caoe Esa and F W. Hixson, Esq., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing year.”
“That the thinks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Officers
of the Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of T A oiBBS. Genera! Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chair-
man, Mr. George J. Cohen, said: —
“I am pleased to say that the Directors are again
enabled to prelent to you a satisfactory Balance-
sheet, and though the stringency in the money market
that I referred to at our last meeting has been some-
what intensified, with a consequent restriction in
business, yet I tliink you will agree with me that the
result of our operations for the past half-year is
gratifying. Naturally there has been contraction in
our figures, but without decrease in strength.
“In comparing the figures with those of June last
year I would first mention that by payment of final
instalments our capital paid tip now stands at
£1,750,000, the limit authorised by general meeting.
During the same period the-Reserve Fund has been
increased by £BO,OOO, and if we take into considera-
tion the appropriation recommended to-day it will
stand at £1,630,000.
“Notes in circulation show a reduction of £*B,ooo,
and you will observe that only about £30,000
remain unrepresented.
“During the period under review our deposits have
decreased by £BlO,OOO, which is more than
accounted for by reduced Government balances, and
the withdrawal of Federal Government moneys at toe
beginning of the present year, to the extent together
of over £1,100,000. In connection with this item
I may mention that our Fixed Deposits have in-
creased during the twelve months, while our Current
Accounts, though less in amount for the reasons
•tated, are much larger in number.
“Profit and Loss shows a satisfactory increase of
£24,000, and our total reserved profits will now
stand at £1,687,000.
“These variations cause a net contraction in our
total figures of £657,000.
“Our total Liquid Assets show a reduction of
£275,000, yet notwithstanding this the ratio of
these assets to our total liabilities to the public is
over 47 per cent., practically the same as it was a
year ago.
“Our lendings have been reduced ,by £384,000.
“The last item, Bank Premises Account, shows an
increase of only a little over £ 2000, although we
have expended a considerable sum in land and new
, buildings.
“Realising how greatly the success of the Bank is
dependent on the efficiency of the staff, and desiring
that the officers should feel that their efforts are ap-
preciated, the Board have again voted them a bonus.
In addition, special consideration has been given to
the pay of the staff, and as a result the Board have
fixed upon a tariff of salaries and increases on a
scale more liberal than lias hitherto existed. The
Board feel sure that they have the fullest concurrence
of the shareholders in this. . .
“It is intended to call the proprietors together at
an Extraordinary General Meeting before the end of
the vear to consider the advisableness of increasing
the paid up capital of the bank from £1,760,000 to
£2,000,000, the new shares to be issued at par.
“I have much pleasure in moving that the Report
and Balance-sheet be adopted, and that they be
printed and circulated among the proprietors.

“Booshee”: Mention of Docy Stewart and the
death of her third husband (B. 31/7/’l3), brings to
my mind her tirst, Jim Collins. When Docy Stewart
married him she retired from the stage, but soon
returned and made a very welcome reappearance at
the opening of the new lioyal in Melbourne on Cup
night, 1872. An opening address was written for
the occasion by Dr. Neild, dramatic critic for The
Australasian, and Docy Stewart read it. She re-
mained at the Royal for some years, doing excellent
work as actress and vocalist, but left in 1877 to
start on a tour of the world in companw with
Maggie and Nellie and dad in “Rainbow Revels,”
a drawing-room entertainment written for the family
by Garnet Walsh. In the ’Bo’s Jim Collins died,
and a year or so afterwards his widow married actor-
manager H. R. Harwood. Harwood was then very
well-to-do; but the bank smashes of ’93 blew him
out, and within a year or two he died —threw up
the ghost. Then Docy Stewart married Chamboisier,
later on of the Savoy. But that end of her eventful
life has been written *t>f by men whose thatch is
thicker than mine.
A glimpse into the profits of the leasing side of
the moving-picture business was lately given by
John Payne, in the M.L. House of Reps. There had
been a howl in favor of the Anti-Socialistic farmers
being provided .with Socialistic manures. Payne
perpendicularised himself, with a suggestion that
the Government should take over the control of film
importation and distribution in M.L. This, he
said, would ensure some educational films, while
supplying from £70,000 to £BO,OOO a year to buy
fertiliser with. As Payne is in the picture business
himself, his estimate as to a local picture-combine’s
profits may be taken as fairly reliable or else
wildly sarcastic.
“Lud”: What a gasp of astonishment among the
actresses in court when it was announced that the
Marquis of Northampton had agreed to give
£50,000 damages to Daisy Markham! She’s a
little widdy of a sort, having divorced Harold Moss,
a stockbroker’s clerk, in 1906. She is 27, and has
been nine years on the stage. In court she was pale
and agitated (of .course). Under her black satin
coat was a white silk blouse, open at the
neck. A pink rosebud blushed on the lapel of the
coat. Her big black hat was trimmed with chiffon,
fastened in front with a large jet brooch.
These things I mention as being possible aids to
other damsels in distress. A-t. any rate, no other
actress has been able to score so well. Miss For-
tescue got £lO,OOO from Lord Garmoyle. Phyllis
Broughton’s little lot from Dangan/ now Cowley,
was £sooo—the same as Birdie Sutherland got
from Marjoribanks, afterwards Tweedmouth. True,
Northampton’s income is £150,000, and a gentleman
with £150,000 a year ought to be specially careful
how he plucks his Daisies.
“Quo”: Something went hideously wrong with
the works in the S. M. Herald office the other day,
with the result that the notice of “The Dancing
Mistress” contained some cold truths. The piece
was “machine-made,” it had “not a gleam of orig-
inality,” the humor was “pumped,” “the tedious
rubbish” of the comedian “would make a panto-
mimist blush,” the co. nothing with the
“barren nonsense,” and, generally, everybody con-
cerned was “ill-used by composer and librettist.”
How rare is frank comment of that sort in the
Australian daily press, and how much better for
the public if there were more of it! The tragedy is
that we get it but once in five years or so.
“0.K.”: Nordica, in “the sere, the yellow leaf,” re-
minds one somewhat of the Albani who made her first
appearance in Australia many years ago, under Wil-
liamson’s management—not the Albani of the second
visit, whose singing and platform frivols had de-
generated into a joke. Nordica is still capable of
suggesting her former greatness. Her high notes
have a smoothness and a quality of tone which dis-
tinguish the top-notch voices, artistically produced,
from the best of the second-class voices. Her trill
is still reminiscent of its prime. And in all her
work there is the air of the prima donna who has
done famous things, which air, indeed, was more
pronounced in the more florid warbling of Albani
many years ago. Nordica's surviving notes are very
faded (the others are practically gone), but the
perfume of their past clings to them. Her person-
ality is winsome. It has very little of the magnetism
of a OalvS, but in conjunction with her own indi-
vidual style of dramatic expression in singing, the
charming personality counts for a good deal. Her
name having been associated with grand opera only,
and particularly with Wagnerian opera, is no attrac-
tion to the multitude that remains ignorant of the
affairs of the operatic world, but to those who have
long known her by reputation, the appearance of Nor-
dica is interesting and satisfactory. The famous
artist is a has-been. But then, she makes it clear
that she is a has-been, and not a never-was, whereas
Albani, on her second visit, offered no vestige of evi-
dence that she had once commanded a fee of, say,
£2OO or £3OO for starring at a concert.
Hard times for the drama,” says a heading in
London Daily Mail. “Seven plays fail in eight
black weeks.” The seven were “Strife,” “The Seven
Sisters,” “Yours,” “The Duchess’s Necklace,” “Pan-
thea,” “The Cardinal’s Romance” and “The Perfect
Cure.” Various causes are assigned. Some critics
even go to the length of saying that the plays are
no good.
From London: Harcourt Beatty is in the
cast of “The Barrier,” to be produced at
the Strand, where “The Glad Eye” recently
ceased from protracted winking. Matthew
Lang also has a part in this piece, which tells the
story of an American officer who falls in love with a
beauteous half-breed in Alaska, and strikes almost
as many complications as the case demands.
When “The Marriage Market,” George Edwardes’s
latest musical comedy success, comes to Sydney, Arch-
bishop Kelly will have to make another public pro-
test. The scene is in Western America, and in a
place called Medicino Bluff, in Southern California,
an annual marriage market is held among the cow-
boys. Thither resort all the pretty calf girls from
far and wide, and they are playfully knocked down
to the highest bidders, and afterwards spliced in
mock matrimony by a surpliced cowboy. But this
time the surpliced cowboy happens to be a real
R.C. priest, and the supposed mock marriages are
the real goods. Hence arise all sorts of complica-
tions, dances and ditties.
“Vic.”: Berry and Anderson, the Ballarat printers,
have just discovered a playbill for the opening of
the, local Theatre, RoyaLin 1858, under the manage-
ment of W. Hoskins, who had been Irving’s tutor.
The programme includes Jerrold’s comedy of “Time
Works Wonders” and the farce “Deeds of Dreadful
Note.” Hoskins had a Ballarat brother, H. H. Hos-
kins, a solicitor; owner of Nimblefoot, the Melbourne
Cup winner. The Theatre Royal was afterwards in-
corporated in Snow’s drapery emporium. Former
theatres at Ballarat were the Montezuma and Charlie
Napier. Favorites at these were Kate O’Reilly, Kate
Warde, Greville, Con Warde, Bob Dale and Stuart
O’Brien. The dramas which most obtained favor
were such as “The Green Bushes,” “Flowers of the
Forest,” and “Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” with an infusion
of old-fashioned tragedies, such as “Jane Shore,” in
which Con Warde used to rage. Little -Toe Dias,
Tong afterwards known in Melbourne as Josh Hea-
wood’s crony, was a favorite low comedian. Hoskins
raised the Ballarat standard, with productions like
Shakspeare’s “Tempest.”
“Circle”: There is one thoroughly unreal touch in
“The Butterfly on the Wheel.” When foolish little
Peggy realises how siispicious her shallow friends
are after the unfortunate evening with the co-re.,
she delivers a carefullv-written, involved and wholly
“literarv” speech to the effect that she is a little
fool; but, unlike the hired fool, does not possess a
cap and bells costume—and so’ on for several beauti-
ful, well-marshalled lines. I can’t remember how it
goes exactly, but it must look very nice printed.
Yet it is utterly useless. No butterfly Peggy could
talk such stuff, and as for getting impatient” Smart-
Setters to listen to it !
Ethel Dane, Australian actress, and wife* of Aus-
tralian actor Cyril Keightlev, has discovered a new
form of the gentle art of advt. She has made a pet
of a two-year-old bear, and London papers have
been snapshotting her as she goes for a morning stroll
with her little bruin,

IN BANANA LAND
As a result of a commotion made by Kir-
svan and Adamson, M’s.L.A., the Denham
Government has decided to delete No. 18
from the Queensland Traffic Regulations.
This obnoxious rule of the railway service
deprived employees of the right to wag their
longues as citizens. Under it all they might
do politically was to record a silent vote on
polling day. Twice previously the Assembly
had carried resolutions for guillotining Reg.
18, but the Government took no notice. Con-
stant dripping has worn away the Tory
stone, and it will stand no longer in the
way of Mr. Shunter saying what he thinks
about Denham and Paget—or Denham and
Paget’s opponents, who may be equally in
need of some plain English some day.
"Rangan”: Something has gone wrong,
and the Mac Keen petrol cars, which the
Queensland Railway Department imported
from America for suburban traffic have not
been put on the lines yet. What’s wrong
with the steam Purrey cars now running in
Rockhampton? They have been working
perfectly for two years now, and can be
made locally (Rockhampton makes its own).
With a trailer they cost £l5OO. and carry 80,
while a Mac Keen car costs £4500 and carries
70. With the facilities at the Ipswich rail-
way works, something like four Purrey cars
could be turned out for the cost of one Mac-
Keen ; and the Purrey cars have now been
proved under local conditions, while the
others haven’t been.
“Rangan” : If you have a “double” in this
world, pray that he may break his neck.
One Alexander Butcher, of Rockhampton,
had a “double” in Alexander Anderson. They
were so much alike that you could not tell
the other from the first one. Anderson was
human and frail, and Butcher, being a casual
Australian, did not pray that Providence
would break his wicked neck. So it hap-
pened that when Anderson had loved a lass, ,
not wisely but too well, and shortly found it
convenient to drop south, leaving tangible
evidence of his affections, it was Butcher who
one day received notice from the lass to pay
up. Of course he refused. Later, before
a bewildered P.M., four people swore that
Butcher was Anderson, and four more swore
that Butcher was not Anderson, nor Ander-
son Butcher. After 16 gasps the P.M. man-
aged to say that he was quite convinced that
Butcher was Butcher, and Anderson was
Anderson, and that it was Anderson whom
the infant should make goo-goo noises to.
Now the enlightened Butcher prays that the
unrighteous Anderson may break "that which
keeps his head joined to his body, but he sees
that he ought to have sent up his supplica-
tions long ago.
“Phil F.”: If the teetotal principles of
Young Bros., of Fairymead (B. 31/7/T3),
impelled them to run their molasses into the
Burnett instead of selling them for the manu-
facture of rum, the sacrifice did not prevent
the spread of drunkenness. I remember see-
ing fish which had been “nipping” at the
molasses pipe floating about on the surface
of the Burnett hopelessly inebriated, to the
joy of small boys who used to row out and
pick up the goods before they had time to
sober again. After a few weeks, however,
the boozers grew canny, avoided the molasses
overflow, and took to strenuous teetotalism.
“Caber” : Ken Grant, one of the few men
who have left the Labor party and still re-
main in the Bananaland Assembly, finds
himself in a tight corner just now. In the
days when Grant was a “red-ragger” he
yelled lustily for separation, and on this
ticket Rockhampton stood him up and
blessed him. His old colleague, ex-parson
Adamson, tabled a motion in the Assembly
the other night for the subdivision of
Queensland info three separate States, and
Grant’s hour of tribulation arrived. To
support the resolution would be to go back
on the Denham crowd; to vote against it
would mean that his name would be spelled
M-u-d in Rockhampton. The only excuse he
could fashion was that this was merely
another trick of the Labor party’s, and so
he would vote against it. Which settles
Kenneth McDonald Grant.
THE RADIANCE OF
A NOSE.
JUDGE (to cotinsel, -who has some difficulty in reading a document): “Do yon want more
light , Mr. Hair-Splitter ?”
OPPOSING COUNSEL (with very red nose): “Yes, your Honor; he does, very badly.”
COUNSEL FOR DEFENCE (to Judge): “Thank you, your Honor, I do want more light;
but I wish you to understand that I object to any reflection from my friend on
the right being cast upon me."
Again the Northern degenerate! Martin
Gorman, an ancient Hughenden identity of
70 years, got lost in the Flinders country
the other day and wandered around exposed
to the dreadful climate of those parts for
seven days. When picked up by a search
party the old fellow was little the worse, and
made no particular pother about his suffer-
ings. Trifles of that kind didn’t worry him.
Toowoomba is asking itself if it had not
better shift camp. It had been so busy
over the small-pox scare that it quite over-
looked a case of leprosy in the heart of the
city. In spite of occasional official secrecy,
the number of cases of the foul disease that
come to light in Queensland is depressing.
The “cheap” colored labor with which the
North still foolishly flirts carries a terrible
sting in its tail.
Townsville, the uninteresting, has dis-
covered a much-needed sanatorium, Mount
Spec, five or six miles from the railway to
Ingham, and about 40 miles from Bobby
Towns’s town. A party of citizens has
just returned from a week-end trip with
glowing accounts. The place is 3000 ft.
up, there is “scenery to vie with the Blue
Mountains,” no mosquitoes, 20ft. of alluvial
soil, settlers’ cottages with Deaches,
lemons, oranges, grapes and all cold-country
fruits, necessity for blankets winter and
summer, and occasional frosts—a regular
luxury for coastal natives. Townsville is
just about to celebrate its jubilee, and feels
entitled to the Mount Spec sanatorium as a
birthday present. But it will cost £6OOO to
turn the place into a get-at-able resort;
and alas ! “Bob” Philp is no longer Premier.
The Kicking of the ’Roo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cook Government has ukased that the kan-
garoo must depart from the postage stamp.
The people clamored for reform
In fashion clear and strong,
And language rose a trifle warm
When Joseph dallied long;
A score of things cried out, “We need
A man who’d dare and do!”
Said Joseph in decided tones —-
“Kick out the Kangaroo!”
The tariff wants up-building high,
The imports keeping down ;
The Asian eats Exclusion Fie
With dark and angry frown ;
And so our Navy must expand.
Our new-born Army too.
“I know,” said Joe, “the way to go —
Blot off the Kangaroo!”
At Darwun there’s an open gate
That bids the foe come in ;
To leave it so is tempting Fate
And gambling with our skin ;
If naught is done to link us up
Some day the land will rue.
Said Joe, “Decamp from off that stamp.
You wretched Kangaroo!”
The tread of moneyed Ring and Trust
Comes nearer day by day;
And dear will be the worker’s crust
If they should have their way.
How shall we block their little game
And spoil the broth they brew?
Said Joe, “Ho-ho! The trick I know—
Just shift that Kangaroo!”
And when he leaves his little stage
The books will tell with pride:
“He rushed around in noble rage
And Heav’n and earth defied.
He tinkered not with tariff rates;
No alien Trust he slew ;
With warlike boot this valiant coot
He biffed the Kangaroo!”
QUEENSLAND.
PHILO ROO.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 9, 1913.
My Dear Thabgomindah,—
The Chiel and Lady Macgregor, Captain
Silver and his aristocratic-looking grey-
haired wife, an assortment of officers in
colored duds, and a lot of mere clay watched
some 4000 cadets march past the square
of garden between the Lands Office and the
Police Commissioner’s Office this afternoon.
The lads swung along splendidly, with rifle
on shoulder, and, though some had been en-
rolled only a month, they looked quite
smart. Amongst the civilian onlookers were
Arthur Morgan and Pinlayson and Huxham,
M’s.L.A. P remier Denham was engaged
elsewhere, opening a f@te or something else
with a vote oF’two tied to its tail.
On Friday afternoon the Macgregor pre-
sided at the annual meeting of the Society
for the Hindrance of Brutality.
The Stricklands are expected here on
Monday. Lady Edeline and the Governor
will camp at Government House, and Miss
Strickland will shelter under J. H. McCon-
nel’s roof at East Brisbane.
The event of the week was the Hilma
Ohman-Harry Mills wedding. The little
bride looked smaller than ever by the side
of her 6ft. 2in. Englishman. Her dress was
of white satin covered with wonderful old
lacfe, which had belonged to the bridegroom’s
mother. There were six bridesmaids, and
the female part of the audience gave a gasp
of envy when it saw their frocks. Mollie
Brenan, Marjorie Green (the handsome
Maorilander who has been staying with
Mrs. Arthur Feez for so long), and Leila
and Myee Feez were all tricked out in white
net over satin, with sashes of floral ninon.
Each dress was made differently, and the
hats, of ninon to match their sashes, were
likewise dissimilar and heterogeneous. The
maids carried great armfuls of pink roses.
Mrs. Ned Tully’s little daughter and the
Haymen child brought the number up to
six. Their frocks were white muslin over
pink silk, and they, too, were laden with
roses. Archbishop Donaldson read the ser-
vice in a properly-dejected voice, and
George Sampson Jack-Johnsoned the organ.
The groom gave his bride a pair of pearl
and diamond ear-rings, and a team of
ponies. He has been driving the ponies—
hve of them —up and down Queen-street
for the last week, rather to the dislocation
of the traffic.
The wedding was one of the best-dressed
items of the winter. Mrs. Blundell (who is
the brides mother—P. A. Blundell, of the
Queensland Trustees, is her step-father)
looked as pretty as ever. She is a daughter
of Tom King, once Commissioner of Rail-
ways, and comes of a good-looking family.
The air is laden with piebald mutterings
anent the Y.W.C.A. It is alleged that three
young women who came up from the South
had the door of that eminently respectable
and charitable establishment shut in Hi •
faces, and that the carter who was in
of their luggage (a mere Philistine who dirt
not belong to any religious society) w,
them not to faint by the wayside, but
them to some decent place where theyobta S
tood and safe shelter. A Brisbane womaS
who was in Sydney last year also alle,*.
that the Y.W.C.A. there refused to open fh
door to some country girls whose train wa«
late, and who therefore did not arrive
at the holy hostelry until after the uneortiv
hour of 11 p.m. The girls went back J
Redfern station and spent the night there
in the waiting-room. Incidentally I SPP
Thargomindah, that the Holy Children in
Sydney are howling at the Government to
do something to checkmate the White
Slaver.
The captain and officers of the Australian
war-boat Melbourne brushed their hair
shone up the brass wdrk, and gave an At
Home yesterday on board. The Melbourne’s
decks are to be cleared for action again
next Thursday, when a chosen few will
bunny hug and grizzly bear among the guns.
Cassidy, Rhodes scholar for 1913, ■•was
farewelled at a river trip on the old bob-
tailed tub Lucinda on Monday afternoon
Most of the University staff arid all of the
students turned up to say good-bye to him
for he is a very popular young hopeful. On
Tuesday evening a farewell dinner was
eaten in his honor, likewise his presence.
G. S. Hutton, a well-known business man
and a leading light among Masons (the
ones that wear aprons so that the cham-
pagne won’t stain their dress pants), died
early on Monday morning. He had been ill
for some time, suffering from a complication
of liver and kidney disorders. For some
years he was a member of the committee for
the General Hospital, and that institution
profitably borrowed largely of his business
experience.
The Amateur Dramatic Co. is to-night
wrestling with German’s comic-opera,
“Merrie England,” with George Webster!
emerged from seclusion, once more playing
the comedian’s part. Lottie Richter, who
is a better singer than she is an actress,
and Mrs. E. R. B. Jordan, who is surpris-
ingly good at stage work, are also included
in the cast. Over 70 people are helping the
show to take the money at the door without
feeling too bad about it.
Brisbane will be stone broke when show
week is over. King’s dramatic co. is putting
on three popular bellowdramas during the
week. “The Count of Luxembourg” will
get out on Tuesday in favor of “Autumn
Manoeuvres,” Branscombe has two shows of
Dandies going for the next fortnight, and
the pictures we have always with us.
Wirths’ circus, also, is here. This hamlet
is certainly going the pace.
Another feed was given to the social
unemployed by Mrs. Billy Ahles, for Amy
Papi, who is going to marry one Minnett at
some future date (exact time unknown).
The tea was a linen one, and when the 65th
guest had deposited a bundle on the intend-
ing matrimonial victiih’s lap it looked as
though a laundry had broken its stay-laces.
Catherine Laing, who is going to convert
—or as near to it as she can get—-yellow
brothers from their heathenism, was enthu-
siastically farewelled at St. Andrew’s
Church on Wednesday night. The Governor
presided, and various gentlemen with their
clothes buttoned up behind mentioned what
a glorious thing it was for Catherine. I’d
sooner get Catherine’s unbiased opinion in
a year or' two.
The annual ball of the Yacht Club hap-
pened on Tuesday. Captain alad Mrs. Silver
came along, and brought al collection of
officers- with them. It was a .cheerful heel-
shuffle, and if anybody asked for the com-
plaint book it wmsn’t done in my hearing.
Other leaps were managed iby the Old
Girls of the 8.G.5., by the Liberal Young
Women (Digby Denham supplied the chin
music), by the Golliwogs (who were enter-
taining the Blue Birds) and by the Oxley
Lawn Tennis people. Pretty Mrs. Espie
Dods gave a dance, too, early in the week.
Yours at the terminus,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Digby Denham’s Electoral Reform Bill is
the proposal of a Government utterly panic-
stricken by the Federal elections in Queens-
land. As things simply must be evened up
somehow, the idea is to give Propputy a
shove-along by restoring the oostal vote, and
Labor a set-back by abolishing the so-called
“absentee” vote. Under the latter provision
a man must be two months in an electorate
before he can get a transfer —unless he is a
property owner, in which event he can man-
age it in 24 hours. With almost brutal
frankness the Bill aims at disfranchising
every nomad, and that means wiping out
the whole wool- and wheat-harvesting popu-
lation as voters. Even Government sup-
porters are amazed at the chilled-steel impu-
dence of the proposals : and their one excuse
is that a man may fly to anv port in a
storm.

On CONTINENTALS.
The French Grand Prix is an annual event, and was this year held over a wedge-shaped course of 19.6 miles
near Amiens Formerly it was a straight-out speed contest; this year speed was combined with reliability,
each car being allowed forty gallons of petrol for the contest, or an average of fourteen miles to the gallon. It
will be easily understood how important a part the tyres would play in a matter of reliability. Last year’s
event was won by Boillot, also on Continental Tyres. -

The Best Saddle in Australia
for the Stockman
£6llO
w*
m
We have been making saddles for close on half a century—-and
all we know about the game has gone into the making of this
famous Stock Saddle of ours. It is hand-made throughout—no
cheap and nasty machine work anywhere. It is built on the
staunchest tree of specially tempered steel, fitted with an
unbreakable gulletplate, to which are riveted (not screwed, mind
you) solid steel hind crupper plates. This makes the tree
absolutely unbreakable, yet gives just the elasticity and “give”
needed. The seat is made of specially tanned bag leather, with
very long, thick harness leather flaps, that do away with the
need of wearing leggings. There are leather points to the panel, that will
not wear out for the whole life of the saddle. The knee pads are 51 inches
high, and made of solid leather, double sewn inside so that they won’t sag
or give over. The thigh pads are made in the same way and If inches
high. We have the seats with as high a dish as 6 inches, but any lower
dish you want we can give you. The mountings are brass and nickel, and
the Dees are fastened so securely that no tug will strain them. The panel
is serge lined, and with extended facings so as not to fray or wear out by
the rubbing of the swag or tucker gear. The saddle is sold fitted with
fold or split leather girths, i bar irons, stirrup leathers, ring or ordinary
crupper and surcingle.
OUR GUARANTEE. —Any number of these saddles of ours sold ten years
ago are in use to-day and practically as good as new. No stauncher saddle
could be made. The one you buy from us we will guarantee for two years.
If within that time the saddle proves faulty in material or workmanship, if
you return it to us, we will give you a new saddle in place of it free of
charge. If a saddle lastt two years it will last as long as it is cared for
—though of course no saddle will stand being rolled on or being bumped
against a tree.
L. UHL & SONS LTD.,
Harness Builders, Petrie’s Bight, Brisbane, Q.

THE
WORKS
OF
De MAUPASSANT.
ELBERT HUBBARD writes:—
“Men of Genius, who have left
the beaten track to discover a
new country, have ever called
down carping criticism. A prac-
tice centuries old, this, when one
cannot understand nor appreciate ;
’tis so satisfying and virtuous to
stand aside and sneer. Or bet-
ter, take the Presumptuous One
and nail him to a gibbet. For
aeons men have fouled their own
tongues by slander; they have
dipped their pwn hands in the
heart’s blood of martyrs; they
have besmirched their own mem-
ory and polluted their times; yet,
all their destructive' madness has
plucked not a single leaf from
the laurel crown of Genius. You
may kill a great and good man.
Oh, yes ! —you may reproach and
revile him; you may call down
imprecations, but when you are
dead and forgotten, his soul, his
memory will go marching on and
on. De Maupassant, of ail
writers, has suffered most from
misunderstanding. He has been
crucified on the brazen cross of
ignorance. This man never wrote
to please the Rabble. He wrote
because he had a message for the
world, and because Divine Energy
forced it through his pen. De
Maupassant wrote with the con-
viction that in life there could be
no phase so noble or so mean, so
honorable or so contemptible, so
lofty or so low as to be unworthy
of chronicling—no groove of
human virtue or fault, success or
failure, wisdom or folly that did
not possess its own peculiar
psychological aspect and there-
fore demanded analysis. If oc-
casionally some intellectual pig-
my, after reading, screams in fal-
setto, please remember spon-
taneity and mediocrity are never
kin.
“A WARNING.—Those of you
who find enjoyment in blue-milk
brain-wanderings should never
seek De Maupassant. You will
be disappointed. You will not be
able to digest nor assimilate the
strong elixir of his mind, so it
will curdle in your gullet—then
it’s you for mental constipation.”
THE
“STANDARD”
EDITION.
The Standard Publishing Co. are able to an-
nounce the issue to subscribers of a complete
and admirable translation of the entire works
of Guy de Maupassant.
The Standard Edition of Guy de Maupas-
sant is complete in Seventeen Volumes, Crown
Bvo, and consists of nearly 6000 pages,
printed from a new type, elegant and clear,
on pure white antique paper, specially made
for this edition. The books are bound in blue
cloth, with distinctive brown and gold title
labels, silk head bands, and gold top.
We have prepared an illustrated prospectus
of our Standard Edition of De Maupassant.

inquirers. The booklet describes the edition
in full detail, and gives particulars of our
special plan, by which the whole 17 volumes
are sent, carriage paid, for a first payment of
TEN SHILLINGS, the balance being payable
by easy instalments. This booklet will be
sent to all book-lovers and literary students
