ARE YOU
DEAF?
if you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just hew it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. t .No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —-the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion cf the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
0
cured, snd you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are onlv
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send' them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the

at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you

Business, Robbery, etc
Herbert Rowe, one of the heads of the firm of
Blythe and Platt, who manufacture the Cobra boot
polish in Australasia, was entertained at a farewell
banquet by his friends the other evening. The func-
tion took place at Paris House, Sydney, and the
excuse was Mr. Rowe’s impending departure for what
Clara Butt describes in song as “The Land of Hope
and Glory.’’ He left by the Otranto. “Blythe and
Platt” is purely a trade name. The owners of the
huge Cobra industry are the Rowe family—the whole
Rowe family ; for whenever one of the sisters marries,
Cobra promptly swallows him; he becomes part of
the firm. It’s a delightful way to be polished off.
A large mass of N.S.W. loan money invested in “repro-
ductive works” will cease to produce anything but a
crop of interest after this month. The Glenbrook
deviation, on the Mountain line, has been opened for
some weeks, and the trains to the West have been
dodging the ruinous grades ; but the trains to Sydney
have continued to slither down the old track. The
duplication of the line on the deviation is now com-
pleted, however, and the “reproductive” work in
which much money was sunk will consist mainly of
a long, unused hole. The work of tearing up the
mortgage will, as usual, be left to a future genera-
tion.
» * » •
“Fred Ellen”: If you ever put Milk-o’s money in
the billy or the jug, don’t. Not because of masked
burglars who might got there before Milk-o, but
because of the casual habits of Milk-o himself. I
happened to be awake when he called a morning or
so ago. Stumbling out of his chariot de lait, he
grabbed the can and splashed the fluid in without,
as I thought, seeing the cash. He did see it. though,
only the milk got there first. Unhesitatingly he
plunged his hand into the milk and rescued the
cash. Then he wiped his hand on his trouser-leg,
said “Gid-dap,” and went on cheerily with his load
of microbes.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: When Henare sells his land, has a cheque
deposited to his account in the bank, a cheque-book
in his coat pocket and a fountain pen to figure with,
he becomes “te pi-i-i-g rangatira.” Sometimes,
though, his ideas of finance are odd. Old Ropohama
had just started on his second cheque-book, and, in
a week’s time, he received an urgent call from
McPants, the clothier, to “come into the shop—want
to see you.” Ropohama followed. “Look here,”
said McPants exhibiting a blue pencilled “Refer to
Drawer” cheque of the old cannibal’s. “You shouldn’t
do this, you know. You’ve got no money.” Then
Ropohama’s wrath rose like an angry ocean. “Who
te herra te ferra he say I kot no money? I s’ow
you. I kot te prenty! Rook here!” And he
dived into his motor-coat and hauled out about
300 hundred pounds’ worth of “tenners.” “T’ere,
now. You say I kot te no money. Might pe I
kot more money ’an you. Py tchingo, I te good
mind to sue you for te libel.”
* * * #
Anonymous donors of the rare kind who really
don’t want their identity to become a public secret,
might take example from the “shabbily dressed”
citizen who lately handed a small brown paper parcel
to the hall-porter at the London Hospital, stating
that it was “for the hospital.” The parcel—-it had
been carefully sealed in sundry places—was taken to
the secretary’s office and put aside on the presump-
tion that the shabbily-dressed person had left some
light reading for the patients. Its contents proved
to be securities worth £lO,OOO and a half-sheet of
note paper inscribed, “For the good of the hospital.”
The secretary found the bonds and th ngs as good
as they looked, and turned them into cash, and the
identity of the benefactor remains wrapped in mys-
tery. It seems quite easy to do good by stealth
without having to blush to find it fame. Even a
newspaper par is not called for in all cases—any
parcels of securities left at this office will be treated
as strictly confidential.
“Ricardo”: The annual renort of the London
County Council, to hand by last mail, confirms the
dismal statements about the finances of the Big Fog’s
trams. For the first time since the electrification of
the main routes there is decreased revenue ; receipts
for the year ending March were £2,143,559, or
£88,343 less than in the previous 12 months. After
paying £730,687 interest on capital, the net profit
was only £497. The motor-’buses have driven off
with the rest.
“Me”: Some unpatriotic friend presented my kid
with a new toy. It was an alleged replica of the
Australian fleet, tiny warships made of plaster, with
Australian flags on every mast, each a quarter the
size of the ship. None of the toys resembled in the
remotest degree any vessels of the Australian fleet,
and every one, when launched, hurriedly capsized. I
found that the things were “made in Japan.” Per-
haps this is an example of Asiatic humor.
“Vagabond”: “Evrander’s” friend in the Clyde-
bank yard, where the Aquitania is building, is mis-
taken in reckoning that the new Cunarder will grab
the “record size” ribbon from the German Imnerator.
He says the Aquitania skiff is to be just the level
300 yards in length ; but the Deutscher’s measurement
goes 20ft. 6in. more than that if you count in the
figurehead, or lift, more without that trifle. The
Cunard’s only hope will be to call the ship the Uni-
cornia, and stick on a figurehead of a unicorn with
a spike projecting the 201 ft. “Eyrander’s” friend
says he expects the exact gross will figure out 57,000
tons. But even with the extra yard or two of length
the Imperator scales onlv 52,117, and I hardly think
the shorter Cunard is likely to be 5000 more gross.
Imperator’s displacement is 57,000 tons. The Clyde-
banker speaks, too, of the new cockleshell’s 20 knots ;
but the Hamburg-American is designed for 224, and
has already gone over the 234 without breaking a
staylace. One of the most interesting features of
the Imperator is the lifeboat arrangement. There
are 81 ordinary and two motor-boats. About a dozen
of them ate on the lower promenade deck, right in
under cover; which means that in spite of all the
“either-side launching” so strongly recommended
after the Titanic inquiry, the boats of this new
Titanic are more unlaunchable than ever, except
from one side.
* * * *
London has resurrected an ancient industry, and
England is saved again. It is the burning of corks
—the outcome of the phenomenal success of the Pal-
ladium Minstrels, followed by a flood of imitators.
It is only by the burning of corks that the white
man can convert himself into an unimpeachable
Ethiopian entertainer. The corks are burnt to
ashes on iron shovels over a fire. A fine black-
powder is produced, which is sifted and mixed into
a paste with beer. Nothing but plain, ordinary beer
will impart the right hue to the 'complexion A
dozen discarded gates to liquid Hell (or Paradise-
according to the point of view) will produce a four-
penny tin, and if you want less than fourpenn’ortli
you’ve got to either cadge it or make it yourself.
LUCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne papers have been making a disturbance
over dirty pound notes, pointing out the extreme
danger the people run of contracting disease from
them.
My luck, I think, has been the worst
That ever hapless mortal had.
For months with empty pockets curst
I saw friends on the joyous burst;
I saw the vulgar people glad ;
And I was thirsty, I was sad.
I could not find fit work to do ;
At begging I was diffident;
To steal I lacked the courage, too.
I dragged my battered bluchers through
A callous town. Where’er I went
Sour looks upon my rags were bent.
And now I’m down in quarantine.
The small-pox robs my soul of ease.
When 1 had not a blessed bean —
When starving, ragged and unclean —-
I found a quid beneath the trees,
And from it caught this fell disease!
VICTORIA.

Witchcraft.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rl'IIlE winds uf Spring are blowing.
' I 1 And it is good to be
_§_ Where some kind sun is sowing
Gold sparkles on the sea ;
While laughter wings the going
Of m y white witches three.
One witch that wears a dimple
And clouds of fragrant hair,
Hair that the salt airs crimple
While day-beams wanton there;
V witch demure and simple
And delicately fair;
\.nd one whose cheek can ferry
Clear blushes all the day
From shore to shore of merry
Blue, rippling Laughter Bay ;
With red lips like a cherry.
And black hair blown astray;
And one whose silken tresses
Are soft and mousey-brown,
With tenderest caresses
Her white brow smoothing down—
A witch who wears for dresses
Pearl lustres called a gown.
Amid the happy beating
Of my divided heart,
I know that I am cheating
A goddess who is smart
By metaphoric eating
Of my tripartite tart.
But, such an incantation
My merry witches sing,
My young imagination
'On broomsticks or the wing
Soars through the whole creation
Drinking a three-fold Spring.
Macbeth was well provided
With beldames in his day,
Strange bearded dames who sided
With Banquo in the play,
And fears and hopes divided
In an unpleasant way.
But my three fill the measure
Of gay and careless hours;
With all the silver treasure
That laughter round me showers,
They pluck the sweets of pleasure
And fill my world with flowers.
Yet wickedly they hold me
In bonds I may not break ;
While dreams of Spring enfold me,
I loaf for their sweet sake.
Whose shining eyes have sold me
To ways of tea and cake.
To ways of cake and chatter,
To paths of frivol young.
In sunlit glades to scatter
Sweet things I should have sung
But why should singing matter
Where wattle dust is flung?
Ambitious hopes are sleeping,
My greatness needs must wait
While joy her tryst is keeping
With Nancy, Jane and Kate;
I reck not of the reaping
Of harvests ripening late.
If life’s large debts are growing
I’ll pay them with a smile;
When clear, young winds are blowing
It is not worth my while
To mark the debit owing
Or bills of gladness file.
And so my witches hold me
Until the sun goes down.
Till big, warm nights enfold me
And all the world grows brown.
And various wheels have rolled me
Right back to beer and town.
Till o’er some midnight potion
I droop a weary eye,
While drear with late commotion
Wild hooting cars go by,
And I’ve a sleepy notion
That witchcraft’s wells are dry.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
The Black Doll.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HAROLD URWIN prided himself on
being of the Norseman type, He
tried to heighten his resemblance
to Haarfager by encouraging a natural
exuberance in the matter of golden ia -ii,
and incorporating into his abun an .
straw-colored moustache strips of wns er
right down to the ,iaw line, whence ie
yellow fronds fell with piratical luxunanco
almost to his breast. He was nearly ■
tall, and had shoulders like Bjorn H'onsi t
or Rolf the Ganger. This imposing viking
was a reputable salesman in a city so
goods warehouse. He had a subtle ant
silky persuasiveness, invaluable for 11 ,al
purposes, and his successes in lace ant 1,1
gorie were the talk of the trade.
Annie Urwin, his wife, had Jj’lte
and was so poor in pigment that, 1
her good churcliwoman’s detestat o
artifice, she was compelled to f *
brows and eyelashes to save her lace
utter extinction. . . f .
Baby Urwin was the daintiest 11 ,
imaginable, with a wonderful o
those affectionate little artifices, eac
tition of which throws a mother into
paroxysms of happy adoration, f j
was the palest of yellows, and suri
the flower-like grace of her aQ £ a
like a faint holiness. Her mother kep
Irene spotless; a smudge on baby me
have been the blackest of sins, so k -
was it repented, so speedily eradicat •
father lost his wits in babv s presene •
marvelled at her with an infatuation iuue
Pathetic. The three together unfailingly
suggested pale primroses.
Baby Irene was aged three f n
half when her father brought borne
black doll. It was a Christmas gift-
was just a nude piccaninny r ?f i ‘ s V < 1
moulded in plaster, and painted P|tcli b «
with very red lips and staring white ey
Ai first sight of this toy Irene screamed
with terror, and toddled away to hide her-
self. W hen they showed it to her again,
she took refuge in her mother's arms, and
lor some time after she buried her face in
the protecting maternal breast whenever
the black doll was brought out of its box.
Gradually, however, they wore down her
reluctance. Harold was most insistent.
“It’s such a contrast to her ethereal
beauty,” he said. "She will look so quaint
nursing the little nigger. We’ll have her
photograph taken for one of the papers, and
call it Darkness and Light.”
Presently Baby Urwin was induced to
look wonderingly at the black doll from a
safe distance. One day she grew bold, and
touched it. That evening she had it in her
arms, hugged to her breast, when her
father came home. She was seated on the
carpet under the breakfast-room table, rock-
ing her little body, and crooning softly.
Harold punched the atmosphere, and
roared his delight.
“Just come and have a look, mother!”
he called. “Did you ever see anything to
beat that? What’d I tell you about her
gettin’ mashed on that pick?”
But mother had been peeping in at the
family pride at short intervals throughout
the afternoon.
“She’s been nursing it for hours—the
darling,” she whispered.
“Come to daddy, little sweetheart,” said
Urwin.
But for the first time Irene declined.
She bent brows of stern reproof on her
father. “Hush!” she answered, “you will
waken baby.”
That was the beginning of Irene’s in-
fatuation. Afterwards she was rarely seen
without the black doll. Even when the
family went visiting, Pick must be one of
the party. It was a standing joke with
the Urwins’ friends to invite “father,
mother, Irene, and the black.” Many people
criticised Annie’s dubious taste in seeking
to advertise her little daughter’s exquisite
charm by continual repetition of this
odious comparison; but by this time poor
Mrs. Urwin was jealous of the black doll.
It had robbed her of Irene’s usual regard
and of much of her love. The tendernesses
that had been lavished on “pretty mamma’’
were now bestowed on “pretty Pick.”
None of the funny infantile demonstrations
went to mother. The black doll was little
Irene’s only confidante, her companion, her
fetich, and her baby. She would talk to it
for hours; she would sit perfectly still for
long intervals when Pick was supposed to
be sick or asleep, or she would croon over
it through a whole evening, down on the
floor, rocking her body, and chanting: —
✓ Croon, croon, croon
To de lil’ brack coon.
Yo’ mus’ sing ’im, yo’ mus’ swing ’im troo de long
afternoon,
Till de ole man be hangin’ out de new-washed moon,
’N’ de hoodoo_ loons
Come a-seekin’ baby coons,
Croon, croon,
Croo-00-oon.
In after life it distressed Mrs. Urwin
that her mind could call up no other pic-
ture of Irene as a child, but that of her
sitting on the breakfast-room carpet, rock-
ing the black doll, and singing:—
Croon, croon, croon
To de lil’ brack coon.
When the girl was _ seven her father
bought her a beautiful pink and white doll,
with flaxen curls and large, blue eyes that
opened and closed, but Irene turned her
back upon it. When they endeavored to
force her to acknowledge and accept the
white doll, she wept bitterly, with her face
in a corner, hugging Pick tightly in her two
ai *Next evening the two legs of the new
white doll were found sticking out of the
bars of the kitchen stove. No more ot it
“Pick told me to do it. lliat was all
the explanation Irene could give.
When Irene was 14 she had a black doll
named Pick. This was not the same doll,
for the child had always welconied a new
black doll if it were like the original lick.
In fact, she had insisted on having a new
one when the old one wore suspiciously
white in places. It was thought better to
buy her a new one than to have her refresh
the complexion of the old one with boot
blacking or stove polish. n ,
At 14 Irene no longer nursed her black
doll in public or before company. Her love
of Pick had now the zest of a secret wicked-
ness. She kept her black doll hidden in a
bottom drawer in her bedroom, and at laie
intervals her mother found her. sitting on
the rug by her white bed, rocking her trea
sure and crooning the negro lullaby. .
At 17 Irene was a deeply religious girl.
Her parents had deliberately designed it. It
seemed to Harold that her spintuelle beauty
demanded a sacred setting. T «n e had not
served to coarsen any feature of the girl.
daintiness. Her rose whiteness was tinned
with the tender pink that steals through the
shell of the nautilus, and her hair, soft am
•ihnndant, had the pale gold of raw silk.
When Urwin saw her singing in the choir,
or white-robed, taking part in some church
festival, he was suffused with a kind of holi-
ness that thrilled along his nerves, and liber
a ted the spring of tears. It seemed that he
ha Th b e e *Sfri “great pride in her church
wor k There was a nun-like fervor in her
devotion to the Sisters’ Mission, a bodi <>
sr aS,ief jaw strarsi

had become a greater decalogue—an estab-
lished standard of moral conduct. Edgar’s
wooing was tinged with the fine traditions
of “the Firm.” lie had attended Irene Er-
win’s church for two years. For a year lie
Had loved her. The time was ripe for fur-
ther developments. Within six months from
the Sunday when lie first appeared on Har-
old Erwin’s mat, the engagement was an-
nounced. Jamieson, senior, had taken st< ck
<ff Irene over a space of 11 months, and at.
length had announced his, entire agreement.
She was, he said, “just the wife for the
Firm.”
. The engagement lasted a year The young
people were to be married in March, in the
meantime Edgar had not made much pro-
gress as a lover. He kissed her right cheek
when they met; he kissed her left cheek
When they parted. It was a formal cere-
mony, without emotion. But there was a
man stowed away somewhere in Jamieson’s
excellent black suit, under his spotless bell L
topper and the courtly suavity of the perfect
shopwalker, and that man was crying out
With increasing fierceness against the bar-
riers Edgar dared not break. He loved
Irene with passion; he was forbidden to
offer anything but cold service. He longed
t» take her fiercely in his arms, to hold her
close to him, and kiss her to life and warmth,
and always tile rigid saint seemed to rebuke
him.
, Once when Edgar’s kiss was impetuous
Irene cried out. and ran from him. As his
love grew she built up against him higher
and higher her ramparts of ice.
Irene had manifested little feeling when
young Jamieson began his courtship. She
accepted the engagement as a natural se-
quence, and was not opposed to it; but now,
as the wedding day drew nigh, unrest came
upon her like a fever. She was troubled,
tormented, never still, and her face that
knew little of smiling even in her childhood
became infected with a haunting fear. Her
parents noticed it.
t “Won’t you tell your mother, dear?” Mrs.
Erwin pleaded. “Here in my arms upon my
heart, whisper it.”
; Irene pushed herself free. “I have nothing
to tell you, mamma,” she said.
„ Edgar, too, had watched the development,
dfid was troubled by it. He even protested.
I “You are not fair to me, Irene. I feel
Sometimes that you shrink. Do you mis-
trust me? Do you think I may not make
you happy ? I love you. I will do anything
—anything you ask of me. Your work may
go on. You shall be near your people always.
For God’s sake, speak your heart to me.”
She offered her cold cheek to' be kissed,
Snd sent him away grieving and perplexed.
Irene’s parents told him that all would be
well, that this attitude was only natural in
qi gentle and timid nature confronted with a
great life change. He tried to be content.
On the morning of what was to have been
her wedding day. Irene was missing. There
Was a letter for her parents, merciless in its
brevity:—
•From to-day I know I can no longer be a
daughter to you. I see clearly that I am cutting
myself off from all, whom I have known. Good-bye.
IrenE.
The blow stunned Urwin and his wife. It
left in their faces the pathetic look one sees
on the face of a suffering animal. They
were affected alike, and would sit for hours
without exchanging a word, but the least
sound, a footstep on the path, the closing of
the gate, a knock, a cry, caused them to look
at each other with mute questioning, - and
eyes in which hope faded to pitiful vacuity.
Their sun had gone out, the stars had fled
from their heaven. Old age came on them
almost in a day.
Harold still worked for the Firm. Habit
is almost omnipotent. There was scarcely a
discoverable difference in the unctuous and
persuasive salesman. It was only in his
home that he lay prone under the burden.
They were? spending their savings in the
search for Irene. That search was begun
hopefully. When a year passed and no news
came of her, it was continued as a holy mis-
sion. If there were whisperings outside they
never reached those tw r o forlorn people in the
lone home. Harold and Annie visited no-
where now. saw.no one. The utter abandon-
ment of their misery had closed out even the
kindest hearts.
One evening when Erwin returned from
business, he. found his wife sitting on the
floor of their daughter’s bedroom, a black
doll in her lap, weeping desolately. She had
found the Pick secreted in a wardrobe
drawer.
Irene had been lost to them for over two
years, when a telegram came from Tread-
well, the private detective in whose hands
they had placed the case.
“Something for you. Come to Sydney at
once,” so the wire read.
They caught the express that evening.
Treadwell met them on the platform at the
Sydney station. He had a taxi-cab waiting.
They were driven away in haste. The agent
knew the hunger of their hearts. Up two
flights of stairs in a dingy slum building.
Treadwell opened a door, and stepped aside
for Urwin.
“Go in.” he said.
. The room was squalid. The furniture con-
sisted of little more than two broken chairs
and an old packing case. On a mattress
against the opposite wall a tall negro lav
asleep. Seated on the floor in the centre of
the apartment was a slim, fair woman who
bent over a sturdy black baby that fought in
her arms.
Annie stole to her husband’s side, clinging
to him, for the woman on the floor was
chanting a quaint lullaby :■ —
Croon, croon, croon
t To de lit’ brack coon,
Yo’ mus’ sing him, vo’ mus’ swing him troo de long
afternoon.
EDWARD DYSON.
Jimjams.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
present article pretends to do no
| more -than touch the fringe of this
fascinating subject. There are hooks
enough dealing with hallucinations of the
ghost-seeing kind, but the pathology of Jim-
'jams has never received the attention it de-
serves. It is, of course, dealt with every
,day by doctors and druggists, but in an
empirical fashion only. I believe it to he
of sufficient importance to he specially
studied with a view to discovering its laws
and placing it upon a scientific basis. Jim-
jams are, by most people, looked upon as a
mental malady half-horrible_, half-grotesque,
instead of being regarded as aji invaluable
key to the obscurer workings of the brain.
* * * *
This is the age of Specialism, and here is
a fine opportunity for a specialist. He should
begin by carefully noting the particular
variety of: jimjams produced in the mind of
some selected subject by the exhibition of
different stimulants. There should be no
difficulty in obtaining a subject; the billet
would be rushed. The cost of the liquor used
in obtaining the desired results would be all
the expenditure necessary. There'might be
an inquest or two in the course of the ex-
periments. hut any intelligent coroner’s
'jury would unhesitatingly pronounce a ver-
dict to the effect that deceased perished
accidentally in the interests of science.
I used to know —lie is dead now —one of
the best authorities on .Timjams in the whole
of Australia. He informed me that a burst
on rum made him see rats, while at the
wrong end of a whisky-burst he invariably
saw snakes. When he had mixed his liquors
to an heroic extent he saw neither rats nor
snakes, but, instead of them, an old woman
with a black shawl around her head, and a
carving-knife in her hand, who wanted his
blood, and chased him around the room to
get it. He could give no reason why the
visions varied with the liquor. Other ex-
perts have told me that their experiences
have been similar. Here is a point that
should be elucidated. The questions to be
answered are: Hoes a certain kind of liquor
when taken to excess produce a certain kind
of jimjams regularly, or does it evolve, say,
rats at one time, and snakes or beetles at
another? Does it produce a different kind
of phantasmata with different persons? Is
it possible to raise new strains of jimjams
by cross-breeding, i.e., by mixing liquors
methodically, and in accordance with a defi-
nite plan? A satisfactory solution of these
problems would be a valuable contribution
to psychology.
* * * *
I have not known many persons afflicted
with jimjams, but I have known a few. To
begin with myself. I was riding on the
train from Melbourne to Brunswick one
dark winter evening when, to ray astonish-
ment, I saw a long line of black rats as big
as terriers, and with fiery eyes', running
parallel with the train. They were moving
in Indian file, and turned neither to the
right nor left, but kept straight on, as if
they ' had important business in front of
them. I shut my eyes and rubbed them—
but the rats were still there. I was highly
amused, and not in the least scared. I knew
that they were not real rats, and I was not
at all surprised when the entire procession
vanished suddenly. I never saw anything
of the kind before, and have never seen any-
thing of the kind since, nor can I account
for having seen it then.. Jim McNab (that
was not his name, but he was a Scotchman,
and it will do) told me that his jimjams
took the shape of a black parrot that
perched,on the head-rail of his bed. and kept
repeating all through the night: “Cut your
throat. .Tim McNab! Cut your throat. .Tim
McNab !” In his case the hallucination Was
so vivid that he used to curse the parrot, and
try to catch it and wring its neck. Another
man I knew used to see beetles by millions
crawling over the floor of his bedroom. Tliev
were very real beetles to him, and he laid
baits of poisoned paste for them.
An interesting, if somewhat cruel, experi-
ment was tried on a young man in Mgl
bourne during the Boom time. He was
drinking heavily, and his friends resolved to
make an attempt to scare him into sobriety.
One of them, a Cbll ins-street sharebroker,
had had a young alligator sent him in a
case from Queensland. The case, with the
lid off, was placed on the office table, and
several persons in the secret were present
at the hour when the young man usually
called. He came in, and. noticing the case,
observed jauntily. “What have you got
here?” lie looked into it with a bloodshot
eye, and started and turned pale. Then
he laughed nervously and exclaimed, “By
Jove, it did give me a scare!”
“What gave you a scare?” said the share-
broker solemnly.
“The dashed little alligator.” replied the
young man.
The sharebroker looked at him sorrow-
fully. and the other persons in the room
went one by one to the table, gazed into
the case, and shook their heads sadly,
“There’s nothing in the case,” they 1 said.
“O Lord,” cried the young man ; “I’ve
got ’em again.”
He was taken out for a pick-me-un and,
in the meantime, the alligator was lifted out
of the case, and placed in an adjoining
room. When he came back he looked into
the case once more.
“It’s no use boys,” he said, “I’ve got ’em
badly. It’s there still.”
The foregoing is an instance of jimjams
produced by suggestion.
* * * *
The jimjams of the Cultured Person are
much more terrible than those which afflict
the man of small education. The latter
sees rats, or snakes, or beetles of different
colors, _ but it takes more than a vulgar
visitation of that sort to shake the nerves
of the Cultured Person. The Dweller on the
Threshold, as described in Bulwer’s “Zan-
oni,” is about the size he takes in jimjams.
Dr. Samuel Warren, author of that onee-
famous book, “Ten Thousand a Year,” had
a patient who used to see a Thing in human
shape sitting on a chair by his bedside,
after he had been drinking heavily. The
Thing had no face, and no eyes to see with,
yet, in some horrible wav. looked at him
with an expression of unutterable malignity.
But jimjams of that kind are fortunately
rare.
* * * *
The Specialist in Jimjamology should
lay himself out to discover why the rats
and snakes, and other zoological spectres
seen by hard drinkers,are so often gaudily
colored. Possibly the congested blood in
the veins of the eye has something to do
with _ it. And here is another interesting
question: Is the nervous, imaginative Celt
more subject to jimjams than the stolid
phlegmatic Teuton? When that is an-
swered the specialist can proceed to investi-
gate what difference, if any, there is between
the jimjams of the two races. This opens
a wide field of scientific research. I leave
it to him. (

Adze slipped and caught his shin—Gaping wound i
which refused to heal Another Zam-Buk triumph, I
Also Wife’s five-year-old Ulcer Completely Healed.
MR. W. J. Ward, of 244 Perth Street, Subiaco, Perth,
W.A., who has been engaged in mining pursuits
all his life, and taken part in the principal “rushes”
on most of the fields on the Murchison, Kimberley, and
the latest at Bullfinch, says:—“While timbering in a
mine at Mount Magnet, W.A., through insufficient light,
I made a false stroke with an adze. The edge slipped
and caught me on the shin, inflicting a terrible wound.
The pain was dreadful, my leg swelled up to the size of
two, and 1 was completely disabled. My leg soon got
into a very bad state, and, although I tried many reme-
dies and had medical attention, nothing eased the in-
flamed condition. The wound was gaping open,
presenting a repulsive and angry appearance.
“Zam-Buk was recommended to me, and I commenced
treating myself with this splendid balm. I dressed
the injury night and morning, and in a few days the
aching pain was relieved, and the swelling and inflam-
mation resumed a more healthy appearance. T per-
sisted with Zam-Buk until the wound was completely
healed, and new, healthy skin grown over.”
“My wife, going into the kitchen one dark night,
struck her leg against the front of the grate. The
abrasion soon developed into an ulcer, around which
the surface was black and blue from congealed blood.
The wound refused to heal, and several times she went into the hospital,
and for over twelve months was an outdoor patient, but it did not get any
better. After suffering for five years, she commenced using Zam-Buk.
This splendid balm quickly drew out all poisonous matter, and cleansed
the wound, giving it a chance to heal, which it did rapidly. My wife'
continued applying Zam-Buk until her leg was completely better. Now
we are never without a supply of Zam-Buk handy.”
